O UXISSIjVEIS
We wish It to be understood that the character of tin .
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of tin
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings-
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every comriiunity, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
froth the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of tin
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stand-
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN'S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
neveroease from their toil ; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it. has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
'The Bulletin’s ’ Scrap-Book .
LEONAINIE.
Leonainie —Angels named her;
And they took the light
Of the laughing stars and framed her
In a smile of white;
And they made her hair of gloomy
Midnight, and her eyes of bloomy
Moonshine, and they brought her to me
In the solemn night—
In the solemn night of summer,
When my heart of gloom
Blossomed up to greet the comer
Like a rose in bloom;
All forebodings that distressed me
I forgot as Joy caressed me —
(Lying Joy ! that caught and pressed me
In the arms of doom !).
Only spake the little lisper
In the angel-tongue;
Yet 1, listening, heard her whisper—
“ Songs are only sung
Here below that they may grieve you—
Tales but told you to deceive you—
So must Leonainie leave you
While her love is young.”
Then God smiled and it was morning,
Matchless and supreme;
Heaven’s glory seemed adorning
Earth with its esteem.
Every voice but mine seemed gifted
With the voice of prayer, and lifted
Where my Leonainie drifted —
From me like a dream.
1853.
JAMES WHITCOME RILEY.
*[The foregoing verses, in imitation of Edgar Allan Poe,
were very incorrectly printed in a recent Bulletin —this
paper (which did not know who wrote che lines) having
republished them just as they appeared in the journal
from which they were clipped years ago. The author,
Tub Bulletin now finds, is James Whitcomb Riley, wide y
known as a clever American writer of verse—chiefly in Ihe
Hoosier or Indiana dialect. “When the Frost is on the
Pumpkin” is in his more characteristic vein.]
WHEN THE FROST IS ON THE PUMPKIN.
When the frost is on the pumpkin and the fodder’s
in the shock,
And you hear the kyouck and gobble of the strut-
tin’ turkey-cock,
And the clackin’ of the guineys, and the cluckin’
of the hens,
And the rooster’s hallylooyer as he tiptoes on the
fence;
O, it’s then’s the times a feller is a-feeling at his
best,
With the risin’ sun to greet him from a night of
peaceful rest,
As he leaves the house, bare-headed, and goes o .t
to feed the stock,
When the frost is on the pumpkin and the fodder’s
in the shock.
They’s something kind o’ harty-like about the
atmosphere
When the heat of summer’s over and the coolin’
fall is here.
Of course we miss the flowers, and the blossoms
on the trees,
And the mumble of the huramin’-birds and buzzin’
of the bees;
But the air’s so appetizin’; and the landscape
through the haze
Of a crisp and sunny morning of the airly autumn
days
Is a picture that no painter has the colorin’ to
mock —
When the frost is on the pumpkin and the fodder’s
in the shock.
The husky, rusty rustle of the tossels of the corn,
And the raspin’ of the tangled leaves, as golden as
the morn;
The stubble in the furries—kind o’ lonesome-like,
but still
A-preachin’ sermons to us of the barns they growed
to fill;
The strawstack in the medder, and the reaper in
the shed;
The bosses in theyr stalls below—the clover over-
head ! . ....
O, it sets my heart a-olickin’ like the tiokm of a
clock,
When the frost is on the pumpkin and the fodder s
in the shock !
JAMES WHITCOME RILEY.
John Hausmann, the Melbourne and Syd-
ney bookmaker, has made Sydney his head-quar-
ters for the past two years. He has been a
bookmaker for a quarter of a century, has been a
member of Tattersall’s over twenty years, and has
probably not made an enemy the whole time. He
is a safe man, who attends strictly to business.

have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.

city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Archibald.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, JUNE 8, 1895.
A Buried Infamy.
J ust now, while the eyes of Australasia are
on the Dean alleged-poisoning case, is so
obviously the inopportune time to dig up the
old but enduringly-smellful infamy of the
Mount Rennie trial that no apology is neces-
sary for recurring to that half-buried story.
The right time, according to all orthodox
ideas, to refer to it would be when the pre-
sent agitation was over, and the community,
temporarily tired of such subjects, had
settled down to that state of stolid indiffer-
ence which commonly passes for a judicial
frame of mind. The wrong time, as already
mentioned, is just now, and The Bulletin
deliberately selects the wrong time on the
ground that it is the only right time in such
cases. Nearly every good thing which has
been done iu this world has been effected
only by doing it at what “all right-thinking
persons ” would describe as the wrong time,
and, therefore, this paper always endeavors
to select that time, and its only regret is
that it sometimes makes mistakes and selects
an occasion which, unfortunately, is not so
wrong as it looks.
# * *
The Mount Rennie case differed from the
Dean case principally because there was
practically no after-inquiry whatsoever. The
condemued-to-death Dean got his inquiry
because great public pressure was brought
to bear on the facile Premier Reid,
who saw quite clearly that resistance
would mean the death of his Ministry.
The main causes of this public pres-
sure were that Dean was young and
rather handsome ; that owing to his position
as master of a ferry-boat he was well-known
to many influential citizens, including news-
paper-men ; that there was a sort of pic-
turesque halo round him because he had
earned a reputation for bravery by saving
several human lives at the risk of his own ;
that his accusers were his somewhat fas-
cinating wife and his sinister old hag of a
mother-in-law ; that he was a Freemason and
had the whole Masonic influence behind him
—and that, in a minor degree only (alas,
that it should be so!), his trial was a
parody on the forms of justice. The Mount
Rennie trial was a hideous parody on the
forms of Justice, and it was rushed through
under the presidency of the same Judge, who
“summed up strongly against the prisoners”
—just as he did against Dean, and who took
a hurried verdict from a jury exhausted to
stupidity by day-and-night sittings. But
the Mount Rennie prisoners were not hand-
some, even according to the widest inter-
pretation of the term. They were not
acquainted with any solid financial citizens
or respectable men. They hadn’t been
known to save anybody’s life under pic-
turesque circumstances, and they had no
Masonic influence. Also, they had not
among them one good-looking wife whose
portrait would look well iu the papers, or
even one mysterious mother-in-law with a
history. And the public agitation in their
case, instead of having only to deal with the
kindly and complacent though rather con-
fused Reid, ran up against the metallic
backbone of the dunderheaded Minister
Dibbs, and the callousness of the London
man-about-town in whom, at that time, hap-
pened to be vested the Law’s final preroga-
tive. So Dean was granted a second trial,
and the Mount Rennie convicts, with an
even better case, were hanged or sent to
rot in gaol for life.
* * »
The Mount Rennie prisoners were found
guilty mainly on the evidence of the
girl Hicks and two other witnesses. The
testimony of the former was to the effect
that she was a good, pure girl, who had been
brutally robbed of a woman’s best possession
—her honor. The Court accepted her at her
own valuation. The face of the law was
set against any serious enquiry into the
character of the Crown witnesses—just as it
was set against seriously enquiring into the
character of the Brisbane street-walker who,
figuring in Sydney as an outraged innocent,
got the man Buttner condemned to death,
and all but succeeded in having him hanged
—the jury having been utterly misled by the
woman’s statements and the medical evi-
dence. In fact (as witness the severe judi-
cial rebuke experienced by barrister Cana-
way at the trial), the Mount Rennie girl’s
purity and good character were aggressively
taken for granted by the Court. In much
the same way the irreproachableness of the
unspeakable Mrs. Seymour’s career in her
later years was taken for granted in the
Dean case—wherein counsel for the defence
was sternly reproached for attempting to
disparage the record of the old harridan who
began life as a pickpocket and prostitute,
and was at recent dates figuring, on her
own admission, as pimp to a procuress. The
jury found the Mount Rennie prisoners
guilty because they were led to believe
that the prosecutrix the female who
drove out to a lonely place with a
strange cabman, and thereafter got into
trouble, apparently with (at first) her own
consent—was a pure and virtuous girl, and
because they hadn’t sufficient cool intelli-
gence to see the glaring improbability of
the assertion. The after-inquiry into the
Dean case has shown how a man’s way to
the gallows may be greased by stupidiy-rash
judicial assumptions. A few more such
inquii-ies in past times might possibly have
shown how other men had actually arrived
at the noose from the same cause.
• * *
The two witnesses who made up nearly all
that remained of the case against the Mount
Rennie prisoners were named respectively
Stanley and Smith. Their honor, truthful-
ness, and reliability was also taken pretty
much for granted in the unseemly heat and
haste of the shameful and gory burlesque
which passed as a “trial.” The reliable
Smith, who had been “ruled out ”iu Maori-
land over some turf-trouble, was, shortly
after the Mount Rennie trial, sentenced at a
N.S.W. up-country criminal sittings to a
long term in gaol for arson. The reliable
and valorous Stanley, the Crown’s chief
witness, even while ex-Ohief Justice Stephen
and other credulous admirers were getting
up a testimonial to reward his heroism
in the defence of female virtue, was
stated to have traded off a forged
cheque on a city publican in exchange
for cash and drink. The cheque, by the
way, purported to be signed by a firm who
acted as treasurers of the Stanley testi-
monial fund, of which Mr. Stanley appears
to have been in much need. A criminal in-
formation was sworn and a warrant issued
against the heroic alleged cheque-negotiator,
but apparently so averse were the au-
thorities to discrediting that valorous
person that he was not arrested until a news-
paper reporter took the trouble to point him
out in the street to a policeman as the subject
of a warrant. Then, by some process
which was never explained, the bogus cheque
was paid, and the affair “squared,”
and quietly dropped into oblivion. Stan-
ley, on whose evidence chiefly several
boys had been sentenced to death and
four (of the average age of 18 years only)
actually strangled by the hangman, was
allowed to depart for regions unknown—the
money collected to reward heroism having
possibly been used to compound a fj’ony.
The smothering of this unsavory business
was evidently done by persons of influence-
no others could have stopped the machine of
justice when once it was set in motion. The
action of these persons—whoever they might
be—not only prevented Stanley from being
tried for his alleged crime, but also prevented
a review of bis character and career,
which might have shed a bright and com-
paratively early light on the whole Mount
Rennie tragedy. And as no influential man
or men with position to lose would be likely
to break the law and risk his or their own
safety merely for the sake of a shady person
who had uttered a forged cheque, if indeed
he had not personally committed forgery, it
is only feasible to assume that the squaring
was done to smother impending revelations
and to avert the ridicule to which the hero-
worshippers might have been exposed. So
far, there ha® been no public inquiry as to who
compounded Stanley’s alleged crime, why it
was compounded, and why no action has
been taken against the person or persons,
whoever he or they may be, who thus de-
feated the ends of j ustice.
* * *
The Mount Rennie prisoners were, with-
out exception, defended by junior barristers,
some of whom actually got not one penny for
their arduous task. The accused were, in
one sense, practically undefended—they had
no adequate means of hunting up the
antecedents of the witnesses against them,
and the police and the Crown Law Depart-
ment—whose business is, and has ever been,
to show one side of the case only, and get a
conviction—made no effort to do so. How
this kind of tacit suppression can be effected
is sufficiently indicated by the recent
case of Mrs. Seymour— nearly all the
evidence against whom has come (after the
prisoner’s conviction and death-sentence)
from sources outside the Sydney detectives,
though several members of that force
were unquestionably well aware of the
woman’s character. “To find a parallel to
the Mount Rennie trial,” said the late W.
B. Dalley, Q.C. “one has to search the
darkest and bloodiest days of Irish history.”
It was the most infamous affair of its kind in
all the post-convict days of Australia. The
first day (Monday), the court sat for 11
hours ; the second day, for 141 r hours ; the
third and fourth days (Wednesday and
Thursday), it only sat for hours each day,
and it is notable that, on the evening
of each of these two days, the presiding
Judge attended a fashionable public dinner !
On the fifth day (Friday) proceedings began
at 9 a.m. and went on until 3.30 a.m. on
Saturday (18| hours). Then (the jury and
barristers and Judge having had 5| hours
wherein to eat and sleep and wash)
the Court re-assembled at 9 the same
morning and went on until past 11
at night (over 14 hours), when nine
of the accused were found guilty and
sentenced to death ! The counsel for the
prosecution got its statements in at the early
part of the week when the 12 men in the
box were still fully awake and able to under-
stand what was said to them. When the
defence came on, the Court sat for over
38| hours with only 5| hours’ intermission !
The prisoners slumbered fitfully in the dock.
Read (afterwards hanged) slept in the dock
while his counsel (who had then been 15
consecutive hours in court) went through the
mockery of beginning to address the jury on
his behalf at midnight ; the lawyers dozed
intermittently at their table ; the jurymen
slept at intervals in the box, and “ between
whiles ” passed a salts-bottle up and down
their ranks for the purpose of stimulation ;
one juryman was so ill from sheer exhaus-
tion that his life was said to be in danger,
and counsel might almost as well have
wasted words on a corpse. When an ad-
journment was applied for at midnight on
Friday (by a poorly-paid counsel in the first
stage of phthisis, who declared that he was
utterly exhausted), it was pointed out to him
(thus does history anticipate itself) that
unless the trial proceeded the jury “ would
be kept over Sunday ” —“ thereby throw-
ing on me,” as the counsel afterwards
said, “ the responsibility of choosing be-
tween offending the jury and absolutely wast-
ing my speech on the wretched boy I was
defending.” And so, scrambling on night
and day in the foetid court and the swelter-
ing atmosphere of midsummer, the dozing
lawyers feebly addressing the sleeping jury,
and che prisoners occasionally waking up in
the dock to hear them, the case jolted along
to the midnight verdict and the inevitable
gallows.
• • *
There was practically no evidence, ex-
cept that of the girl, and some police-
reported alleged admissions by certain of
the accused, that any offence had been
committed— i.e., that the girl was not a
consenting party. If a crime was com-
mitted, there was practically no evidence,
save that of the same girl and Stanley
and Smith, that the prisoners were the
persons who committed it. The girl’s iden-
tification of the prisoners was shown to be in
part faulty, for at least one of those whom
she identified was able to prove a conclusive
alibi —iu itself an interesting commentary on
the value of the whole identification. The
fact that Judge Windeyer summed up
strongly against the accused, and leantstrongly
towards belief in the respectability and truth-
fulnessof theprosecutrix, is equally discounted
by the fact that the same visionary has since
practically apologised for Mrs. Seymour,
and seriously lent the light of his counten-
ance to a solemn enquiry as to the authenti-
city of Mrs. Mellon’s ghosts. And the fact
that something transpired which afterwards
made some police official or other person find
it necessary to commit a gross breach of
the law rather than let the career of the
chief Mount Rennie witness be examined in
rhe light of day makes it eminently desirable
that this old iniquity should even now be
fully dealt with by Parliament.
Four of the Mount Rennie prisoners are
dead—they went to make a picturesque
hanging one summer morning eight years
ago ; but the other five still exist, some in
gaols and some in lunatic asylums ; and for
their sake and for the sake of justice their
case and the manner of their trial should
now be reviewed. As a postscript The Bul-
letin here quotes the ineffectual protest
addressed by Mr. T. 0. O’Mara (since dead),
one of the accused’s counsel, to the cold-
blooded Carrington, who, to the disgrace
of justice and civilisation, was then Gover-
nor of New South Wales :
May it please your Excellency:—
At the late trial of the youths accused and convicted of
participation in the Mount Rennie outrage, I appeartd as
counsel on behalf of Robert George Read. At a critical
stage of the inquiry it became my duty to protest in open
court against the manner in which the trial proceeded, so
far as it affected my client. That protest I believe to
be so well founded, that I cannot rest content without
prosecuting it to the very last stage. Hence it is that I
address your Excellency. It is rarely that counsel
obtrudes upon the attention of the Executive that which
he has already bad the opportunity of urging elsewhere,
but there are times when to be silent is a crime, and this
I believe to be eminently one of those occasions. To give
a prisoner—particularly one who is being tried for his life
—a fair hearing, it is not enough that the dry forms of
oiir procedure, humane as it is, be observed. The
accused is entitled to much more. He always receives
much more. He is entitled to demand that un-
due strain shall not be put upon the advocate in
whose hands lie, so to speak, places his life. He has a
right to demand that when his defence is submitted it
shall not be heard by a worn-out and jaded jury. He has
a riaht to protest that at an hour almost approaching to
midnight on the fifth day of a laborious and, to him,
awful investigation, he should not be called to enter on
his last attempt to show a jury that he should not suffer
death, and for a most atrocious crime. And when re-
minded of the value to the jurymen of their own time he
has a riaht to reply, as Read did, through his counsel,
but without avail, that in the determination of an issue
so terrible to him the time of the jury is not for one
moment to be considered, except in'so far as it belongs,
for the moment, wholly to him on the one side, and to the
public, his accusers, on the other. When the question is
whether he is to die the death of a cowardly ruffian, and
when he asks, as he did, for fair opportunity to show that
he should not be doomed to such a death, is it right, my
lord, that he should be told that the jury must not, if
possible, he “ kept over Sunday?” And is he to he further
told that he has had a satisfactory trial, when the most
solemn part of a criminal proceeding, the Judge's
summing up, is delivered to men almost drugged
in some instances into a state of stupor by
the unreasonable exactions put upon the attention
which they were so anxious to bestow upon the
case. Has he not a right to demand that from beginning
to end of this inquiry, so terrible in its consequences to
him, his case should he heard and decided by men whose
faculties have never slumbered for one moment? When
before, rny Lord, in a British community, has it been
seen or heard of, that jurymen have slept, or, rather,
under pressure of Nature, have been compelled to sleep
in open court during an inquiry so awful in its character
as to involve the lives of eleven men ? And yet it is a
fact in this case jurymen did sleep—dead, at times, in
spite of themselves, to the voice upon whose every accent
they were supposed to hang for guidance—that of the
presiding Judge. It is demanded that Read be hanged
because the crime in which he was alleged to have taken
part was unexampled in its atrocity. If that be so, then
let the care with which his guilt is proved, hut, above all,
the patience witli which his defence is heard, be in pro-
portion to the horrible nature of the charge levelled
against him. Do not tell him that those who try him must
not be “ kept over the Sunday.” Do not tell him
that he is to make his last appeal for his life under
circumstances which compel him to make, and most
jusily, open and public protest. Do not tell him that he
was jusily denied the request he made, when he had been
for five days in the dock, for a few hours’ delay that the
jury might again meet, refreshed and more capable of
giving that to which lie was entitled as a most sacred
right—the closest and most intelligtnt attention to every-
one of the few feeble words that were about to be uttend,
for the last time, on his behalf. Do not tell him that
he was unreasonable when, on the fifth day of a prolonged
and exhausting trial —a trial without precedent almost
in the number of men accused, without precedent in its
awful claims upon the attention of all concerned in it,
because it was without precedent in the nature of the
crime charged—he asked at 12 o’clock at night that those
in whose hands he had placed himself, his counsel and
the jury, might have a few hours rest. He asked
this favor, my lord, and asked it, I regret to say, in vain.
If there is anything, as your Excellency well knows, by
which British criminal procedure is nobly distinguished
from that of other countries, it is by its humane and
jealous protection of the rights of an accused person from
the moment of charge to that of conviction. And this
protection is mercifully extended to him, and not, my
lord, as some people appear to think, withdrawn from
him, in proportion to the enormity of the offence of
which he is accused.
Some years ago, when the community, terror-stricken
by the prevalence in our midst of a crime now happily
extinguished, demanded the blood of an unhappy young
man, Sir James Martin addressed to one of your prede-
cessors, on behalf of the cause of calm and temperate jus-
tice, a stirring remonstrance which time has, I believe,
more than vindicated. I wish I could, in the same noble
language, deprecate the unreasoning anger which in some
quarters clamors for the blood of Read, and which,
because an awful outrage has been committed, will only
be appeased by a victim. It is terrible that the
daughters of our people should suffer outrage, hut it is
only less to be deplored that the passion thereby justly
excited should forget (as it appears to me to have done in
this case) the serenity with which English law loves to
deal out its justice—particularly to those unhappy beings
who are called upon to answer for their lives.
I have nothing further to address to your Excellency on
the merits of the case. As it concerns Read, it would he
an impertinence for me to speak if your Excellency is of
opinion that the manner in which he has been tried repre-
sents the true spirit of that which we are all bound to
vindicate, and on behalf of which I have presumed to
address your Lordship —British justice.
I have the honor to be, my Lord,
Your most obedient servant,
T. C. O’MAHA.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Voice from the Piebald Laud.
The voice of the Brisbane Courier comes from
the piebald province of the North :
The Protectionist does not usually venture to demand
that duties shall be continued to the end of time. His
plea is that the duty is necessary to establish an industry,
and his promise is that in a few years, when Prot eotion
has thus done its work, the duty will be abolished. The
exceptional thing about the sugar-industry is that the
duty has not done its work. It has not established the
industry. Now, at the end of 40 years, the industry is as
far from being established as ever, and protests that the
abolition of the duty will be its ruin just as it might have
protested 30 years ago. More than that, according to (he
statement of Mr. KNOXhimself, tbereis not the slightest pro-
spect of its being established. . . Of the 35,640 tons cf
sugar already noticed as imported into New South Wales.
29,840 tons came from Queensland, notwithstanding the
£5 duty. That import represents the power of the
natural home of the sugarcane to push its product against
the fiscal barrier. But suppose the barrier removed 7
Then Queensland would step in to supply the greater part
of the 24,000 tons of sugar now grown in New South
Wales, and the colonies as a whole would be saved the
sums now wasted in the unnatural bolstering of the in-
dustry in the South.
(1) The Protectionist doesn’t promise that Pro-
tection will be abolished in a few years, any
more than Freetraders promise that" Freetrade
will he abolished in a few years. Protection
must go on till wages and hours of labor arc
equalised, either through foreign competitors
rising to the Australian standard, or through
the Australian sinking to their standard. If


neither of these events happen, then it must
go on for ever. (2) The New South Wales
sugar-industry hasn’t been protected for 40
years, for the sufficient reason that it only
began 31 years ago. (3) So far as can be
Fathered from available statistics, the New
outh Wales sugar-industry can stand against
Queensland without Protection, if both provinces
employ white labor. It all turns on that one
question. In both provinces the industry had
to compete against Java, Mauritius, and other
nigger-labor countries. New South Wales did it
by Protection alone ; Queensland did it by nigger-
labor and Protection. The Queensland sugar-
duty is exactly the same as that of New South
Wales; the Queensland sugar-industry, sup-
ported by a heavy tariff and cheap alien labor,
is working under conditions just as unnatural
as those in New South Wales, and it is nothing
short of lying and unutterable gall to quote the
doubly-protected cane-industry of Queensland
as an example of Freetrade and a beacon to her
benighted neighbor. An artificial reduction of
wages, arrived at for the benefit of a special
industry by a Polynesian labor-law, is just as
much Protection as an artificial increase of
prices arrived at by a Tariff Act. Queensland
should prove that her own industry is in any
sense a “natural” one before the Courier
again raises its voice in the Piebald Land, and
tne New South Wales alleged economists who
point to Queensland in this matter should first
acquire some rudimentary idea of the real state
of things in that province.
One Adult One Vote.
The Vic. Parliament met last week, and the
Turner Govt, presented a programme which
fairly entitles it to be regarded as the most pro-
gressive Ministry in Australia, Kingston’s
alone excepted. The Turner combination
proved, by the first few divisions, to have a
very strong majority, and Patterson’s Oppo-
sition crowd was evidently cast down as well
as feeble, and looked like a party with some-
thing lying heavily on its stomach. Therefore,
so far as can be seen at present, Turner’s bills
—even the bill for establishing the principle
of One Adult One Vote : —will go through
the Assembly with comparative ease, and then
the most Democratic of them will be decisively
thrown out by the Council, as heretofore.
The Companies Bill, the Insolvency Bill, and
the various retrenchment measures will prob-
ably survive and become law, but the One
Adult One Vote idea is abhorrent to the House
;>f Rich Landlords, and it is practically born
dead. Nevertheless, there is nothing else for a
democratic Assembly to do but to keep on
passing such bills from year to year, and compel
the Council, if it objects to them, to throw
them back regularly in the face of the public,
and take the responsibility. The Vic. Council
can’t be swamped, because it is an elective
body ; and there is hardly any hope of making
it democratic, for it is a House mostly
elected by landlords, and in which only rich
landlords can sit according to the terms of
the property qualification, which qualification
can’t be altered except with the Council’s own
consent. Therefore, the Assembly, for the
present at least, has only two alternatives.
It can cease to spend its time in passing demo-
cratic measures which it knows in advance will
be lost in the House of the fatted land-grab-
bers, in which case the public will presently
cease to notice that it is the said House of land-
grabbers that blocks democratic progress.
Or it can simply follow the expressed will of
its own constituents, and pass the same demo-
cratic measures year after year, and leave the
Council to throw them out each session and
earn new odium with each rejection, and thus
make the 48 rich landlords keep rubbing in the
fact that they are the one hopeless, stupid,
soulless snag in the misguided Constitution.
The latter is undoubtedly the wiser course. The
Vic. Council wants to reject everythingin theway
of democratic reform. It also wants to pose as an
enlightened and valuable public institution, and
it certainly should not be allowed to do both
things at once. It is almost impossible to
believe that Turner’s Adult Suffrage Bill will
pass the Council, but the time spent upon it
will be well employed none the less. And even
if it is introduced and rejected every session for
the rest of this century, the time devoted to it
will still be well employed. Every time the
Council rejects it, that body advertises itself in
huge letters as being the great, incurable ass of
Victorian politics. The public hasn’t fully
realised this yet, and the fact wants to be
mentioned in a loud voice over and over again
until everybody has heard it and learned it by
heart. Therefore, every time the Council is
made to hang out its vast, hairy ears as a sign-
board, a service is rendered to the country, and
the time when the country will fully realise the
contour of the animal and insist on its removal,
by legal means or otherwise, is brought a little
pearer.
A Model Municipality.
In the Fortnightly Review for April appears
an article entitled “Glasgow: a model muni-
cipality,” which should be read by all those
Australians who barrack for “ private enter-
prise,” and assert, on no visible evidence, that
state and municipal Socialism are costly, ineffi-
cient, impracticable, and an all-round failure.
In Glasgow, the Corporation created and owns
the water-supply, which was brought at
vast expense from a lake 35 miles distant, and
the city has now the purest, most abundant,
and cheapest water in Great Britain. It is
also commencing to supply hydraulic power at
a pressure of lOOOlbs. to the square inch, which
will largely supersede the use of steam through-
out the city. It owns the gas - works, and
since it took them over in 1869, it has
trebled the supply and reduced the price about
60 per cent. It supplies electric-light at about
the lowest rate in Great Britain. In addition
to this it runs the tramways—which it took
over about six years ago. During the period of
its ownership the service has been greatly im-
proved, the hours of labor for the emp'iyes
considerably reduced, the fares have been ;ut
down from 20 to 33 per cent.—a halfpenny is
now the minimum, whilst the highest fare
charged is 2d., and the trams pay all
work in w exp-ms 'nterest on capital and pay-
ment to sinking-fund, and contribute .£9OOO a
year to the general revenue. Also, it is now
proposed to make a penny the maximum fare
throughout the city. The Corporation runs the
“ City Improvement Trust,” whibh buys up and
destroys dilapidated rookeries, and builds, in
their stead, model tenements with wide streets
and every possible facility for comfort and clean-
liness. It builds and maintains seven large model
lodging-houses. The capital expended in these
operations was borrowed at 3J per cent, and
returns 5 per cent. It is also building a Home
for the accommodation of families, chiefly those
in which both parents go out to work, where
the children will be fed and cared for during
the absence of their father and mother, at a
charge of Is. 6d. per week for each child.
Further, the hard-headed city fathers supply
museums, half-a-dozen parks and sundry art-
galleries ; playgrounds, furnished with gym-
nastic apparatus and all kind of games for the
use of poor children ; lectures, Saturday-night
concerts (admission 3d. and Id.) ; and they are
now building a People’s Palace for recreation
purposes in the crowded East End. They con-
trol and pay the local police forces and fire
brigade, maintain the hospitals, and are
steadily extending sewage works which will
ultimately purify all the city sewage and make
the foul and evil-smelling Clyde a pellucid
stream once more. The Corporation buys up
barren lands, fertilises them with the solid pro-
ducts of the sewage • works, and then re-sells
them, and one of its recent transactions in this
line raised the value of one tract of moor by
something like 3000 per cent. It supervises
workshops, factories, and dairies, maintains an
excellent drainage system, cleans the streets
and by-ways, builds its own bridges, and sup-
plies public wash-houses, and swimming and
other baths. These baths are opened free at
certain hours to children attending the
public schools. All this is done out of
a rate of about 2s. 3d. in the £ ren-
tal, and the rate not only covers all
charges but includes payments to various sink-
ing funds. The total debt incurred in con-
structing the water - supply, tramway and
sewage systems, buying up the gas-works, build-
ing new tenements, lodging-houses, town-hall,
museums, art-galleries, wash-houses, swimming
baths, and bridges, opening new streets, buying
up and destroying unwholesome slums, &c.,
was about 10Jj millions, and this liability—de-
spite a steady decrease in rates, a reduction of
60 per cent, in the price of gas, and of about 60
per cent, in the price of water —has already been
reduced by the operation of the sinking-fund
principle to a little over eight millions.
In about half-a-century, it is hoped, the
city will be absolutely free from debt, and then
the profits of the gas, water, electric-lighting
and tramway services will probably go very
near to extinguishing rates altogether. The
people who boost up “ private enterprise,” on
the ground that state and municipal Socialism
is always and of necessity a failure, should
think hard over the results produced in Glas-
gow —likewise those in Manchester, Birming-
ham, Bradford, and other cities, which are
inaugurating a similar policy. If the Aus-
tralian asserts that equally good results can’t
be produced in Australia, then he merely asserts
that he is by nature, or by training, an incom-
petent ass, who hasn’t sense enough to run the
business on commercial principles.

Woman—Old and New.
“ At the baths ” dear to every Australian
youngster, there is always a deep end and a
shallow end. At the deep end you dive in
head-first and swim exultingly : hut, if you
cannot swim, you drown. At the shallow end
you enter feet-first. You need not wet your
head at all, but you can lie on your back and kick
and scream delightfully. The deep end is gener-
ally calm ; the shallow end is the scene of
riotous splashing which first attracts the eye of
the observer, and satisfies the souls of the non-
swimmers who dare not trust themselves beyond
their depth. This is a parable, and here is
the application thereof. The deep end is logic ;
the shallow end is rhetoric. Rhetoric makes
most noise and fuss ; logic is quiet and strong.
With all their bravery of display, the rhetori-
cians know their powers inferior to those of the
swimmers at the other end. But they cannot
swim in argument, and prefer to shriek and
chatter where they are safe rather than take
the risk of drowning in the deep waters of
logic. Now this is the application of the ap-
plication. Woman generically is a rhetorician,
guided by sentiment, intuition, impulse. Man
generically is a logician, relying on induction,
deduction, the lessons of experience, the chain
of cause and effect. There are many rhetorical
men and a few logical women, but the principle
holds in the main. Girls always cluster more
than boys at the shallow end ; kick and splash
more ; are longer in learning to dive from
the deep end. The New Woman, the woman-
suffragist, the shriek-woman, is essentially a
shallow-ender. With rare exceptions, her elo-
quence is mere denunciation of Monster Man,
vague extolment of Virtuous Woman. Now,
Shriek for breakfast, Shriek for dinner, and
Shriek for tea is just as unsatisfactory as the
Toujours Beans of Rue Australia. One’s soul
begins to loathe Shriek, as the souls of the Rue
Australians loathe Beans. There is nothing
to be gained by representing the sexes as
eternally hostile. Even the physiological
opposition is simply a device for completer
union ; the average woman is half a man, as the
average man is half a woman ; and the scientific
imagination goes so far as to declare that every
man is a woman manque, as every woman is a
man spoilt, since each individual contains
the elements of both sexes, and obscure
causes over which the individual has no control
determine at an early period of existence which
set of elements is to become active and obvious
and which to remain passive and hidden. Now,
it is in accordance with the fairly well
ascertained tradition that the best brains
at any given time are on the Tory side (this
involves a very interesting argument not ger-
mane to the present question), that Miss E. A.
Badham, opposing the Shriek-Woman-Suffragist
at last meeting of N.S. W. Economic Associa-
tion, should have dived decisively into the deep
end. Her paper is the most able contribution
to the covs of the woman-suffrage argument
that The Bulletin has seen anywhere ; and,
quite apart from its conclusion, its intrinsic
merit as a piece of argument deserves that it
should be reprinted and widely circulated. But
it is again in accordance with tradition that
the Shriek-Woman should continue to splash
about unconvinced, and the Shriek-Woman
is probably right which involves another
very interesting argument to show that
popular instinct illogieally goes to its mark
in defiance of the most beautifully-constructed
Conservative syllogisms, and that the Conserva-
tives of every generation are ready to admit
that their forbears unluckily failed to notice a
vital flaw in their beautiful premisses. For
Miss Badham’s conclusion is simply this : that
a large number of women do not wish for the
suffrage, and are justified in refusing to take it.
But she does not show, and perhaps does not
desire to show, that the number of women,
small or large, who do wish for the
suffrage, should not have it if they can
get it. Some women do not think with Miss
Badham; therefore, for Miss Badham, some
women are wrong, and she gives many good
reasons to prove them wrong. Miss Badham
does not think with some women ; therefore, for
some women, Miss Badham is wrong, and they
give many good shrieks to prove her wrong.
But—and here is Miss Badham’s flaw —the
shriekers are on a higher ethical platform than
the logician, for they cry for justice, while she
reasons to expediency. Miss Badham’s right
is right for her, but her opponents’ right is
equally right for them ; and in looking
for an absolute national criterion of right,
we must look to justice rather than to expe-
diency. And there is a plain argument from
justice (which Miss Badham does not touch)
that the average woman is, or with a little
training would be, quite as well qualified to
govern herself as the average man. Therefore,
if she wishes for a share in the government,
give it to her; if she doesn’t wish for it, let her
leave it alone. Let expediency, if she chooses,
rule her conduct in relation to herself: justice
her conduct in relation to her fellows.
“Natural” Industries.
One of the results of the sugar-tax controversy
is an inrush of correspondence in the Sydney
daily press pointing out that cane-growing is a
purely exotic occupation in New South W ales,
and one which should be discontinued in the
public interest. The climate, even in the
Northern districts of the province, is alleged
to be too cold, and this and various other cir-
cumstances go to show that sugar production,
under present conditions, is not a “ natural ”
industry, or one which can hold its own, except
by extraneous assistance. Therefore, a large
number of voluminous individuals urge that
this allegedly profitless business should be
abandoned, and the few thousand people who
live by it should take to some natural industry —
some business which is so excellently adapted
to the country that it can live and prosper and
compete against niggers, Chinamen, cheap
Europeans and others under pure Freetrade
conditions. And The Bulletin here rises
to ask, with a loud voice, what is
the name of that “ natural ” industry ?
Even if the whole province was turned into a
sheep-walk it wouldn’t afford sufficient employ-
ment for the male population of New South
Wales, and, besides, wool is becoming every year
less profitable. Wheat-growing cannot be set
down with certainty as a “ natural” industry ;
its existence was always precarious under Free-
trade conditions, and there are any number of
countries which can produce wheat at far
lower cost. Coal-mining, so far as the export
trade is concerned, is unnatural, as evidenced
by the fact that Japanese coal and Japanese
cheap-labor are everywhere cutting out the
Australian miner. Gold-mining, as shown by
the miserable profits of most New South W T ales
goldfields, as compared with those of Victoria
and Queensland and South Africa, is only
moderately “ natural” at the best, and it can’t
employ any very large number of workers with
profit. Apart from wool and fruit-growing and
silver-mining—which, all combined, can’t keep
the province alive— The Bulletin doesn’t know
of one absolutely natural New South Wales
industry, in the Foreign - trade accepta-
tion of the term that is to say, one
industry in which the province can hold its
own against the competition of foreign cheap-
labor and the steadily-diminishing cost of land
and sea carriage—and even fruit-growing is not
a matter of certainty. Most of the industries
which formerly existed under alleged Freetrade
conditions did so because they were heavily
protected by the high rate of freight then exist-
ing, but as freights go down the list of “natural”
industries is steadily reduced till it threatens to
vanish altogether. Therefore, when it is urged
that the exotic sugar-trade shall be abolished,
and the people who live by it shall live by sud-
denly becoming experts in some entirely new
trade which isn’t over-crowded, and which can
live in free competition against the nigger who
is rich on ss. a week and every other rival, The
Bulletin badly wants to know the name and
address of that trade. Are there now even
three really and truly and wholly “natural”
industries in all the temperate regions of Aus-
tralia ?
Obiter Dicta.
Editor Bulletin, —The missionary of paradox
to the English-speaking peoples is dead. His
death is a living paradox ; for the lily of the
city will now toil and spin and yet not be
gloriously arrayed. The death of Oscar Wilde
is sad, not only from pity for the man, but from
pity for literature. His corpse will be gibbeted
on the Via Scholastics as a solemn sermon for
dulness. He patterned after the French, and
wrote to show the Teuton a new possibility of
the pen ; hereafter his style is to be associated
with his shame to scare the writer from new
paths. This will be done by dull and pious
people to whom wit is a real danger. Wilde
alive strove well for a new literature ; Wilde,
dead in his oakum, will push back with strong
force other—possibly better and brighter—
workers in the same field. Sad is the death —
and yet sadder its manner. The man has been
hacked down by the conventions which he
outraged. A wholly mistaken idea of law
—an unripeness of legislation —is the cause
of his fall. He was a sinner; judged by a
normal, healthy-minded standard, a vile sinner.
But the law should not deal with sinners but with
the outragers of the rights of others. It is no
business of the human code to punish vileness;
if so, why are some of the horrors of monopoly
and adultery and poverty and prostitution un-
heeded? Of old. Parliaments and powers
deemed it their duty to uphold the Decalogue
and to roast people into Heaven. Modern en-
lightenment tends to leave to reason or religion
the maintenance of morality, and to reserve for
law the mere keeping of order. Such is the
logic of legislation ; but it has not yet been
fully recognised. A year or two hence Oscar
Wilde would be flouted by decency, but be of
no more interest to the policeman than was
Socrates or Sallust or Alcibtades or Horace
to the law-keeners of their time. The man will
probably never crawl out of his prison ; if he
should, he will then see that he died two years
before because of one of the last limitations
ivhich convention had set upon the logic of
legislation. —F. R.
[The Bulletin has received about 40 letters to this
effect, and it prints the above to satisfy a certain class
of correspondents and to enable it to remark that
Wilde's “new literature” was not so valuable that its
extinction among the oakum should matter much. And
the law had a right to squash Wilde as an “outrnger of
the rights of others.” It is the right of every callow
youth not to be exposed, it the law can help it, to the evil
influence and example of a dirty-minded fat man. Wilde
was, in a moral sense, a dead oat in an alley, and the
Sanitary Department got on to him, which it had every
right to do. —Ed. B.]

Dear Bulletin, —In a recent par. yon make
some assertions which seem at variance with
existing legislation. Yon say: “If Baronet
Newnes stole or received valuable stolen
literary matter taken from an English paper he
would have to pay damages; but as Miss
Turner happens to be an Australian writing
for Australian papers she cannot claim the pro-
tection of the English Copyright Law; for
English copyright applies to all English pos-
sessions, and so runs in Australia ; Australian
copyright is not operative in England.” This
is hardly in accord with the provisions of the
International Copyright Act (1886), by which it
is enacted that the Copyrights Acts shall apply
to a literary or artistic work first produced in a
British possession in like manner as they apply
to a work first produced in the United
Kingdom, provided (to get out of the legal
jargon) (a) that it shall not be required to be
registered in the United Kingdom if it can lie
registered in the place of “ production,” and

TO DYSPEPTICS

We assert that Granuma Porridge Meal will
prevent or cure all forms of indigestion in persons
with whom there is nothing organically wrong,
and who will obey the simple laws of health.

will not supply it, write to us, and we will cause

who keeps it.
JAMES INGLIS & CO.,

(6) that no copy of the work is required to be
delivered to any person in Great Britain. The
Copyright Acts, I need not explain, give all the
necessary powers to the author or his assignee
to prevent pirating. The colonial copyright
has received a very considerable extension since
the passing of this Act, and it may be posi-
tively stated that a book or any public literary
matter produced in Australia obtains at once
the same copyright throughout the British
dominions that it would have enjoyed if
first produced in the United Kingdom. A
certificate or document purporting to be a
true copy $r extract from the register of
copyrights in a British possession, signed by the
officer keeping the register and authenticated
by the Public Seal of the British possession or
by an official seal or signature of a Governor or
Minister, is admissable as evidence, and all
courts (throughout the British dominions) shall
take judicial notice of it and admit it without
further proof. The British accession to the
Berne Convention or International Copyright
Union carries the colonies en bloc into that
union, and authors in the British possessions
have protection or copyright practically
throughout the world. All that would be re-
quired to enable The Bulletin to sit upon
Baronet Newnes is the authenticated certificate
of copyright referred to above and proof of
publication. Any court in Great Britain then
would do the rest. Why does not some per-
sistent young barrister take up this interesting
branch of law ?— Fred. Walsh.

There is so much Irish drama in this
world that an impression is beginning to prevail
that Ireland spreads over three-fourths of Europe
and Asia, and is peopled by 10,000,000 evicted
farmers, 5,000,000 hard - hearted landlords,
60,000,000 humpbacked informers and process-
servers, and 100,000,000 colleens, bog-trotters,
pigs, squireens, priests and policemen. Maggie
Moore’s new drama, “Sweet Innisfail,” is rather
better than most of its kind, however, and Miss
Moore’s adorable accent lends it an unwonted air
of realism. It begins with a farmer who is cast
out'by a gouty despot because he can’t pay the
rint. One of his daughters is secretly married to
the son of the gouty marauder, while the other is
engaged to a brilliant youth whose sole visible
means of support are a red waistcoat. The son
gets midnight skull-fracture in a cave and
becomes a lost wanderer without a memory.
The farmer and his two girls starve in a
cabin till the old man takes to an aimless
kind of sheep-stealing, accompanied with tears
and general decay of the legs. The youth in the
red waistcoat has a number of gaudy fights with
the police, routs the gouty landlord in a rough-
and-tumble scrimmage, drives off 18 armed
Fenians, casts the squireen into a lake, also
wallops him, and arrests him ; likewise, he gags
the process-server, hangs him once or twice, drags
him a considerable distance over a rough country,
throws him through a window, captures him in a
wood, and kicks him frequently. And at last the
process-server finds the incriminating documents
in the squireen’s coat, and the mad girl recovers,
and the skull - fractured man who was lost is
found again, and the landlord is shot at, and the
hero’s cabin is burned, and the process-server is
tarred and feathered, and the squireen is arrested,
and the drama wins in a canter, and they all live
happily ever afterwards.
* • •
The honors rest mainly with Miss Maggie
Moore, Miss Lynda Raymond (a new addition to
the co.), Mr. Roberts, and the process-server.
Maggie is as good as she always is; the new lady
promises well, though her want of anything
resembling an Irish accent makes her seem a very
improbable sister to Miss Moore, whose brogue is
the loveliest thing of its kind that ever grew in
all the wide region from Ballyhooley to Killaloe ;
and the versatile Roberts is good all round. Oily
Deering’s process-server is humpbacked and has
amjmpediment in his legs, as a matter of course—
all process-servers and informers are built that
way. But, in addition, he is about the most
original and refreshing crawler who ever rushed
on one short leg and a long one through the
domain of Irish drama. “Sweet Innisfail” is a
large, vigorous, rousing production of its own
peculiar kind, though it gets hurried and inco-
herent towards the end ; and it is adorned with
several really beautiful scenes, and one which isn’t
beautiful and looks like a mountain of raw beef
with a tree sticking out of it sideways,
* ,* * *

“ Mam’zelle Nitouche,” the
story of the good girl of the con-
vent-school who goes out “on a
tear ” and drinks more champagne
than is good for her, and goes on
the stage, and disguises herself in
boy’s clothes while her accomplice,
the organist, gets drunk and has
his hair cut, ought to be good for
much longer than a week at
the Lyceum. On its last appear-
ance this opera was powerfully
supported by the “Living Pic-
tures,” but the present experience
shews that it can hold its own
without the aid of an apparently
nude female in a big gilt frame.
“ Mam’zelle ” is not so much an
opera as a comedy set to music.
Nellie Stewart and the organist
have a most awful night out, and
get back over the garden wall in
the early morning, much after
the fashion of the “ Magistrate ”
when he had been chased by
police and stray dogs over half
London. This isn’t especially
original, for half the comedies in
existence consist in a good man
coming home with a bashed hat
and a black eye in the early
morning, and explaining matters
at great length ; but Nellie
Stewart’s bad girl comes home
looking fresh and dainty, and gets
back into her convent dress, and
puts her bright young head
under the tap, and is all the
better for her experiences. As
for organist Lauri, he is an amus-
ing wreck and a dreadful example
of _ the evils of the accursed
drink. Apart from these two,
nobody matters very much. A
few other characters bear a hand,
but Nellie and the oppressed
organist and a ballet of eight are
practically the whole show. The
Bulletin mentions with regret
that “La Cigale ” will take the place of
“ Nitouche ” on Saturday.
** * *
The “ County Wicklow,” at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), has the merit of being exactly like every
other Irish drama that ever existed. There is
nothing in all this world which has quite so much
sameness in it as the Irish drama. All the charac-
ters begin with “ O.” There is a camel-shaped
informer, with one bow-leg and one knock-knee ;
the primitive Irish huiuor can only express the
moral crookedness of the informer by making him
a toad-like wreck. Also, there are a fine old man,
a holy father, some soldiers, two beautiful girls, a
noble-hearted rebel, and a good deal of whisky.
The rebel is a tiresome person, who never does
anything in particular, and who talks like a burse
cistern about Ireland ; he can’t even eat half-a-
slioe of bread without rising between each bite to
make a column or two of remarks to the effect
that Ireland will yet be free. The consequence is,
that he doesn’t get a chance to digest his food pro-
perly ; no man could digest properly who always
gets up with his mouth full and the crust
in his hand to state that his bleeding coun-
try will rise again at some date not yet
fixed. Some day, The Bulletin is going to
express its feelings on this great subject by writ-
ing an Irish drama of its own. In it the rebel will
not make one eloquent remark about Ireland all
through, and will have a chance to get his meals
properly : there will be no whisky ; the colleen
will be a plain girl with bare feet, and there will
be twelve handsome informers, which is probably
about the right proportion. Also, the pig, which
seems to be unaccountably omitted, will be a lead-
ing character; and the hero will be justly hanged
for being a bore and a weary repetition, and for
always being on the point of doing something and
never doing it.
* * #
Be Charles Erin Yerner, The Bulletin
has come to the conclusion that the greatest Irish
comedian is a most over-rated person. J. W.
Sweeney, as the informer, bears the heat and
burden of the grammar, and never appeared to
such advantage as when alongside the exceedingly
unconvincing Yerner. The other characters
tackle their brogue with more or less success, and
speak feelingly about whisky, and deliver long
essays about their bleeding country, and keep the
top buttons of their shirts unfastened, after the
manner of the true patriot, whose swelling bosom
would otherwise blow that top button off with a
loud report. The very latest news is that Mr.
Yerner has sprained himself and will give the
public a well-earned rest, and that that really fine
drama, “My Partner,” will be staged on
Saturday.
** * *
Smythe’s latest lion, the Rev. Haskett
Smith, who roars at present in Sydney Y.M.O. A.
Hall, is a joyous old parson who has seemingly
lost a good deal of his orthodoxy in the course of
many years’ exploration of Palestine and Syria.
He is alleged to have spent 25 years in delving
among the mysteries of that sacred and rather
dirty land, and he knows it thoroughly from one
end to the other. There is very little of it at the
best, but what there is seemingly full of old
ruins, broken walls, traditions, lying guides,
and flies buzzing round dead camels. The
lectures are illustrated with innumerable views,
and these views do much to knock the reverence
out of the business. The utter poverty, squalor and
melancholy commonplace of blighted Palestine,
the horrible resemblance of its towns to the
Chinese quarter of a decayed goldfield, the gloom
of the scorched and yellow land, and the blank
The Late VIOLET VARLEY.
misery of Jerusalem—that little city that slum-
bers at the far end of a by-road which leads no-
where—all stand out conspicuously. Parson
Smith doesn’t attempt to emphasise these matters,
but they loom up none the less, and, after hearing
that learned orator, The Bulletin’s desire to
roam over the Holy Land is dead beyond hope of
resurrection.
** * *
Mr. William Walshe, the young Australian
tenor, better known as “Billy,” to whom the
Sydney profession are giving a benefit prior to his
departure for America, is a native of Melbourne,
a large and once-flourishing town in one of the
adjacent provinces—near Geelong. For a long
time he drove a quill in Melb. Water Supply De-
partment. Acquired some fame also as a
cricketer, playing for E. Melb. Played for Yic.
against the first Australian Eleven. Was a pupil
of Mme. Lucy Chambers, the famous teacher of
singing, and sang with the Melb. Metropolitan
Liedertafel and Philharmonic Society. Made his
operatic debut at Melb. Princess’, with the Emelie
Melville opera 00., as principal tenor in “Fati-
mbza,” and, after playing a round of operas with
them in Australia, accompanied them to India,
China, and Japan, where, with the dashing
Emelie, Yerdi, and Farley, they coined money.
Walshe remained in the East two years. Returned
to Sydney, and sang the tenor music to Sullivan’s
“Golden Legend,” with the Sydney Philhar-
monic, and the “ Messiah,” with Mme. Patey.
He oannot remember how often he has sung for
various charitable objects all over Australia. He
leaves for America per Alameda, after the benefit
concert next Saturday, June 6. He is six feet
high, and every inch an Australian, has a good
quality of voice, and all those who know him wish
him well. Australia is a place for good tenors to
get away from. We always export our best, and
get shoddy in return—in music as in commodities.
** * *
If the Melb. theatrical carnival of ’94 was
disappointing to some people, who wanted a more
tangible entertainment than they got for their
two shillings entrance-fee, last week’s show, at
Melb. Exhibition, made up the shortage. The
gate-money was only one shilling, and the nimble
“bob’’went a long way. Iu the concert hall a
variety-show was available for anyone who could
get there; a programme of sports, commencing
with a children’s race and ending with a costume
football orgie, kept the quadrangle crowded from
two to five o’clock. Until four o’clock, or so, it
was even possible to make a round of the
pavilions, and get occasional glimpses of Beauty
without paying a sixpence toll. Blanche Massey
and Florence Lloyd were included in the legiti-
mate shillingsworth of joy all the time, and this
for sound financial reasons. They conducted a
Monte Carlo establishment, with the assistance of
three other ladies, and pushed the sale of metal
tickets over a circular counter. Blanche Massey,
with her fair young head tilted to one side, and a
besee.-hing smile in her business eye, travelled
round and round the inner ring lisping “Two
shillings to win sixteen.” When about 16 metal
tickets had been sold (for 325.) somebody set a
race-game in motion, and the winner got half the
stake at one fell swoop, the other half remaining
to aid the good cause. The tickets were then
gathered in and Florence Lloyd took up the syren
song, “Two shillings to win sixteen!” Monte
Carlo offered no hope for a man who wishes to
break the bank, but it was a very fashionable
slaughtering-place for 3| hours. A more primi-
tive sort of race-game, known as a “ doodlein-
buck,” was on tap elsewhere. Twelve gamblers
staked a shilling apiece. The winner netted six
shillings profit and the doodlembuck carried over a
balance of six shillings to its next account with
no more compunction than a bookmaker feels
under similar circumstances. However, the girl
who gathered in the spoil was nice enough to eat,
which Ikey Mo isn’t.
• * •
The main hall of the Exhibition Building
contained 11 pavilions, large or smaller, so there
wasn’t too much space left for several thousand
visitors to walk about in. Important personages
found themselves shoving one another unortho-
doxieally in the main hall. James Service and
Justice Webb got jammed at the opening to
Decima Moore’s pav., along with the sheriff’s
officer, who had come to the Carnival on the off-
chance of being able to stick a formal demand for
“costs” into a certain divorce-court petitioner.
Lady Clarke and her pretty daughter were so
tightly drawn into a vortex of influential citizens
on the threshold of Louis Bradfield’s enclosure
that they had to enter “on the nod” and pay
when they came out, Lady C.’s credit being
good at charity fairs. It cost a shilling at
first and two shillings as time wore on
to hear Bradfield give a few imitations of
actors and sing a little in his private tent. He
wasn’t an “astounding bargain” at the price,
although he had a splendid sale. In the tent
immediately opposite Johnny Gourlay and Co.
played the fool at brief intervals for sixpence, and
Benno Soherek, the musician, treated waiting
audiences to some of his card-tricks. Scherek’s
tricks leave a smell of brimstone behind them.
One feels that the gifted Pole may be called for
at any minute by a tall, dark gentleman in a red
suit. At four o’clock or thereabouts, the rush of
humanity to the choicest Beauty pavilions was
arrested by a well-thought-out system of sixpence
to go in. Maud Hobson’s bower was free to gentle-
men because that stately Hebe dispensed drinks
from a bottle, and the male sex is supposed to
mean business when it sniffs pizen. Ladies had
to pay sixpence for the privilege of viewing Maud
at close quarters, also they ran a serious risk of
being lured into ordering lemonade at a shilling
per glass. Consequently, they preferred, as a
rule, to stay outside and gnash their little teeth.
Grace Palotta, in another shop, sold tea and cake,
therefore women were allowed to gaze upon her
gratis, whilst men dropped another sixpence into
the fund. Miss Palotta’s pavilion seemed to be
the particular haunt of coupon-sellers. The
pleasure of buying a Beauty Prize coupon was
intense. You got one for sixpence, and, having
got it, filled in the name of your idol and posted
the document in a box. No charge for postage.
• * •
The Doll House and Richardson’s Show
panned out well, especially the latter. This was
a gorgeous entertainment for sixpence, and Bland
Holt took the money at the doors. They
played “ Hamlet ” to crowded houses as often as
they liked in Richardson’s. The tragedy always
opened with an apology from Harry Monkhouse,
who regretted to mention that the melancholy
Dane’s tights wouldn’t fit anybody larger than
little Fred Kaye. Soon afterwards the company
came on and died, all of them save Hamlet’s
mother. She had retired to nurse a grievance
against the management. The old lady was an
Ophelia of 40 years standing, and for the first
time in her life Monkhouse had asked her to play
the Queen. Actors and actresses in all directions
worked hard to make their theatrical carnival go,
A good deal of the money taken within the build-
ing was the reward of sustained effort. The
side-showmen pegged away steadily, and the
people who had created and arranged the whole
business travelled around to see that it was good,
and the final result was a profit exceeding .£lOOO.
** * *
The Gaiety Co. having vacated Melbourne
Princess’s on Friday night, and a verdict of tem-
porary insanity having been recorded against a
mob of shattered old johnnies who saw them off,
the B. and B, Co. took the stage on Saturday.
Melbourne had not quite recovered from its bad
attack, so there were a few seats to spare in court
when “ The Case of Rebellious Susan ” was called.
Susan’s case didn’t promise to overcrowd the
court at any future time. It is neither very
dramatic, nor strikingly humorous, nor full of
moral instruction, yet it seems to express a pro-
found belief in its own importance, like all plays
by H. A. Jones and Ibsen ;
That’s about the size of Susan’s case. She
set out with the intention of “ carrying on,” and
when the inevitable lover had hugged her, on
their way home from church, she was horribly
afraid that the gentleman might brag about
it. She thought she desired to assert a principle,
whereas she probably wanted an ordinary co-
respondent, who denies everything that has hap-
pened, on oath. Author Jones doesn’t appear
quite certain that his heroine wanted as much as
this, and he brings her back to her husband for the
sole reason that the story wouldn’t be pretty if bn
left the unsatisfactory rebel wandering about at
Poor rebellious Sue
Laughed at what was due,
Due to cold propriety, in a warm sassiety.
When her husband went astray,
Lady Susan ran away;
Took a trip abroad,
Spooned on Cecil Ward ;
Sentimental, flighty Sue
Had a mind to try a new
Masculine variety.
Poor rebellious Sue,
In a year or two —
Funking notoriety, viewing with dubiety
Love of an “ illicit ” kind,
Got a chance to change her mind.
Cecil went astray,
Much to her dismay.
Accidental-convert Sue
Started married life anew—
Settled down to piety.
Had Cecil Ward been true
To rebellious Sue,
Soothing her anxiety, whims, and contrariety,
Then she might have stayed abroad
Snorting at her lanky lord,
Till in legal course
He obtained divorce,
Leaving her to live a life
Commonplace, as Cecil’s wife,
Wearied by satiety.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone wjio mak. s
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats
large. Another sample of wife is introduced—an
uproarious, ginger-headed innovator who preaches
a new morality to the women of Oiapham, and
commits penal offences against law’norder.
“ Ginger ” is the author’s excuse for asserting that
woman’s true sphere of usefulness is her own
fireside, which ancient saw issues from the
mouth of a moral philosopher, one Cato, Q.O.
with an aggressive air of being new and original.
But the question of woman suffrage, &c., is not
affected by the vagaries of a casual Clapham
lunatic. “The Case of Rebellious Susan” is a
bright little play, all the same, and if the character
of Susan’s husband were tolerably well acted the
general effect might be almost impressive.
# # «
Mr. Dorrington is a very young man in an
unthankful part, and he walks through his hope-
less task with despair writ large upon his legs,
Miss Noble, as the heroine, is Miss Noble at her
best in a character which hangs rather loosely
about her narrow style. The ability of Tither-
adge and Boucicault is even more than usually
conspicuous. Miss Temple scores a few notches,
and Miss Faber’s excellent performance will make
her a Melbourne favorite immediately.
** * »
“The Union Jack” is re-appearing at
Melbourne Royal, after an absence of six years.
It re-appears to much better advantage than
before in several respects, and has only one very
weak spot. Miss Housley, late of “The New
Boy ” Co., is unequal to the sad responsibilities of
the girl who’s been betrayed. Slow music doesn’t
harmonise with Miss Housley’s stage walk, nor
has grief marked her for its own. The ex-board-
ing-school miss is about two feet shorter than the
average height of a villain’s dupe, and a good
hearty curse would blow her away. Edith Blande,
in a long, melancholy cloak, is the true type of be-
trayed womanhood for melodramatic purposes ; n~
other type fills the bill. There are two active and
intelligent monsters of infamy in “The Union
Jack.” One of them holds the other in
subjection with forged papers, until the hero
tikes a flying leap from captivity through
the open port of a man-o’-war ship, and
surprises the first villain in a drawing-room at
midnight. When the hero has shaken his hated
rival like a dawg he goes away, whereupon the
second villain stabs his colleague, recovers the
incriminating papers, and charges hero Baker
with the gory deed. Baker is somehow saved
from the gallows, by Mr. and Mrs. Bland Holt,
and marries a ward of the wicked corpse, whose
other ward has been suffering persecution from
the real murderer. Or else it was the murderer
who had the two fair wards, and the corpse that
assisted him to plot against the men they loved.
Anyway, the grave closes over both of them after
the other one gets hanged for his crime. Miss
Hilda Spong is earnest and vigorous, as well as
sweet, on this occasion, whilst Miss Virgie Vivienne
is equally charming within light comedy limits.
W alter Baker’s nobi! ity of character i s only exceeded
by his muscular strength at a crisis, and the twin
pyramids of iniquity are feelingly represented by
Albert Norman and Cosgrove. Familiarity with
crime has hardened A. Norman considerably of
late years. His scowl improves steadily, and his
moustache is full of evil. Bland Holt shares a
large slice of the drama with his sweetheart, Mrs.
Holt, an interloping soldier (Charles Brown)
coming in for a few of the crumbs. “ The Union
Jack ’’is strong in comic relief and scenery, and
all that makes melodrama lively. Also, it shows
signs of having been partly constructed by the
literary hand of Sydney Grundy.
** * *
Mr. Clifford Halle’s lectures on “ Singing
as an Art,” at the Athenaeum Hall, have fully
justified their deliverance. Clifford Halle talks
of matters he understands in an entertaining way,
and at the conclusion of a lucid address he gets
his precepts home to the hearts of his hearers by
showing them how he himself can interpret a few
test ballads. Mr. Halle’s singing is the best en-
couragement that a student can receive, inasmuch
as the professor’s voice seems to be quite
an ordinary baritone organ brought to a high
state of perfection. Halle, phre, the pianist,
implored his accomplished offspring to go to Aus-
tralia and impress upon the rising songsters of
this country the necessity for hard graft; and
echoes his parent’s opinion that Australia
will produce plenty of fine singers when the gene-
ral desire to “come out ” and shout, after three
months’ tuition, has been sobered down into a
determination to Work. There is no “ haw ”
about Clifford Halle, for an artist who preaches
Work, with a capital W, has never yet been
known to “ haw ” in this world.

At Poverty Point.
Lalla Miranda’s accent, when she sang at a private
concert in Paris, was voted “rather agreeable than other-
wise ” by a critical French audience. Every sort of
foreign accent has a charm if nicely' rendered, even when
the speaker is dealing with his, or her, own language. A
cultivated Bostonian talks the brightest English, and a
ditto Britisher talks the brightest American. A provin-
cial turn of the tongue can please a metropolitan ear all
the world over, vice versa. Isn’t that so?

denly fainted the whole funds of the 00. weren’t sufficient
to buy her a glass of brandy.
Carmencita, the Spanish dancer, now starring in London,
has a curious notion of the duties of a manager or agent.
Having had occasion to complain of some press criticisms,
she ordered her agent (Leopold Jordan, erstwhile of
Sydney) to go at once and thrash the editors. But Mr.
Jordan didn’t.
(( Dear Bulletin, —The most graphic touch in that little
“In Town ” scene has been unnoticed. The captain gets
a bundle of letters. He rejects all as duns—except one,
addressed in a female hand. In apparent confirmation of
the idea that there is a lady in the envelope the captain
discovers it is perfumed. Then, of course, he opens it—
to find he has been “ had ” by a clever creditor.—F.
It seems that the bilious Gilbert had fair reason
for his last quarrel with Sullivan. There was a nice
girl in the case. A revival of “Mikado” was under
consideration, and Gilbert proposed to bestow the part of
Yum-Yum upon his friend Miss Nancy M’lntosh. Sullivan
and p’Oyley Carte scouted the idea. Gilbert went away
in his wrath to find a new musical collaborateur, and
wrote “ His Excellency ” for the main purpose of giving
Nancy a good show. Nancy is a success. Gilbert has
never made a really bad choice of an artist for a leading

An ingenious writer in a Melb. paper says that the
French lozenges now on the market, which contain
essence of whisky, brandy, gin, &c., will do away with the
necessity of going out between the acts to “ see a man.”
Absurd ! Most of the time men go out in order to be able
to curse the play, and particularly damn certain of the
actors, and criticise with more or less delicacy the legs of
the actresses. Besides, even if this were not so, the state-
ment referred to is unreasonable. Where is the lozenge
that will give the essence of a long beer?
Another example of the fact that men are far more
modest than women, writes a Bulletin correspondent. At
the evenings of Melb. Shakespe . ; Society, when unex-
purgated plays of the immortal Lard are read by a num-
ber of the members of both sexes, each impersonating a
character, the men on reaching any dubious passage im-
mediately Bowdlerise the text by skipping the lively por-
tion , or, if not quick enough to notice what is coming,
they read the words in an undertone amid blushes and
with obvious confusion. Not so with the women. With-
out the slightest nervousness they go straight ahead and,
giving full value to the expressions, declaim them in, if
anything, a louder tone than the accompanying unblush-
ful letterpress. [Accounted for by the absolute innocence
and guueiessness of the women.— Ed. B.]

Dear Bulletin,— Melb. critics fairly yelled with delight
over “In Town,” and called W. and M. to book for not
putting this on first. There is positively no righteous
excuse for this beyond Bradfield's gentlemanly and clever
clowning, and for that they' were content to sacrifice the
unctuous Monkhouse, Kay, Bantock (the latter tw>o do
nothing in particular in “In Town ”) Maud Hobson, and
Grace Palotta—all delightful entities in “The Gaiety
Girl.” Misses Hobson and Palotta are merely visionsin the
later production. The literary material in “The Gaiety
Girl” is smart, that of “In Town” mere shoddy—not a
line worthy of quotation marks in a mile of it. The fact
is London critics enthused persistently over “In Town ”
as the first of its kind, and the Melb. critic’s opinions are
always imported ready-made—he is the least self-opinion-
ated man in creation.—Yours, Skull.

Several Melh. suburban ball proprietors object to the
various juvenile minstrel co.’s playing- in their halls on
the “silver coin ” principle, on the ground that it lowers
the status of the halls by causing roughs to congre-
gate. At South Yarra, where the manager of a juvenile co.,
who had been showing locally to crowded “ silver coin ”
houses, made a direct charge of 6d. all round, in accord-
ance with the hall-proprietor’s instructions, only 85
people attended, and the show closed down.

Mrs. Edmund Phelps, the duchess of “In Town,” never
acted under her connubial name until after the death
of her famous father-in-law, Sara Phelps, 16 years ago.
She had been long a widow when that happened. In
the old days of Webster and Chatterton’s management at
the Adelphi and Princess’s, Mrs. P. was known as Miss
Hudspeth. She played all sorts of characters creditably
—lrish heroines, comic slaveys, or Jack Sheppard, in the
antique drama. As comic slavey she could sometimes
force a laugh from old man Phelps, starring in the same
company, and he didn’t easily lose his countenance on
the stage. Besides, he couldn’t forgive her for having
married son Edmund without his (Samuel’s) permission.
All things considered, she must have been very funny.
