Dear Bulletin,—" M.” says he knows how Oscar Wilde
does his “factory-made epigrams,” and he doesn’t like
them, anyhow. Why doesn’t “ M.” publish a couple of
gross to prove he understands the mechanism? Was it
“ M.” who originated the remark that Hamlet is only a
collection of popular sayings ? “ M.” tries to depreciate
Oscar Wilde’s success as a playwright by cheap allusions
to the French —“ M.’s ” depreciation is the sincerest form
of flattery. Oscar Wilde and Israel Zangwill have given
an epigrammatic tone to English literature. An epigram
proves the unfamiliarity of the obvious—and when some
wit says: “To be intelligible is to be found cut” it is
easy for little critics to pooh-pooh. An epigram is the
nexus between the preposterous unreliability of Fact and
the reasonableness of Fiction ; a small round stone from
the brook Life slung by an expert hand at some Goliath
not of Gath ; an octopus with its gloves on ; an arrow
pointed by pessimism and fledged by optimism ; a sweet
cynicism, a convincing absurdity, a triumphant soap-
bubble. There is a market for “ M.’s ” epigr: ,ms so that
they be made in the factory where Oscar Wilde makes his.
But this is not “M.’s” business. Oscar Wilde may be
pecked at by daws now—and the fear is that “ M.,” hav-
ing had a peck, may want a quart. In the meantime,
here is a Wildeism for “ M.” to beat from the French or
from his own magnificent mind: “There are only two
tragedies in life—one is not getting the thing one wants—
the other is getting it.”—N.

George Bernard Shaw, in Saturday Review: “I have
twice had to resign very desirable positions on the critical
staff of London papers of first-rate pretensions—in one
case because I was called upon, as a recognised part of my
duties, to write corrupt puffs of the editor’s personal
friends, with full liberty, in return, to corruptly puff my
own; and, in the other, because my sense of style revolted
against the interpolation in my articles of sentences
written by the proprietor’s wife to express her high
opinions of artists unknown to fame and me, who had won
her heart by their hospitality.”

“ Charley’s Aunt ” has been played in Europe,
America, and Australia, up to date, more than
7500 times. Penley has only a lease of it for seven
years, with the option of continuance for a short further
term. After that it reverts to Brandon Thomas, who has
kept nearly all rights but the English, and (The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent believes) Australian. Penley
and Brandon are not such warm friends as they might be,
but they don’t openly quarrel. The bone of contention
is Penley’s story of his part in making it the success it
has been by cuts and additions. Brandon’s tale is quite
a different one. “I alone did it,” is the burthen of his
song. Anyhow, “ somebody ” did it, and did it very
well, making a much finer gold-mine than the London-
derry.

The cruel scandal of the second Synnot
(Melb.) divorce-suit doesn’t affect the question
raised in the first suit. Synnot originally sought
to break the tie that kept him from marrying a
wife who would live with him, by producing evi-
dence that he is now married to one who will not
live with him. Twenty-two years ago Synnot,
admittedly “excited ” at the time, slapped Mrs.
Synnot’s face. She left him. He made her a
handsome allowance, after begging her to return
home. Less than a year later he inaugurated a
series of attempts to persuade Mrs. Synnot to
change her mind. No result. Presently Mrs.
Synnot’s lawyer asked for a deed of separation
and a settlement. Synnot offered to*j>ettle his
entire fortune on her if she would return
“home.” Not she. Synnot, “believing he was
acting in the best way to effect a reconciliation,”
signed a deed of separation, by which he settled
.£lO,OOO on the lady he had married. That helped
to settle his chance of getting her hack, presuming
there was a chance remaining up to then. Mrs.
Synnot, with her .£lO,OOO, was adamant. What
he had done for the best proved to have been done
for the worst. From time to time Mrs. Synnot
rejected her husband’s social suit with scorn, and
when he ultimately tried a legal suit tiiat likewise
was a dismal failure. Mrs. Synnot set up the
separation deed, which was an impenetrable
defence for that circumspect matron, and threw
in the ironical complaint that her “ husband
before God ” had committed a lot of adultery since
she (under whatever provocation) put herself
asunder from him in 1873. That is the Synnot
case divested of wild allegations and judicial
hysteria.

Dear Bulletin, — It is a mistake to think
that, to procure the deportation of an absconder
from justice to Melb. from Sydney, the punish-
ment for tiie alleged crime must be “ a year bard.”
Sec. 13, Part ii., of the Act, enables deportation,
where a warrant has been issued in an adjoining
province, for “an offence punishable by that
colony ” —so that it is possible for a man to be
extradited on a mere chargeof insulting behaviour.
Even an absconding witness in a criminal case can
be extradited. The 12 months’ imprisonment
mentioned only applies to extradition between
N.S.W. and the U.S., or Canada, or Africa.—
Shark.
Q. Judge Real had a case for decision, the
other day, hinging on a point not unlike the crux
in the famous Q- Investment Co. cases. Ex-Chief
Justice Lilley decided these cases against Arthur
Palmer and Co. to the wrath of the Govt., which
brought Judge Windeyer from Sydney to review
his decision—it was upset. Eng. Lord Chief
Justice Davey, by-tlie-by, afterwards backed up
Lilley, and the only reason the Investment Co.
didn’t proceed with the appeal was that if it
had won it would have lost, seeing that it
had to prove on dummied titles. The point was—
could a valid sale be effected of land conditionally
granted by Govt., when the conditions had not
been fulfilled. Real remarked (following Lilley)
that he thought the sale was invalid, hut shirked
the point by permitting the sale of interest, if any,
without deciding the question of legal title.
That curious tale about the discovery of
the North Pole, by a person whose, name sounds
like a cat-fight beside a waterfall, has seemingly
collapsed, and the world is still left wondering.
The discovery of the Pole is a simple matter, after
all, and the fact that no one has found it is re-
markable. There are two ways of discovering
any place ; one is to go there, and the other is to
come back and say that you have been there. The
Pole will probably be found the latter way. Some
day the solitary survivor of some lost expedition
will straggle in and say that he has been there,
and as there will be no one to contradict him, the
great mystery will be solved there and then, and
the survivor will be knighted and probably get a
pension as well. There is very little chance of
him being bowled out. All he has to state in the
way of description is that he arrived at a place
which was covered with ice and looked like every
other place thereabouts, and that be saw a walrus
in the distance. The Bulletin regretfully
wonders why an Australian doesn’t find the Pole.
There are some fine liars in Australia.
SIN AND SYN-NOT.
Synnot’s wife, a chilly dame,
Is not witii Synnot mated :
Although his money gilds her name,
They’ve long been separated.
Synnot cried, “My Jot is dree
If wifey’s love I win not !”
“No more of married life for me,”
Responded Mrs. Syn-not,
Synnot sued for a divorce,
His missing missus haughty
Discovered what was true, of course—
Poor Synnot had been naughty.
“ This story of your wrongs I pray
You in my ears to din not;
“ Go, sinner, go your evil way !”
Responded Mrs. Syn-not.
Synnot lost a hopeless case,
And, later on, repeated
His vain attempt; but in disgrace
He slunk away defeated.
The lesson of this legal strife
Read, mark, and learn —and grin not.
He cannot wed a proper wife,
Nor live with Mrs. Syn not.
If matrimony’s moral aim
The Church has truly stated,
All husbands really ought to claim
The right of being mated ;
And we, who judge by Nature’s laws,
For lawyers care a pin not,
And find that Synnot sins because
His wife is Mrs. Syn-not.

At the opening of Yic. Parliament, the
“very highest polite sassiety people” carefully
1 uddled themselves together on the side of the
House opposite-to that on which sat Mrs. George
Turner, the Premier’s wife. Mrs. Premier is not
exactly in favor with the “lydies” of Melbourne—
having much of her husband’s undiplomatic
brnsqueness of speech, and few of the monkey-
tricks of the pseudo-refined imitators of Cockney
manners and customs. This ostentatious piece of
cutting was an act of gross ill-manners of which
only “ lydies ” could be guilty.

One of the surprises in the list of boom-
defaulting prisoners just released from N.S.W.
gaols, was the name of Andrew Fitzgerald. By
most people it was thought that he, at any rate,
would have to serve his full five years. He was
managing-director of the hideously-mismanaged
and now defunct Trades and Labor Co-operative
Store, and within a few months of the formation
of the society, he managed to “snavel,” say,
£2ooo—all made up of the hard-earned shillings of
workmen.
And it seems passing strange that Francis
Abigail should have been released while M'Namara,
tried and sentenced along with him, is still im-
mured. In sentencing M'Namara, Judge Win-
deyer called him Abigail’s “ evil genius,” but, at
the same time, he just as severely castigated
Abigail. Evidently M'Namara’s friends are not
of the right sort. M'Namara is a rascal and knew
better than Abigail how many beans made five ;
but he is suffering from cancer, and deserves any
pity which may fairly be accorded to a prisoner
on account of physical infirmity.
W, P. Smairl, released after serving half his
time for embezzlement, was manager of the
Imperial Building Society. He played up the
shareholders’ money in yachting and cutting a
dash generally; still, he was a convenient scape-
goat for a number of far more influential per-
sonages, some of whom have reason to thank their
stars for a variety of things.
At one Sydney banking prosecution, the writer
sat in court near an old man, a blacksmith.
Against the advice of the wife, who for half-a-cen-
turyhad toiled and saved with him, he had invested
the whole of his life-earnings, nearly ,£2OOO, in a
r Aten, politician-bossed savings-trap. Of course
he lost every cent. His wife died broken-hearted,
and the old man, who bad only ceased work when
past 70, had to go and live in the poorhouse. No
one possessed of any humanity can fail to grieve
at the sufferings of the faithful wives who, with-
out share in their husbands’ misdeeds, have
sorrowed at their disgrace and worked hard for
their liberation ; hut such cases as that of the old
blacksmith —only one out of a thousand victims —
have cried aloud for vengeance and have hardened
the public’s heart.

The theory lately ventilated in The Bul-
letin that Mrs. Dean was possibly never poisoned
at all is being worked out before the Commission,
Judging from the weighty medical evidence
already given in support of this view, it is plain
that the Crown will find the gravest difficulty in
fully demonstrating that Mrs. Dean’s system ever
held a single grain of arsenic. As to discovering
where the poison was bought—well, you can buy
arsenic and morphia tabloids from any chemist in
Australasia without signing the poison-book.
• * • ♦
One very important aspect of the Dean
poisoning case would he much better understood
by the public had the wood-merchant, who stated
before the Commission that Mrs. Seymour threw
water over him, been encouraged or allowed to
tell his story in full.

No less than three Consuls ( vide Age)
sufficed to support the dignity of the black Re-
public, Liberia, at the Melb. Birthday levee.
Even Servia was represented by a Consul. Scores
of foreign gentry emerged from obscurity with
multi-colored button-bole ribbons representing,
in some way or other, various insignificant petty
States of which nobody had ever heard. It is not
four years since that a “Colonel” (aged 23) bad
the right of entry to certain Government ’Ouse
official feeds on the strength of his appointment
as Consul for a country whose Consulateship in
another province was publicly on sale for <£s. It’s
a good job that a Consul has to wear a uniform at
official feeds the cost of hiring a second-hand
one effectually bars a Governor being eaten out of
bouse and home by every Tom, Dick and Harry
who can get some sort of Consulship for the price
of a long beer.
To call the proposed new Sydney Sunday
penny paper the Sunday Morning Herald will
be a clumsy infringement of the S.M. Herald pro-
prietary’s rights. Morning Herald is the name
of Sydney’s oldest daily ; to call the new paper
Sunday Morning Herald will be to clearly imply
connection with the daily. There are thousands
of good names for newspapers which have never
been used, or even thought of, and such a lack of
originality argues badly for the success of the new
venture.

The other night, one of several johnnies
occupying a Melb. Princess’ Theatre private box,
noticing a “devilish fine gel” in the front stalls
beneath, artistically telegraphed a kiss at the
very moment her husband chanced to look up and
observe the cause of her smiles. He arose and
'-uitically shook his fist at the johnnie, who,

seeing the audience enjoyed the fun, retired
behind the curtains. It was reserved for a few
spectators to witness the stirring scene which
subsequently occurred in Spring-street, wherein—
contrary to expectation—the johnnie, after being
roundly abused, waded in and gave the husband
“ the father of a bating.”

People have wondered why more has not
come of a late “society” Melb. libel-action. A
suit to remove the unsuccessful defendant trustee
from office was on the lists, but it is said that care-

consequently, as there was intestacy, there is no

* * m
The proposal by the late N.S.W. Swimmers’
Conference to establish a swimmers’ “Legion of
Heroism ” is simply a disgusting instance of
medal-hunger—prompted by the same sort of
motive which induces a Malicolo boy to wear a
fish-bone in his ear. No decoration is worth a
rap which does not come from a disinterested tri-
bunal. The proposed league of heroes would be
a vulgar and unblushing mutual-admiration
society.
The Frock-coated Democracy is coming !
At the Melb. Davitt reception the majority of the
Labor M.sP were trussed up in the sinister gar-
ment named. Hancock and Prendergast were
specially conspicuous. There is already a sort of
Sunday-school superintendent aspect about the
working-man representative, which his outbreak
of frock-coats on special occasions accentuates.
Davitt, in his plain brown suit of tweed,
was a striking contrast to the highly respectable
local Balbuses. By the way, if Prendergast
and co. were acquainted with a certain theory
associated with frock-coats, they would have their
own proud possessions in that line converted into
home-made pants forthwith.
• « *
The notable Bradford-Powell will-case
(N.S.W.) has to lie re-argued, Mr. Justice Foster,
who originally heard the case, having retired from
the bench on pension on the ground of ill-health
before delivering bis judgment. The case is
clearly one in which the costs of the first trial
should be paid out of the Public Treasury. No
citizen should have to pay for justice twice over.
The Bulletin wants to hear any argument to the
contrary.
# # •
Dear Bulletin, —Should that breeched
abstraction, chivalry, hold good when a scraggy
she-male in baggy knickers astride the ■ ncertain
bike violently collides with your person 1 One
old gentleman in Melb. evidently thinks so. On
a recent Sunday I saw a bevy of cyclists careering
up Oollins-street, among them several “ rational-
ised ” women. On turning a corner one partiou
larly attenuated she took a short cut across the
pavement, where her machine fell, bringing down
with it and herself a portly old gentleman who,
thinking his assailant a mere reckless male, was
preparing to utter terrible curses, when he noticed
from the loosened strands of feminine hair that
he had been laid out by the New Woman. Where-
upon he arose meekly, doffed his hat deferentially,
expressed profound apologies, picked up the scat-
tered fragments of the cycling woman, and cour-
teously helped her to re-mount. I never saw
chivalry so grossly misplaced.—R.C.B.
Men and women riding on tandem bicycles
—tlie woman in front, the man behind, as if to
typify the altered relations of the sexes—are
beginning to be not uncommon sights in the Melb.
streets. They are profane spectacles to anybody
with a grain of romance left in his composition.
Woman ceases to be an angel, even by courtesy,
when she bestrides a bike.
# * •
Mrs. Fred. Want and her daughters, says
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, intend to
return to Sydney shortly, but will not remain
long. Poor Fred’s death was somewhat unex-
pected. He had been ill, but latterly seemed
much better. Like his lamented brother Ranny,
he went out like the snuff of a candle.
* * *
A lawyer doing fair business was
divorced from his wife some time ago, £i a week
/limony being granted against him. Both lie and
his former half again dipped into the matrimonial
lucky-bag, the latter securing a rich husband. As
the £4 was a mere fleabite compared to hubby
No. 2’s income, hubby No. 1 thought he might
discontinue the alimony, and petitioned the court
to that effect. But he was unsuccessful ; and now
Jhe lady always insists on getting her four pounds
of flesh, so that the legal limb is working his
tongue out of joint merely to exist himself and
provide this revengeful one every four weeks with
,£l6, which she usually carefully places in the
poor-box or gives away to a Chinese mission.
# * *
A Melb. dame lately appealed to “ Tatter-
sail ” Adams to supply her on credit with 50
tickets in the V.D.L. Bank lottery and 50 in a
Melbourne Cup sweep. “One of these tickets will
Certainly win a prize,’’ she wrote, “and then you
can deduct the cost of the tickets from the prize.
Please to be so good as to oblige in this instance.
It will benefit all round. God will bless you for it
and give you every success in your new under-
taking.” But even her further assurance that she
had been for many years a member of the Wes-
leyan Church didn’t help the transaction through.
m * •
Few know that most of the Melb. churches
subtract the average of their ordinary collection
from the special collection taken up on Hospital
Sunday. Only the balance goes to charity. The
pastors, unlike “ Tattersall ” Adams, take more
!han 10 per cent.

The hard-up police-magistrate scandal still
blazes in N.S.W., and should be ended without
delay.
# # #
In Chinese laundries it is customary for
the Chow, when wetting starched clothes, before
ironing, to spray the water on them with his
mouth (as he also sprays wedding-cake when icing
it) instead of sprinkling with the hand. A
person wearing underclothing that goes through
this process should certainly feel far from comfort-
able. If the laundryman happens to be con-
tagiously diseased there is probably no surer
method of passing-on the trouble.

St. Patrick’s R.C. Cathedral, Melb., which
has been 46 years in construction and is now to b-
completed within a few weeks, has a peal of bells,
cast in Dublin in ’52, whereon it is inscribed that
they were cast for the “ Catholic Cathedral, Mel-
bourne, South Australia.”

More than one man who lately went from
Australia to float more or less wild-cat mines in
London, and who started out wearing, as it were
patched trousers and a dickey, has returned in
purple and fine linen, and accompanied by—a
typewriter. Lady type-writers, by the way,
seem to be much preferred in successful mine-
promotion circles to the male article.

N.S.W.
The recent reappearance of Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis as Hamlet, at Melb. Bijou, surprised a lot
of people who hadn’t previously seen the old-time
favorite since her return from Hingland. Mrs.
Lewis has disposed of much solid flesh of late
and although the figure of her Hamlet
doesn’t suggest nervous disease, it compares
favorably with the outlines of several male
tragedians in the same capacity. Absurdly high-
heeled shoes and a superfluity of false hair on her
head and face were the chief objections to this
lady Hamlet. She spoke her words excellently,
Photo, by Talma.
Mrs. G. B. W. LEWIS, as Hamlet.
but for some reason or other her memory
proved “fluffy.” “One never forgets Shakes-
peare,” as veteran Alfred Dampier used to say.
Yet Mrs. Lewis, who ought to know her Prince
of Denmark by heart, was often “stuck” for a
word the other night. Her shoes were to blame,
probably. They must have been giving the
estimable artist a bad time.
A small one-horse S.A. butter-factory
lately presented a case of butter to G.O.M.
Gladstone, who forwarded it on to the poor of
Hawarden. So far, neither Mister Gladstone nor
the poor have been beard from, and opinions in
S.A. as to the strength ot that cow-juice are
becoming pronounced.

Daring hospital self-denial week in Melb.
a tarpaulin was spread out inside St. Paul’s

Harry Monkhouse (whom the
writer remembers as a slim young man
in pantomime at the Grecian Theatre,
when Oscar Barrett conducted the
orchestra, and clever artists were cheap
in that neighbourhood) is the one dis-
tinct humorist of the George Edwardes
Co. He creates characters after his
own large image. If his deliberate,
easy-going method be a seedling from
that of Paul Bedford, or Odell of old,
it is none the less original and up to
modern requirements. Monkhonse’a
impersonation of the melancholy mum-
mer, changed for a time to a clockwork
figure in “The Mountebanks” —a part
identified with little George Lauri at
this end of the world—was a particular
success “over yonder.” As for his
long Mariner bold (Snazelle’a character,
here) in “Paul Jones,” it was one of
the sights of London which photo-
graphed themselves on the cockney
heart. Harry Monkhouse is essentially
an English comedian—there can be on
doubt about that—and his name fits
him like a glove. We have had a sad
experience of at least one comedian
who brought a very Hinglish reputa-
tion with him, therefore it seems neces-
sary to mention that Mr. Monkhouse
conjures upjiorecollections of the old-
fashioned joker in question. .His per-
formance in “A Gaiety Girl -—the
only bit of Monkhouse that Melp. has
sampled at tirns of writing—excited a
general craving for more.

Melbournian: “My new parson
lias a buggy and pair, which, these
times, simply slays him,”
From the Judge's remarks in sentencing
Oscar Wilde, it may be surmised that accused was
charged under what is known as Labouchere’s Act
passed a few years ago to meet decisions which
quashed convictions in this class of offences where
both were consenting parties. The maximum
penalty under the other law is 15 years. It is a
curious reflection on English jurisprudence that
while Italy and Austria were studiously omitting
all reference to these offences “by consent,” and
so practically squelching the most odious of all
classes of blackmailers, the Britishers were enact-
ing such a law, thus practically inviting the
11 unemployed ” “over there.”

“The only child of Major and Mrs.
Blank” is how a Brisbane paper puts it. You
cannot be too careful.
Parson Hunter, of Fitzroy (Melb.),
devotes himself largely to the reclama-
tion of the bad youths of his parish, and
does it with little success, but with a
hopeful heart. His pupils frequently fall
from grace, and when they are run in
they mostly call on him for testimony as
to character. Then the kindly old
preacher gets into the box and swears
that he always had great hopes of the
wild-haired larrikin in the dock, and
regards him as having been “ led astray,”
after which the Bench generally gives
the offender three months. The Bench
used to be touched by the pastor’s re-
marks, and let down the offender lightly,
but it found that nearly every rowdy
youth in the district has “ had ” the good
man at some time or other, so now when
he gets into the box and testifies, it
generally gives the offender one month
for his misdeeds and two months extra
for taking down the benevolent old
preacher,
• • *
The Bishop of Salisbury reports
to a gathering of English clerics that the
commercialism displayed by the Aus-
tralian clergy is something awful, and
that the churches down-under “ compete
with each other like hotels.” Yet the
British parson’s commercialism enables
him to grab all the fat Australian
“shops,” and Australian parsons are too
humble to compete with the imported
brother for a well-paid Bishopric. The
unctuous critic proposes to cure the
ungodly state of things he describes by
setting up Church Establishment in Aus-
tralia. Wise and gracious prelate! A
big, bloated monopoly would certainly
cure competition. The Bulletin meekly
wonders if the establishment of the
Anabaptists or the Plymouth Brethren
would suit holy John O’ Salisbury ?
While R.O. Archbishop Carr, Anglican
Bishop Thornton, Presbyterian Pope Rentoul, and
other big guns of the different churches were
thundering defiance at each other over the ques-
tion of the unity of the churches, the Rev. Mr.
Williams, of the Church of England, and the Rev.
W. Trebelcock, of the Wesleyan Connection,
were taking part in the presentation to Rev.
Patrick O’Reilly, R.C. priest at Bacchus Marsh,
Vic., of a purse of sovereigns and an illuminated
address. An eminent military authority once
remarked that the hope of the British army lay in
its non-commissioned officers. Similarly, the hope
of the churches may lie with the inferior clergy.

A remarkable feature of the big theatrical
carnival at Melb. Exhibition was the enormous
preponderance of the sex in the crowd. Women
were everywhere, and in the enthusiasm with
Photo, by Falk.
HARRY MONKHOUSE, of the Gaiety Co.
which they fought and clambered and clawed and
tore to get a glimpse of “ those Gaiety creatures ’’
they left the much-abused “Johnnie” absolutely
nowhere. About each counter and stall through-
out the afternoon was a dense circle of women,
ten deep, gazing upon the presiding “ girls ” with
expressions varying from a soulful gloat to a
scornful sneer. But the eternal female didn’t buy
—she just stood there, as grim as bricks, and
delivered her hubby and cousins and brothers from
the great temptation.

A new custom: Melbourne barmaids, when
quitting one hotel for another, advertise the fact
in the dailies, the announcement being followed
by an advt. from the new boss, stating that he has
secured the treasure.

Except at one hotel, drinks are still a
shilling each at Goolgardie. The town is over-run
with sly-grog shops, at any of which a request for
“lime-juice” is followed by the production of the
whisky-bottle. The publicans have entered into
a combination to keep up prices for the present.
A bottle of whisky bought across the bar-counter
costs 12s. 6d.
A CARNIVAL PURCHASE.
A rosebud on ray table lies,
A thing divine :
It seemeth dead to other eyes
Than mine, than mine.
Its petals shed a fragrance rare
For me alone,
A perfume which no morning air
Hath ever known.
Yon rosebud bringeth heaven near
To me, to me.
O, fragile, floral souvenir
Of ecstasy !
I bought it from an actress (whom
Do you suppose ?)
And in my heart ’twill ever bloom —
Palotta’s rose.
Melb.

The human body is a victim to those
dreadful enemies, the microbes, and to prevent
those treacherous foes to approach you there is no
better method than to keep yourself clean and not
allow food to decay in your mouth or the clogging
of the epidermis. To prevent these things, one of
the most leading chemists in Sweden brought out
his world-renowned Amykos, which, without
doubt, is the best toilet requisite with which to
gargle your throat, wash your mouth and face.
Amykos is strongly recommended as a preventa-
tive of sore throat and bad breath.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
June 4, 1895.
My DbarMoorabinda—
Sydney University Dramatic Society broke out
brightly at Sydney Royal Standard, one evening
last week, in “The Critic,” by Sheridan, preceded
by “Cousin Zachary.” The first piece was quite
d Vamateur, the performers seeming to be all
elbows and feet—but if it was put on for the pur-
pose of lengthening the entertainment, then
“ Zachary ” was a huge success, for it made the
first part seem 14 hours long. It was permeated,
by the way, by a real, live actress, who proved
her title clear to the regular stage by the rush of
rouge to her cheeks and her sang froid whenever
a wild stage-cuddle loomed in the “ business.”
The “ Suds ” always stage some dramatic
antique (nothing of the “Withered Leaves”
calibre for them!) and Sheridan’s “ Critic ” was no
exception to this golden rule. The first act was
the more polished, and in it Mr. Gregan M‘Mahon,
as Sir Fretful Plagiary, beautifully dressed in
lavender satin, embroidered vest, and clouded
cane gave another clever character sketch—a line
of business in which this gifted amateur would
shine in any company with a little more general
stage experience. Mr. J. P. Jones, as Sneer, appears
to be quarrelling with his part, but he has a good
stage-profile and a pair of legs to go upon. Mr.
H. De Lissa fitted the part of Dangle like a new
glove; Miss Wilton (professional) was a good
Mrs. Dangle, with Mr. Pickburn, as Puff, a
flattering critic, such as theatrical managers love.
He spoke too fast for his points to tell, otherwise
his diatribes would have been quite as applicable
to the modern “society” journalist as to Sheri-
dan’s creation. I think it was rather a mistake,
though, for Puff, who was dressed in black tights
and a black coat, to have the tails of that coat
lined with white! From some points of view, it
rather suggested the good old nursery-rhyme of
“ Giddy Giddy Gout.”
The hilarious burlesque-rehearsal of the second
act was more of a romp than a “reading,” but
still the audience roared with laughter, as it
was meant to. It is no unusual thing to see
people convulsed with merriment at amateur-
shows—the worst of it is, that it generally happens
when the’tears are playing “ Hamlet.” In the
funny part, Mr. Pickburn was too aggressively on
deck to be pleasing, but Mr. Le Gay Brereton,
Mr. Y. Creagh, Mr. MacMahon, and Miss Roseby
—a very graceful member of society, who wore
diaphonous draperies, and a hat like a big white
petunia blossom—all did excellently for the home
team. Tilburina was played by a plump and
humorous professional damsel, in white, who re-
frained from claiming the whole of the stage, as I
have seen pros, do under similar circumstances.
After the play (which was fairly well-mounted
and excellently dressed) part of the audience went
on to the stage to felicitate the performers to the
clink of glasses, for the “Suds” are nothing if
not hospitable. By the way, the veteran Charles
Burford still coaches the ’Varsity mummers.
A novelty last week was the appearance at Syd-
ney School of Arts of a blind concert co. which
gave a press and medical invitation matinee.
These blind musicians are from the Ormond
College of Music (Melb.), and are going under the
charge of Mr. and Mrs. Webb on what I predict
will prove a successful tour round the world.
Mrs. Webb’s brother is one of the performers,
so it’s quite a family party. The co. pre-
sents a pleasing ensemble on the stage, the girls
(more cheerful under their affliction than the
youths—women are so adaptable!) in matric.
gowns and mortar-boards, and the youths in
evening dress with boutonnihres. If the
manager had not to move them about the stage like
chessmen they would look just like ordinary folk,
and listening to the clever performance would
never suggest to you that the performers can-
not see. The repertoire of the co. is excellently
chosen, and Mr. Grey, who has a clear and ring-
ing tenor voice, Miss Wotton (soprano), Miss
Drummond, a merry-faced girl with a clear, arch
soprano, and Mr. Bartlett (a born comique)
appear to be the stars (if there are any stars in
such a good all-round co.). The way they played
the violin, piano, flute and clarionette was a re-
proach to many gimlet-sighted ignoramuses who
can’t play two bars of anything.
Blindness runs in all shapes. For instance, a
well-known Sydney man, verging on the unmen-
tionable age of 60, must have been very short in
his worldly sight to be so taken in by a larky
matron as the newest story says he was. It was
the old yarn with a brand new denouement. He
approached her on the ferry-boat—Mrs. Guelph,
from Blank? He was sure he knew her. The
dame, who knew perfectly well who he was and
what she was doing, assented readily and chummed
in. “ Have you lunched ?” said he as they went up
town. “No.” “ Then may I have the honor of
dining with you ? ” He wasn’t blocked there,
either, for the quarry assented once more and
named a fashionable hostelry, whither they repaired.
Like the Duchess of Plaza Toro, the lady had
“ wine with her dinner,” and afterwards Lothario
(who is “by no means a beginner” at flirtations)
tenderly enquired if she would like a drive or a
nice walk I “Oh, thank you, I must go to the
jeweller s,” replied the little lamb —“ but you may
come if you like.”
At the jeweller’s the hoary Romeo suggested
that she would like a nice little present from him.
The lamb assented nimbly, and chose a handsome
bangle eleven guineas—which the wolf paid for
without a murmur. Outside, a drive to Dash, a
frisky watering-place, was suggested, but the
syren had other purchases to make, and, the wine
working vyonders, Don Juan persisted in present-
ing her with other articles of value. Then came
the denouement in the Strand! “Now shall we
go and have tea at Dish?” “I am so sorry to
refuse your very kind offer, but, really, I have to
meet my husband at 5.30,” she said, frigidly, “so,
good afternoon,” and away she sailed, bangles and
all. The story should have gone on to relate how
at that moment the wicked one’s own wife turned
up, and the curtain fell on a baffled Bluebeard with
empty pockets going sadly and respectably home
to tea. The moral (if there is a moral to such a
business) seems to be “Never pay in advance.”
Give me back all those presents I promised
vou, t ’ said Danby, in “Cinder Ellen”—a speech
no “man of the world” (meaning thereby the
flesh and the devil) was ever known to smile at.
The conversazione to the new Chancellor of
Sydney University—J udge W mdeyer—eventuated
in the Great Icechest (I mean hall) of the afore-
said hotbed of learning the other Wednesday, and
was a polite success. The only jumble-up was
over the inner reception committee or absence of
it, and people zig-zagged in the stone doorway
and made passes to shake hands with inoffensive
citizens sitting near by in the draught, who glared
ferociously, upon which the entrants plunged
blindly into the bosom of the assemblage in an
absolutely unwelcomed state. Evidently, the
cream of the milking was hanging round the front
door waiting to receive the guest and his party,
for I noticed a great rush of robes in Judge Win-
deyer’s wake when he came in to slow organ music
at 8.30.
The Chancellor rather set my teeth on edge, for
he wore a strictly correct (from a ’Varsity point
of view) but brick-dusty mingling of terra-cotta
and vermilion on his robe. Lady Windeyer wore
amber satin draped with cream lace, and carried
a posy of pink roses—altogether, the Chancelloress
looked more sartorialiy up-to-date than we are
accustomed to see her. The dressing didn’t seem
to be phenomenally lovely (though I liked a
dainty white trimmed with the very finest black
lace in a manner suggestive of fern-iike splash-
work or pen-and-ink drawing). For one thing,
the ’Varsity men’s robes kept one’s nerves on the
jar so. One professor of science was parrokeetish
in red and Erin-go-bragh gown ; another bag of
learning on two long legs suggested Portia or
Card. Moran in his scarlet gown ; another pro-
fessorial person did violence to chromatics
in a gown picked out with red and purple,
and, as the colors were fighting behind his back,
they didn’t make his eye ache as it deserved.
I think the very ugliest robe was a black one with
a long, diamond-shaped patch of scarlet edged
with rabbit-fur. The effect was that of a rabbitter
with one gory skin thrown over his shoulder.
Of the gowned ladies a petite B. A., without her
mortar-board, looked magazine-picturish, but a
welter-weight matrieulator was unwise to sport a
small, round red fan, considering how very wide her
face was—it suggested a mole on the moon. A
tall girl-graduate had a bunch of pink roses stuck
under her mortar-board, with incongruous effect.
I can’t say I like the cap worn indoors of an even-
ing—it makes you think of fancy-dress; and
when a girl wears flowers under the classic head-
gear it looks as if she were poking fun at it. Dr.
Women-Only Zenana Ryder (a bit of a spookist,
I’m told) turned up and didn’t seem to have much
to say for herself—l suppose because there were
men present.
A neat musical programme was provided,
arranged by Mr. Barff. A new lady vocalist sang
as one would expect a highly-trained tom-tit to
warble, but Miss Colborne-Baber (she goeth ’Ome
next year), in pink, sang, “I arise from dreams
of thee ” most charmingly, and later on she and
“Daddy ” Hallewell gave a pleasant rendering of
the duo, “La ci darem.” Daddy, going alone,
made a great hit with “She alone charmeth
my sadness;” and Mr. Harrison White ac-
companied the singers, besides contributing
organ soli. After that, Professor David gave
some “ pre-historic peeps” in one of the
outer schools; with lantern slides showing the
great megatherium, likewise the leviathan, com-
bined with a few sidelights on the moa. After
which there was a great rush for light refresh-
ment, and more conversazione, every woman
making the same remark about how thoughtful it
was to put rugs on the cold marble floor. And I
expect most of the men thought so, too, even if
they didn’t say so, for cold feet aren’t a feminine
monopoly, I presume.
Talking about the University reminds me that
an erudite lady connected with the seat of learning
aforesaid tells another good story which will stand
repeating. A medical - studentess —a tall and
strapping Swedish girl—was walking home down
a London street at midnight’s solemn hour, when
the inevitable man approached and requested to
be allowed to walk with her. The studentess was
equal to the opportunity. Taking a cool glance
round, she said, airily, and in her most gentle-
manly manner : “ Oh, certainly if you’re
frightened!”
I hear, by the way, that in Sydney Medical
School two, at least, of the lecturers, when they
arrive at the more confidential portions of their
discourse, turn the girls out and enlighten the
youths alone, and then they turn the youths out
and let the girls into the wrinkle. But don’t you
think this is mere squeamishness on the part of
the professors aforesaid ? For if a girl can absorb
medical wisdom from the lips of a strange man
(and medical professors are generally very far re-
moved from the male old-woman type) then I fail
to see why she cannot hear it in men’s company.
There is safety in numbers, also.
Apropos of medical schools —a quiet-looking
girl got married, the other day, and most of the
medicos in and around a certain Australian city
sent her a cheque because she was the best
“subject” in the city hospital for some time
owing to her supposed incurable hip-disease.
All the doctors gave her up—but they
didn’t waste her—they utilised her as a living
object-lesson, and paid her handsomely too, and,
in her turn, she earned her fees. When a lecture
was on or an argument got up. Hip-disease was
unclothed and weights put on her feet, and then she
was strung-up, like mutton on a hook. Dr. Saw-
bones and his troupe of green students would come
along—lecture and argue, and turn her round and
round—talk more scientific jargon, and go their
way. Then the wardsman would take her down,
revive her, and string her up again in time for the
next medical demonstrator and his grisly crew.
The odd part of it was that, through all these
ordeals, she was bright and happy.
“Mam’zelle Nitouche” had a bright and glorious
resurrection at Sydney Lyceum last Saturday,
though there were one or two little rifts here and
there. For instance, Nellie Stewart (who makes
a touch-and-go Nitouche—it suits Nellie to have
lots to do) had a new lilt in place of the tuneful
Nautch song, though there was nothing to justify
the alteration. I couldn’t hear what the new
warble was about, but it went to a clashing of
things like tin-pot covers and ought certainly to
be called the Saucepan Lid song. The only re-
deeming thing about it was that Nellie wore the
old familiar flesh-colored jersey with a rainbow
skirt, a blue zouave and barbaric ornaments. The
tricky jersey, by the way, was the only garment
which showed signs of wear and tear, for it
wrinkled round the waist more than usual, and
thereby lost some of its—force. Flo. Young was
the one who wore a new dress—a fresh-butter-
colored brocade sprigged in pale-pink, with
shoulder-flaps of venetian-green —the polonaise
opening over a pale-pink petticoat, draped in
white lace. A black velvet hat on one ear,
powdered hair, dimples and diamonds, completed
the handsomest get-up in a radiant show which
only weakened when novelties were billed instead
of old numbers.
For instance, the new version of the green-room
wasn’t half so exciting as the old pas when the
ballerinas tumbled like a house of cards in the
position (I mean the girls were) of a wide-open
pair of scissors. Beside the startling pose even
the highest kicker of them all fell flat and unpro-
fitable. Wallace Brownlow, as Vicomte, has a
part that suits his military-man-in-mufti style
down to the little tin spurs. Both Ids songs (he
wisely stuck to the old ones) were artistically ren-
dered and loudly encored. That is the penalty they
pay on the stage for doing good work. They encored
Nellie S., who is beginning to look pale and tired
(now don’t laugh—l know actresses’ complexions
are never according to nature, but all the same
any intelligent person can discover a pale woman
even under four inches of grease-paint !) for the
energetic “Drum Song” in the last act, and in
vain she shook her golden head at them.
I think Lauri must keep all his parts in pigeon-
holes to be taken when wanted, for his Celestin
is the very same Celestin as of yore —only that
when he got on the stump and brandished a Home
Rule green umbrella it looked for a moment as if
Mr. Michael Davitt had come to town. By the
way, the frisky barrack-scene (I believe this part had
to be very prudently translated to suit English sus-
ceptibilities) rather pricked open the recent Sydney
military scandal (the authorities, cn parenthe.se,
are getting out of the fix they ought never to
have been in by granting the hero prolonged leave
of absence). “Is there a lady in the barracks?”
enquires the Commandant; and 1 fully expected
a still small voice from the gods to reply—“ Ask
General Hutton.”
There was a crowded house, including a gentle-
man who had just had his nose put out of joint,
and who appears to be plunging wildly into the
society maelstrom to drive dull care away. I
think it is a great sign of despair in a mar-
ried man when his dress-suit never has a night
off. By the way, I saw a new dress-weskit
at the opera—cream and satin with small black
buttons ; but ’tis not enough. No johnny is a
real johnny, don’t you know, in anything less
than silk stockings, knee-breeches and buckled
shoes—though heaven knows how New Women
dread the terrible disillusion which must come
when men unveil their lead-pencil understandings,
for, judging by the bicycle youth, the art of calf-
padding is still in its infancy.
Regarding Premier Reid’s pert message, if the
English big-wigs really want to punish G.H.R.
for his little boutade they should take him at his
word and let him discover a home-made Gov.; and
then Potts Point would rend him limb from limb
in baffled rage at losing the chance to be a sister
to a real live peeress. Many unauthorised rumors
are in the air re the vacant Governorship, but
there is nothing definite as to who will fill dead
men’s shoes. Perhaps the aristocracy are super-
stitious, and dread a ghost on the battlements or
a spectre behind the arras ? The most likely-
looking of the unauthorised appointments is cer-
tainly Mr. Munro-Ferguson_, Liberal M.P. for
Leith Burghs, whose portrait lately appeared in
The Bulletin as probable Governor of S. A. Mr.
Munro-Ferguson (they will knight him before
starting, of course), I remember, was something
like Gov. Hopetoun facially a young-looking
man, clean-shaven or hairless, as the case may be,
simply and primitively attired in a pair of bare
legs and a kilt.
To-night (Thursday) the Fresh Air League ball
takes place in Sydney Town Hall in the presence
of the acting-Governor and party, Major-General
and Mrs. Hutton, etc. A large number of tickets
(10s. 6d. each) have been disposed of, and the ball
promises to be a distinctly gay affair.
A recently-printed report that two young Syd-
ney ladies, sisters, well-known in musical circles,
had, in conjunction with a Melb. lady, started a
dress-designing establishment in Regent-street,
London, is, I am assured, altogether incorrect and
without foundation, the ladies in question having
gone to Europe strictly “ on pleasure bent.”
The Theatrical Carnival at the Exhibition
proved (writes my Melb. correspondent) that
Gaiety loveliness is not a mere arrangement of
grease-paint and foot-lights, but one that will
stand the garish light of day without dire results.
On the whole, the G.G.s looked as well in mufti
as in full war-paint. One statuesque beauty,
however, somewhat marred her appearance by
painting her under-lip a fa Japanese geisha, and
making herself look as if she had been eating
damson-jam. However, she made up for this
somewhat brilliant coloring by toning the shadows
round her eyes most sombrely.
The classic-looking Maude Hobson and the
petite Ethel Selwyn served at the same buffet—a
happier contrast than the pair made would be
hard to hit on. Maude was entirely clad in black
satin, except her apron, which was white nain-
sook, accord eon-pleated, with a velvet Empire hat
ornamented in front with a diamond clasp. Ethel
was all in fluffy, filtny-looking white muslin with
many frills and rosettes of white bdb6 ribbon, and
a soft-looking befeathered hat,
Decima Moore, whose gown and hat showed
several different tints of violet, very harmoniously
blended, hoisted her sleeves slightly and served
out tea most energetically. She didn’t even throw
in the smile, which one fairly expected on such
an occasion, but abruptly asked, “Will you take
tea?” then, holding up the sugar-basin, interro-
gated, “With or without?’’ gave change honestly
to the uttermost farthing, and turned to the
next comer. Grace Palotta, however, smiled and
showed her charming dimple in such a way that
one did not observe till long after that in the
matter of change she had not been quite so exact
as Miss Moore. Grace looked more Parisian than
ever in a chic dress of tahac brown (her favorite
color for street-wear, by the way) with yoke and
vast sleeves of checked glace silk. Her hat, an
airy nothing of wired lace, was an excuse for some
pink velvet orchids which nestled in the twists of
her hair and showed up its brightness.
Laura Kearney, in pink surah blouse, black skirt
and black picture-hat begarlanded with roses, was,
with Blanche Massey, running a race-game in a
picturesque little tent of their own. Laura, who
got really enthusiastic and excited, and advised
her patrons to “buck up” and to “plank their
poin,” was quite a foil to the placid and demure-
looking Blanche, who, aesthetically swathed in
black with plumed picture-hat, stood round
languidly and endeavored to charm the nimble
shilling with sweet smiles and little chirping
birdlike requests.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
Demise : Am the Year Round.
Melbourne Herald was the only news-
paper which “interviewed ” Davitt on his arrival
in that city.
Every visitor to Sydney who wants to live
among all the comforts of a palace and to associat
with the salt of the earth, should put up at the
Hotel Metrocele. The Metropole is one of the
finest hotels south of the line, commands splendid
views, and while situated in a peaceful and re-
tired locality, is within easy reach of every place
that a sane visitor would want to go to.
Whoever desires a warm swimming-bath
in cold weather is cordially recommended to patron-
ise Sydney Natatorium, where the hot sea-water
bath is always filled with the clearest and purest
of water, and all other kinds of baths are con-
stantly in good working order. The sea-water
baths in the open involve the disadvantages of
cold, sewage, cuttle-fish, sharks, mud and other
drawbacks, and the Natatorium swimming bath,
being free from all these, is a striking proof of the
ad vantages of art over nature. The Natatorium
Hotel, attached to the baths, is at present to-let.
When J. G. Pangborn, boss of the World’s
Transportation Commission, now in Australia
“seeking evidence,” was entertained by those
dull, dreadful mediocrities, the Melb. Exhibition
trustees, the other day, the usual intolerable
tommyrot was talked through the reek of drink
about the federation of the English-speaking
peoples. Then the Murkan got up and remarked
significantly, as a tip to Australians, that the
secret of thfe success of the United States had been
that Americans “ had always spelt nation with a
big N. ” £ £ Smith and his brother Imperialists
coughed hard and proceeded to discuss the whisky.
Victoria has established a Committee of
Public Accounts, to be elected by the Assembly,
irrespective of parties, and whose business it will
be to keep a perpetual oversight of the Treasury,
and to know everything that is done or doing, or
proposed to be done, with the public money. Con-
sidering how little the average Assembly knows
about the public cash—as evidenced by the case of
B. R. Wise, of N.S. W., who asked about a dis-
crepancy of ,£200,000, and merely evoked the
information that he was a snake and an eel-and
that it seldom knows even that much until it is
too late, this is a reform of great value. Moreover,
the system of non-party committees always helps
towards the extinction of Government by Contra-
diction.
Cable news that “the authorities” have
suddenly determined to suppress horrible practices
at the West End of London must not be taken too
seriously. Hitherto the police have been pulled
back by those same authorities every time they
started to prove a case. If the police were now
empowered to do their worst Mrs. Grundy would
get some awful literature on her breakfast-table
presently. Detectives are never ordered to wade
into any morass where they would be likely to
discover a few noble reprobates. Wilde, after all,
was only the brilliant son of an Irish physician—
a mere commoner, hang it! When it pleased a
shabby marquis to crush the parvenu libertine
the authorities were quite agreeable. But a Lord
Oscar would have been spared the indignity of a
dungeon-cell, anyway.
London Weekly Sun of March 17 prints
an account by “A. J. Dawson ” of a N.S.W.
country editor whose sanctum was invaded by a
maudlin poet, describing himself as “Phil. Laun-
ceston, the man who lived with Adam Lindsay
Gordon, and ought to have died with him.” This
visiter started to turn out copy, “and what he
had written was not mere versifying; it was living
music” which made the editor weep. “A. .7.
Dawson” claims to have many of “Phil. Laun-
ceston’s ” verses, and in a further article on April
17 he is so ill-advised as to print some of them
which, so far from being “living music,” are
arrant trash of the kind that floods this paper’s
waste-basket. _ “A. J. Dawson,” whoever he is,
and “Phil. Launceston, the strangely-faithful
follower of Australia’s one great poet,” seem to
have between them diddled the Weekly Sun with
a fine sample of American fake-yarn.

hats.

years old in November.

died in London, left £32,000.

paper he is represented as bald.

v. Syme trial will be heard in July.

tralia, a nation of knights—and “ladies.”

and perquisites are estimated at £230 per week.

the Melb. Privy Council Judge—should the job
be consummated.

The cathedral is the only building worth looking at—

clergyman to him, “Do you think his is a case of
blackmail?” “No, white male,” replied the
Much-Travelled.
Mr. Way, of Adelaide, has now the dis-
tinction o! being the only untitled Chief Justice
in Australasia, and probably about the only one in
British dominions. Being a bachelor, he can
afford to wait and haggle.

wheels, and walks to town. The 9.30 Ocean-street
tram almost invariably passes Justices Darley,
Innes, Wffideyer, and Simpson at different points
on the journey to the city.

H. H. Champion is starting a penny
weekly social democratic paper in Melbourne, and
will call it the Champion. He has broken with
the Age, disgusted, it is said, at Syme’s policy of
insincere flirtation with social questions.
A Sydney building-society defaulter, lately
released from gaol, by Executive minute, entered
prison a man of 16st. weight, broad-shouldered,
erect and corpulent, and emerged, after less than
three years’ confinement, pale, washy, stooped
and list.
Bishop Webber went ’* hue to fight for
the.church in Bananaland. Laden with shekels
(though not with the expected amount) he returned,
and almost the first thing the ungrateful synod
does is to propose to lower his screw one-half.
’Twas ever thus.
M‘llwraith, it is stated, has lost seven
stone in weight since his last visit to London two
years ago. Which looks ominous, for, broadly
speaking, a man should grow fat as he ages. On
tlie other hand, the woman who grows thin as she
grows old “ lives for ever.”
Dr. O’Hara deposed in Melb. the other
day that his regular charge for removing an adult
leg was 100 guineas, and the job took about five
minutes. “However,” he added, “sometimes
these fees are paid at the rate of a shilling in the
.£.” So that all men don’t cut up well.
A certain N.S. Wales M.P., who gets
■£3oo a year from his country and is one of the
noisest persons in the House of noise and self-
advt., went to a Sydney bookseller, the other day,
and asked for Varney Parkes’ book. “Here it
is,” said the bookseller; “one shilling, please.”
“ What ! ” cried the legislator. “ Charge !
Charge! ME!” “Yes, sir,” replied the book-
seller. “ Then I won’t have it.” And the .£30!)
a year went out.
George Charles Hawker, M. A., the
“father” of S.A. Assembly, who died May 21,
only narrowly escaped the degradation of knight-
hood after an unblemished local career of 55
years. Although one of the wealthiest men in
S.A, politics, Mr. Hawker always “voted liberal,”
and was a strong supporter of the Kingston Govt.
Only last year he earned the enmity of his brother
nlutocrats by helping to pass the progressive land-
tax which would not have got through without his
vote. He also assisted in legalising the progres-
sive succession duties; his son Ned, who is also
M.P., being on the other side on each occasion.
George Hawker was the sen of an English Admiral,
and a Cambridge graduate. He Was born in 1818,
and began his career in S.A. when but 22 years
old. He entered pastoral pursuits and acquired
some splendid properties. Bungaree, about 50
miles from Adelaide, one of the most fertile sheep-
stations in Australia, has been in Mr. Hawker’s
hands for more than half a century, but it is be-
lieved that, as a consequence of his death, it will be
cut up into small farms to the infinite benefit of the
community. Mr. Hawker was elected to the first
S.A. Assembly under Constitutional Govt., al-
though not one of its original members, his return
dating from’sB. His aggregate Parliamentary ser-
vice extended over 27 years. He was a fine.old man,
and never had an ill-word to say of anyone, and,
until the last few months, he looked as if he would
live out a century. He had been married for almost
50 years, and leaves a large family of sons and
daughters, so that his big estate, which is esti-
mated to be worth at least .£250,000, will be very
much divided. Few plutocrats have such a hold
on the workers as was possessed by the dead
veteran.

One Sydney boom-banker, who seems to
have appropriated at least .£lO,OOO and got safely

At the opening of Vic. Parliament, the
Countess Wachtmeister, Theoso-
phist prophetess, was a notable in the
crowd, severely clad in a grey silk waterproof,
round bonnet, and a loner grey veil, looking not
unlike a clumsy German copy of Mrs. Besant.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley is a part-
owner of the Hill of Calvary. This is merely an
item of land-syn.licate intelligence, but there are
a few Australian Judges whose names, if similarly
collocated in such an ownership, would strike
many readers as a species of blasphemous sug-
gestion.
The fulsome Melb. reception quite knocked
up Davitt. He was swamped in the volume of
blather, and in order to escape the bores secluded
himself in a private house at Hawthorn. To keep
up his journalistic work, he has to sheer off the
idiotic idolater whose time is generally “ his own”
and valueless.
Should the “ colonial - Judge-on-Privy-
Council ” job be carried through, it will be not
altogether an evil if it provides an opportunity
for exporting Australia’s most objectionable Judge
—though Heaven forbid that such a person should
ever be shoved forward as representing this coun-
try in any way.
Evangelical brewer Buxton, the new S.A,
Governor, will be between the devil and the deep
sea when he gets to Adelaide. The sinners object
to him because he is a saint. The saints look
askance at him because he made his riches out of
beer. Money, however, covers a multitude of sins
—with both sides.
The father of Havelock Ellis (editor of
“ The Contemporary Science Series,” to which he
has contributed several valuable books) is captain
of a sailing vessel which has long been trading
between England and Australia. He was surprised
at the literary success of his son, whom he in-
tended for the sea.
The N.S.W. and Victorian hangmen were
passed in Pitt-street, Sydney, within 20 yards of
one another, on the same afternoon lately. In-
stinctively the pedestrian looked around for a
Supreme Court Judge and had his picture com-
pleted, for one of their Honors drove down the
middle of the street.
Melbourne and Sydney each possess a
medical expert qualified as a barrister. In Sydney
Dr. R. H. Todd is taking charge of the toxicolo-
gical aspects of the notorious Dean case, before
the commission of enquiry; and in Melbourne Dr.
Mullen, who is both barrister and medico, prac-
tises both professions.
A tale lately printed in a Sydney news-
paper seems founded on an alleged incident in the
career of Mr. (now Mr. Justice) Lines, of N.S.W.,
who, when a goldfields official, being about to
ride a race and having to make up weight, carried
in his belt a lot of gold-dust borrowed from the
Commissioner’s office.

mainly has the Protect!ve policy of N.S.W. been

The most notorious and influential “bad-
pay ” ever known in Australia was lately gathered
to his fathers. As an official lawyer he was privi-
leged to the extent that nobody ever dared to sue
him, but of late years his name stank so badly
that he couldn’t get any tick worth mentioning.
He was followed to the grave Dy a great many
lawyers, but for every shark who paid the last
tribute of respect there was a shopkeeper who
spat upon the old man’s memory. His debts are
morally chargeable against the profession as a
body. If he had been an architect, or a cheese-
monger, or anything other than what he was, he
couldn’t have played the gam.- ter so many years.
A rather distin-
guished English
cleric, now touring
Australia, privately
describes how, as a
boy of ten, he accom-
panied his father on
a visit to Carlyle.
The old man and the
sage, of equally seri-
ous mind, left the
youth in the library
and for a whole after-
noon walked arm-in-
arm backward and
forward along a gar-
den-path. In an
hour or so things be-
came monotonous to
the junior visitor,
who, after overhaul-
ing several dozen
volumes in the vain
hope of discovering
illustrations, and find-
ing only copious mar-
ginal notes by the
great man, was fired
with competitive am-
bition, and, seizing a
virgin tome, pro-
ceeded to scrawl
“Cock-a-doodle-doo ”
and all the various
equivalents of “ Get-
cher ’air out,” “Pull
up yer socks,” cur-
rent at the date. Car-
lyle never discovered
the desecration.
“And,” finishes the
raconteur, “ that vol-
ume was sold for .£5O
at his death, the
purchaser firmly be-
lieving to this day
that the marks were
placed there by the
author of the French
Revolution him-
self.”

Justice Onslow is a grandson of Alex, Macleay,
F.R.S., Speaker of the first N.S.W. Leg. Council.
Like President Baker (S.A.), he is a Cambridge
graduate. Dead George Hawker, who was also
to have been turned into an Imperialist by a touch
of the Queen’s sword, was likewise a Cambridge
M.A.

given precedence in the procession at the State
funeral of the late George Hawker, in Ade-
laide, the jusfc-bebaubled Ghaffey Baker declined

“Tiger ” Inglis was, at latent, about again
in London after a severe illness. He had influenza
of the worst type, accompanied by alarming de-
pression. The Tiger appears to be wonderfully
popular in London both socially and in business.
Inglis, by the way ? was one _of 4 the Londonderry
underwriters, but, with “the wisdom of the Scot,”
cleared out the moment the shares went to a fair
premium.
Mayor Snowden, Melbourne’s new knight,
has the distinction of being ttie worst public
speaker in the city of smells. On important occa-
sions, if unprepared with a few written
he frequently comes up helplessly against a dead
wall and nervelessly begs the assembled company
to excuse him. The poor man, however, alwaya
had his speech carefully written out when it came
to slobbering the Governor.
Design for a window in N.S.W. Assembly,
in memory of two “ Have Beens.”
Louis Ellis, late Yic. Sheriff, retiring on a
large pension, is about as “fit” as ever. The
Civil Service Act thus rids the country of the
services of a good man in full vigor of intellect, at
a cost of a pension almost equal to his late salary.
But the ex-sheriff is one of the exceptions which
prove the rule. The average civil servant is played
out at 60, hut Ellis is a Hebrew.
Now that J. G. Barrett, sec. of Melb.
Trades Council, is in Parliament, the Vic. Parlia-
mentary Labor party boasts the three baldest-
headed men in the House. Barrett’s skull is
an almost exact replica of those of Beazley, mem-
ber for Collingvvood, and of Sangster, the Port
Melb. representative. These, in fact, are the
only three really hairless men in the Assembly.
Frank Madden and Higgins, the Geelong member,
are not rich in hair—but the three Labor men have
heads that reflect the light like the mirror of
Venus.
Basil Thomson, Premier of the ridiculous
little limited monarchy of Tonga, is known to the
natives by a title which means “Expounder of
the Dark ways of Civilised Man.” He relates in
his book, “The Diversions of a Prime Minister,”
how one of the first things he did on taking
office was to quarrel with the Minister of Police.
Thomson dismissed a case which the police
brought before him ; and “it was a new thing in
Tonga for any man whom the police accused to be
let off.” This remark applies to other place*
besides Tonga, as Sydney stipendiaries will doubt-
less recognise.
Oscar Wilde might have preserved an
appearance of Virgilic manhood by making a
cynical defence. It was open to him to strike a
classic pose and languidly remark that he had
adopted the principles of the immortals, &c., &C.
Why didn’t he crown his dying career with an un-
paralleled oration and leave the dock amidst a burst
of fireworks, instead of lying and whining him-
self to a contemptible conclusion ? Quite recently
he dared to preach his practices in a horrible
magazine, and his early audacity as an aesthete
was presumptive evidence of moral pluck. But.
seemingly, the man was sincere in nothing—not
even in vice. The cold grip of the law reduced
Oscar to pulp.
“Can anyone tell who and what ‘Mr.
Granville Lloyd ’ is ,?” enquires The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “He is an adventurous
young gentleman who says he comes from Austra-
lia and wishes to spend some of his spare millions
in forming a racing-stud in this country. He has
registered his colors at Weatherby’s, and induced
Mr. William Allison to enter into negotiations for
the purchase of several good horses, including
Ravensbury. He has taken grand rooms at Picca-
dilly, but at present the landlord has only seen his
secretary. Mr. Granville Lloyd, in fact, is, so far,
an absolute mystery, for not one of the Australian
division knows him. Who is Mr. Granville
Lloyd ?”

A departure from the usual cualoau \
was made by Sir John Madden in the n
method of summoning the Legislative \
Members of the Assembly to the Council «
chamber. The ordinary course, pursued \
by Lord Bopetoun, was for the Governor
to signify his desire to the President and '
for the President to announce that “ Hia
Excellency desires the attendance of the
members of the Legislative Assembly in
the Council Chamber.'’ Sir John, how-
ever,, did the commanding himself,instead
of by proxy. 4i I desire the attendance
of the members of the Legislative As-
sembly,’’ said he in tones duly magisterial;
and Sir William Zeal, utterly unable to
add to the impressiveness of that air of
authority, mildly repeated the words after
Sir John.

trade. What’s wrong with the “ ballot axe ? ”

Abgos wants Vie. to sdopt the Wilson and Mac-
kinnon tariff.
Forty-five Spanish monks stationed at the
New Norcia (W.A.) monastery have just been put
on the electoral roll.
The position now occupied by Premier
Turner in re the Abrahams case is a spectacle for
the gods—or the other fellows.
Australia ought to be in daily cablegraphic
touch with the States via ’Frisco, instead of having
all itsIJ.S. news filtered through royalist channels.
N.S.W. National Ass intends, with mulish
stubbornness, to take the question of the validity
of local payment of members to the Privy Council.
The Kingston Ministry, which is two years
old on 16th June, will then have lasted longer
than any other of the 42 Ministries of S.A. save
those of Hanson, Morgan, Bray, and Playford.
N.S. Wales M.’sL.A. who attempted to
borrow money pending M'Kay Smith’s anti-pay-
ment of members suit, found, in most cases, that
Aaron Melchisedeo wanted more interest and
more security.
The Age is advocating a license for private
railways in Victoria. The thin end of the wedge
to be driven into the heart of the nation. He who
controls locomotion owns the land. The thing
should be resisted to the death.
1 We are all Protectionists when it suits our
book. Sydney Evening News is a Freetrader —
tempered by the fact that some of its proprietary
appear to have a well-founded affection for the
welfare of the N.S.W. Protectionist sugar-interest.
Many Vic. politicians hold that Speight
has been very badly treated, and Murray, M.P.,
said in Parliament, the other night, that he ought
to be put back as traffic-manager with a strong
political head over him. The Age omitted this
from its Parliamentary report, though the senti-
ment was cheered in all parts of the House.

in N.S. W. on the eve
of Reid’s Budget. A
Freetrade victory !

The farce of the Q.
Kanaka-recruiting re-
gulations. If a Govt,
agent were to comply
with them his ship
would come back nearly
empty, his life aboard
would be a hell, and
when he returned poli-
tical influence would
take care he did not get
another ship in a hurry.
If he looks the other
way in recruiting and
the thing gets out, they
“ jug ” him. As of old,
it isn’t the kidnapping

being caught.
Melbourne Harbor
Trust has spent nearly
4J millions in the last
18 years, chiefly on
dredging operations and
on building an array of
wharves which would
accommodate all the
trade of Australia, and
also very largely on
salaries, office-rent and
miscellaneous gurgeous-
ness. It is practically
bankrupt now, having
been forced, for the last
two years, to meet a
good proportion of cur-
rent expenses out of
loans. There are no
more wharves to build ;
the dredging is almost
stopped ; the expensive
staff is practically idle:
and the 17 commis-
sioners meet once a
week and yawn drearily
at each other with their
gold-stopped teeth, and
then draw their fees
and go away. Judging
by present signs, the
Trust will very shortly
j be abolished.

The National Ass crowd will have a hard
fight to keep their metropolitan seats at next
year’s S.A. elections. The Tory women do not
seem to be registering in any number, whereas the
Labor-party are now putting their womenkind on

The generally-accepted statement that the
Vic. Civil Service will pay <£50,000 income-tax
out of a total of £156,000, has received a hard
knock by the discovery that, on this basis, the
civil servants must receive in salaries a good deal
more than the whole revenue of Victoria.
S.A. Govt, expects to have a strong work-
ing majority during the session. The Ministry
does not go in for new taxation, but the National
Ass intends to fight for an income-tax. The chief
Govt, measures will include the State Advances
and State Life Assurance Bills, abandoned last
session.
Barrett, the new member for Carlton
(Vic.) is 37, an unbending Protectionist, and sits
with the Labor-party. He began his political
education when seven years of age, he says, at the
feet of Graham Berry. The radicals sniff at this,
but it is all right if Barrett can live up to Berry’s
past. Berry himself cannot.
The only years since ’69, in which N.S. W,
exported sufficient goods to pay for its imports
and to meet the interest on its foreign liabilities,
were under the semi-protective system in ’93 and
’94. In other words, these were the only years
since the isolated ease of ’69 when the province
paid its way and was solvent. The beastly in-
fluences of Protection again.
Cross, Labour M.P. for Clermont, Q.,
vigorously denounces Fat Man, though himself
about sft. sin. high and 6ft. in girth. He explains
that his assaults are to be taken “figuratively.”
His fad is a State Bank patented by himself, and
he has been lecturing on its advantages lately. So
lukewarm, however, were audiences that Cross
had to interpolate step-danoing and similar variety
items in order to draw a house. Financiers
(theoretical) have a hard row to hoe.

A weird, ungodly species of justice, or
injustice, has lately transpired. A civil servant,
charged with an abominable offence, was acquitted
by the jury, whilst a Scotch verdict of “not
proven ” was found by the Public Service Board.
Practically the Board savs that that jury had no
business to acquit him. Either he is guilty or not
guilty. If guilty he should be dismissed ; if not
guilty he should not be punished. No board can
fairly go behind the law.

Says Sydney Telegraph : “ What would
be the advantage to a combined Australia of im-
port duties on wheat, butter and a host of other
food products, of which, under no conditions,
would these colonies be importers?” It might
also be asked, what would be the good of Free-
trade in these things if there cannot possibly be
any imports? If there can’t be any imports,
neither Protection noy Freetrade would make any
difference. Then why does the Telegraph want
Freetrade so badly ?' When two parties want
separate things, and.orie party declares that they
are both the same thing, then obviously that is
the party which should give way.
Premier Reid's clumsy and much-discussed
cablegram to the N.S.W. Agent-General, telling
him to inform' the Secretary of State that the
province \vak in a hurry to get a new Governor
from ’Ouve" and was far too Important a place to
be kept-long without one, was doubtless inspired
partly by G. H. Reid’s fast-growing sense of self-
importance, partly by a desire to please the S.M.
Herald, which the other day “leadered” that
grave mischief would accrue to “loyalty” through
the protracted absence of a local Imperial figure-
head, and partly with a view to currying favor
with the local Radical party, who, he no doubt
thought, would be much impressed if he cheeked
Downing-street. From a radical point of view,
or otherwise, the Premier's cabled “ sass ” is a
dismal failure. The great mass of N.S.W. voters
awwiobdn a hurry to., get an imported Governor,
and care not one solitary curse whether they ever
see another; they can do just as well without a
Governor as they could do, and have done, without
a bishop. The proper cablegram to send would
read something like this: “ Ripon, London.
Don’t hurry appointing Governor ; getting along
very-nicely on our own hook. Anyhow, don b
trouble seudinor rich holy brewer or pawned lord.
No spare cash ; country can govern itself for next
50 years with cheap rubber-stamp. Reply at
leisure by longest mail. — Reid.”
Cabled that Lord Ripon will not appoint a
N.S.W. Governor until he is assured that the
salary is not to be touched. The only thing to
prevent the province acquiescing delightedly in
this decision is the .£11,500 a year which Lieut. -
Gov. Darley draws as vicegerent and Chief Jus-
tice. If Premier Reid can make a decent bargain
with Darley, the interim may just as well bo
permanent.

Doubtless N.S. Wales will be asked to pay
the law-costs incurred by the National Ass in get-
ting the Supreme Court’s unanimous decision that
the Payment of Members Act is valid as applied
to every Legislative Assembly, and not only to
the particular Legislative Assembly which passed
it. There is even talk, in the face of this decision,
of sending a crew of lawyers to England at the
province’s expense to argue the point before the
Privy Council. Parliamentary democrats should
set summary feet on both proposals. If Fat Man
wants law, let Fat Man pay for it.
How doth the heathen rage ! Two
Kanakas were lately executed for the mur-
der of an old man near Bundaberg; an abori-
ginal has just murdered a Javanese at Mt.

Premier Reid deserves credit for re-
engaging Chief Railway Commissioner Eddy, bub
he deserves blame for not at once having obtained
Parliamentary sanction in fairness to Eddy. The
squabble about salary is paltry. Look at the
salaries enjoyed by men managing Australian pri-
vate concerns : E. M. Young, £7OOO a year ; T. A.
Dibbs, about .£4000; Francis Adams, .£3500;
Goldsbrough’s general manager, .£3OOO, &c. And
look at the difference in the responsibilities ! The
boards who pay such salaries don’t do it out of
mere benevolence but because they get quid pro
quo.
Premier Reid has made a half-promise to
protect a local iron industry by a bonus of ss. per
ton on pig-iron and 10s. per ton on manufactured
iron, and to further help it along by giving it
Govt, contracts at 10 per cent, over English rates.
The business should pull through with this rather
liberal amount of Protection, and Mr. Reid will
deny that it is Protection, and point to the new
industry as a proof of how things go ahead under
Freetrade. Every N.S.W. Freetrade Government
for many years past has practised Protection more
or less, and pointed to the results as a proof of the
advantages of Freetrade. If only N.S.W. had
three years of genuine Freetrade, there would be
no fiscal question in the province for many years
thereafter.

the monometallists on the score of cold scarcity. The
esp ecially in Australia ’’ clause is evidently the work of
the jaded sub-editor laboring under the vague idea that
Westralia is doing wonders. The Australasian increase
has been material, but not overwhelming, and things pan
out something like this :
