The miner “ working on his own ” benefits more
of late years than formerly by the establishment of Govt,
mints, and by the improvements in transit. In W es-
tralia the banks deduct only 2s. 6d. from the value of
each oz. of gold, instead of 10s., as they would do if the
miner had to send to England. On the Palmer, in the
seventies, the butchers were the capitalists, and made a
royalty something like 12s. out of every oz. they “bought.”
On a field so import mt as Bendigo, with the alleged com-
petition of buyers, the price of standard gold so late as
’7l was £3 16s. ; now that the mint is established, and
pays £3 17s. B£d. in Melb., the Bendigo miner can secure
£3 17s. 6d. locally. In the sixties one big firm made
£IO,OOO a year by “buying” gold at the competitive
prices then reigning throughout Vic. These spoils, won
by combination, are now a thing of the past—and the
miner draws full value by getting his gold turned into
sovs.
* * ♦ ♦
Here is an awkward problem. Bi-metallists
propose, not to make silver legal tender at its present
value, which would be bi metallism pure and simple, but
to declare bylaw that this legal tender shall be worth a
lot more than its present value, which is something over
and above bi metallism. Silver just now is worth, say,
2s. 6d. per oz. By making it legal tender a further
demand will be created—how much of a demand no one
knows. This will cause the opening up of many un-
worked silver deposits and, consequently, an increased
supply—how much of an increased supply no one can
tell. The increased demand would naturally tend to
force silver up, and the increased supply would tend to
force it down, and the result would be that the price
would tend to fix itself at a figure which can’t be at pre-
sent ascertaine 1. And if this figure could be ascertained
in advance, which it can’t be, and it could be guaranteed
not to alter, which is impossible, and the bi-metallists
agreed to make the white m tal legal tender at this rate,
which they don’t want to do, then things would begin to
get into shape. Meanwhile, they are about as formless as
a meat-tin which someone has endeavored to open with
a brick.
» * * *
Sydney Mint, speaking for the close of ’93,
reckoned the N S.ff. private holdings of gold and silver
coin at £4,796,954, but it may be that this is an over-
estimate. The present N.S.W. note circulation is about
£1,244,000, and the metallic circulation wnould probably
be twice this. For all Australasia the note circulation is
nearly £4,000,000 -and on the ratio of 2 to 1 the amount
of gold and silver coin outside the banks might be
reckoned at £8,000,000. The total holdings would, there-
fore, appear to be at least £28,000,000.
* * * •
The Bank of N.Z. Estates 00. proposes to raise
in London £750,000 at 4 per cent, with which to redeem
the balance of its old 5J per cent, debentures, the other
£750,000 having been paid off out of the £2,000,000 State-
guaranteed 4 per cent, stock. This is not particularly
good news to the B.N.Z. shareholders, some of whom
believed that it would be possible to pay off the entire
£1,500,000 liability out of the millions new capital
lately raised. Still the ultimate result of this rather com-
plicated settlement of affairs should be a saving of at
least £12,000 per annum to the joint concerns. The
reasons why the bank should go to London for its new
loan, at a time when any amount of money can be
obtained in Australasia at 4 per cent., are not apparent,
but the most conservative being on earth—not even
excepting the Chinaman—is the Australasian financier,
and he probably goes to London from sheer force of habit
just because his fathers went there before him. The last
time the E-dates 00. borrowed in London it was so re-
morselessly “squeezed” that the transaction nearly
ruined it and the bank together, and anybody save the
“ colonial ” financier would have kept clear of the British
money-lender thereafter.
• • » •
In view of the rumor that certain Australian
financial institutions propose following the example set
by a Tasmanian one in the way of raising money by
lottery, it may be pointed out that instead of the scheme
being a novel agency for raising the wind in Australia, it
is an old N.S.W. one. Wentworth introduced in 1844,
and carried ii 1845, a Bill which empowered a particular
bank to dispose of real and personal property by lottery,
and in pursuance of the Bill about twelve thousand allot-
m nts of land, some of them of many acres, with thou-
sands of cattle and sheep, were gambled for by ticket.
* » * ♦
The alleged “magnificent success ” of Queens-
land’s 3J per cent, loan recently floated in London is
mostly bogus, after all, like everything else that is said or
done by the Kanakaland Govt. The loan was nominally
floated at £lOl 12s. 7d., but nearly six months “ accrued
interest” was thrown in, so that the amount actually
realised was barely £IOO. When the expenses are de-
ducted the real net selling-price will probably be only
about £99. And to get this amount the loan is fixed for
half a century, so that, even if interest should go down
to 2 per cent, within the next 25 years, Queensland is
precluded from “converting” at current rates. Under
which circumstances the statement that the success of
the new “borrow” is a proof of Britain’s confid'moe in
the Nelson Ministry, and the Q.N. Bank, and the Kanaka
slave-trade and the policy of raising money in England
to make up revenue deficiencies, is pure bunkum.
» * * *
Turner and Isaacs are introducing a bill into
Vic. Assembly to abolish attachment of debls (garnishee
proceedings) in the case of clerks and “ servants” getting
I ss than £8 weekly. This has been the case in conserva-
tive England for many years, but a bill to secure the
exemption fell through in democratic S.A., and this one
will probably be thrown out by the Upper House of
democratic Victoria. Yet they have a House of Lords in
England! The principle of attachment of debts was
never intended, originally, to apply to such oases, but
was introduced by the back-door of a rule of Court. Only
one of many instances showing the grave danger of any
like judicial legislation.
♦ » * •
The Standard Bank (Melb.) has practically
fixed up its third reconstruction. Under the new re-
arrangement the 4J per cent, depositors are to receive
2J per cent, in cash, and the remaining 2 per cent, is to
be paid in deposit-receipts falling due in 1903. There-
fore, unless things improve materially, the Standard has a
rather bad prospect before it in 1903.

The Bulletin wants to hear about results from
i ime of the patrons of the “ Star Pastel Cos.; James Lor-
raine, manager; 112 King-street, Newcastle, N.S.W.”
Tins “co. advertises that it provides light and pleasant
work for ladies and gentlemen in their own homes, with
payment at the rate of 2s. per piece, and “all materials
free.” Applicants for employment receive a printed cir-
cular demanding 10s. 6d. for materials (in defiance of the
advt.), but promising that all “further materials needed”
will be given free. The “co.” also announces that it pays
2s. each for portraits of celebrities “fairly done,” the
work calling for “ no talent whatever,” and adds the in-
teresting information that “the paths of honesty alone
lead to success, either in this world or the great here-
after,” The pictures take “ two or three hours
each to copy. Tub Bulletin here rises to ask
what the “co.” means by its ruinous generosity
m offering to supply unskilled artists with free materials
for the term of their natural lives? Wouldn’t it pay the
‘co. to hand back the trifle of 10s. 6d. cash down in return
f,. ' f rom some portion of this immense contingent
liaoility ? Also, doesn’t the “co.” employ a per-
manent staff under its own supervision and get good
work for the liberal price it offers? Further, what
happens to the unskilled artist who completes 50 or 60
pictures which are not up to the co.’s standard of “ fair ”
work? Ib he to take out the value of his labor in “free
materials? And, lastly, where does a “c 0.,” pursuing
a career of suoh’disinterested beneficence, expect to go to
in “ the great hereafter?”

amounting to £SO. O i becoming due this was dis-
honored and a further advance of £250 was negotiated

* • • *
The importers’agents in soft-goods are playing
smash with the Melb. local wholesale houses. The big
British houses promptly ship out the latest samples in new
goods, and their agents invariably book orders, at London
prices, long ere the local warehouses have received their
shipments. Another thing—the agents cultivate only the
Lest “marks” in town and country, undercutting tie
local houses by reason of small expenses, and leaving their
competitors the job of nursing the baby, otherwise known
as “ supported accounts.”

into the Treasury, several assignees left to test the salu-
brity of other climes.

The voices of the Sydney tea-men are loud in
competition, and the craze is developing like a fit of
d.t’s. The tea-tvster, ordinarily only a martyr to chronic
indigestion, in boom times gets a kidney complaint which
makes him almost unable to stand. ' And at night he
lays his head beside that of his firm’s chief, and together
their yellow eyes roll ii a frenzy of kidney trouble, and
roam the infinities of the imagination for a facetious new
advt. Inonecas’ a fortnight’s midnight oil ended in a
tea-lottery which was promptly quashed by law.

what province those fire-insurance agents are who “whack
up ” when the policy has been artificially forced to

“devouring element” have been actually reduced to

leading to ttm co.’s refusing payment “on information
received.” It is becoming absolutely dangerous here to
fire up, and “ Wurry M.” has only to indicate a safe pro-
vince tor the job to ensure a big “ trek ” of Vic. store-
keepers, and the fortune of every obliging fire-insurance
agent in that Eldorado.—Ruthless, Melb.

Once again it is asked, why should Melb. gas
made from Newcastle (N.S.W.) coals be sold to consumers
at 4s. 7Jd. per 1000 while Sydney gas made from the same
coals stands consumers ss. 31.? Wages are not dearer in
one place than in the other, the plant in Sydney is sup-
posed to be the more perfect and the better equipped for
economical manufacture, and coal is landed in Sydney at
less than half the cost of taking it to Melbourne.

A Melb. sweating firm has a pious partner who
gives Scripture texts with the weekly wages. Last week
one got his wages out down—and the text
accompanying the deed was, “ The Lord will provide.”
* * * •
The Kingston Govt, is going to issue a 3J per
cant, loan of £311,200 in Adelaide. There will be no
calling for tenders, no underwriting, and none of the
usual flotation expenses, and naturally there will algo be
none of the usual half-yearly charges for remitling in-
terest to London. The bonds are simply to be sold at
par until the whole loan is subscribed, and allowing for
the saving in present and future expenses it will probably
fetch 10s. per cent, more than it would in London. Tins
is the first 3J per cent, loan offered in Australia, an I if it
succeeds, especially in the forlorn little city of Adelaide,
it should be a good oni m lor the future.

to 4s. each.

• • ♦ •
An interesting point in mining law was lately
argued at Milparinka (N.S.W.) warden’s court. A held a
prospector’s claim, but had pegged too much ground.
B wanted the excess, but A warned bim off. 0, a
friend of A’s, came and, with A's assistance, pegged out
the excess. B sought to dispossess C. Held : That, no
survey having been made at the time, O’s pegging was
illegal, A being (even in spite of himself) in possession
until the survey was made. An appeal may be made.
» ♦ * *
Banfield’s sulphide ore dressing process is
shortly to be practically tested at Broken Hill. A small
plant has been erected and will, if results are satisfactory,
operate on purchased ores. Efficient crushing is.claimed
to be one of the features of the process.

Apparently with the object of making hay while
the sun shines, the Central B.H. Cos. is getting another
smelter into running order, indefinitely extending its
contract (or the supply of ore to Block li, and negotiating
for another contract with the Dry Creek people.
The movement in Block 11 (the most southerly
block of the B.H. Proprietary) continues. Now and again
—as on one day early last week—an appreciable subsi-
dence takes place ; hut the actual movement goes on all
the time. When all else is quiet underground you can
hear the creaking of the timbers. So far, when a fall has
occurred, all the men have been able to get out of the
way ; may it always be so ! Last, week’s movement
affected a piece of ground 100 ft. x 60ft. on the surface
down to the 300 ft. level. But nearly all the ore had been
extracted from it.

The amalgamation of the Bushman’s mine at
Parkes (N.S.W.) with that of Baxter and Saddler, was a
magnificent thing—for Baxter and Saddler. After re-
peated failures and reconstructions nine tributors (who
c n uld not get a 10th man to join) went into the Bush-
man’s. They worked the first six months without royalty,
then they struck a run of golden stone, out of which they
made £2300 a man. Preparations for continuous crush-
ings with a 20-head battery are going on at the Bush-
man’s.

The old Dayspring mine, Parkes, N.S.W,, is

workings to have the mine thoroughly examined. Some
tributors working away from the water are making good
wages under big difficulties.

Mining Standard says the Golden Gully is on
the same serpentine belt as the Wentworth. There is no

authority blames the directors for not “gauging the
possibilities of the mine.” That is a little beyond the
scope of the average director. The directors were seem-
ingly guided by the principle that when a mine has spent
£40,000 in getting £25,000 worth of gold, it is time to sell
or abandon it, and the shareholders think go, too.

Col. North appears to have denied that there
was any “ underwriting” cf the Londonderry. Perhaps
not, but the business, The Bulletin-hears, was managed
in this way—that anybody undertaking to take a certain
number of shares was not called upon to stump down any
cash. Thus many speculators came in who promptly got
out when chance offered, and some, like Tiger Inglis,
made money.

Dear Bulletin, —Will a mad dog swim-a
river? What is mentioned as the first case of
hydrophobia in Victoria occurred in Gippsland
the other day. The dog, after trying to bite his
master and two other men, swam the Latrobe
River twice, and then rushed aw'ay into the bush
and has not been heard of since. If a dog with
rabies is rather partial to water than otherwise,
what sense is there in calling dog-madness hydro-
phobia? My own impression is that dogs, even as
men, are subject to different kinds of insanity,
and that hydrophobia is a symptom of only one—
though that, doubtless, the most prevalent—
amongst these. What do you think ?—C.R.
An “Australasian Federated Fruit-
Growers’ Association ” was formed at a representa-
tive fruit-growers’ congress held in Hobart last
month. All the provinces but W. Australia were re-
presented by Government fruit experts, and leaders
in the movement. Victoria sent 18 ; N.S.W., II;
Queensland, 10; South Australia, 1 ; Maoriland,
8 ; and the Tasmanian fruit-growers had all a free
invitation. Secretaries were elected : Hobart,
Mr. F. W. J. Moore; N.S. W., Mr. J. W. Jaffray,
Hay-street, Sydney ; Victoria, Mr. John West,
Mooroopna; Queensland, Mr. W. Sutter,
F.R.H.S. ; South A us., Mr. W. Clarke, Chamber
of Manufactures ; Maoriland, Mr. W. S. Hampson,
Auckland. The association’s object is to advance
fruity menV interests in all possible ways ; and, if
there are any ways advantageous but impossible,
then to leap impossibilities and advance in those
ways also. To show the need for scattering know-
ledge among degenerate post-Adamites : the Vic.
Govt, nursery sent 167 kinds of apples to the
recent Tas. Exhibition, and precisely 159 of those
kinds were unsuitable for export to the London
market.
The U.S. Supreme Court has decided that
the income-tax recently imposed by Act of Con-
gress is unconstitutional, and it is, therefore,
summarily abolished. The U.S. Congress is
notoriously about as corrupt as such bodies usually
are, and the U.S. Supreme Court has a high repu-
tation for honesty; nevertheless, the principle
which permits a just bench to sit in judgment on
the doings of an unjust legislature is bad, and the
results of the system are mostly nefarious. The
law is such a jungle of precedents and techni-
calities, that when the Fat Man hires the best
available legal talent to oppose any law which
doesn’t suit his vested interests, he can generally
find some thoroughly legal means of proving it to
be “unconstitutional.” The Lean Man could do
the same if he had the money, because almost
everything is more or less unconstitutional; but,
then, he hasn’t the money. Consequently, the
Legislature, which often means to go wrong, pro-
duces far belter results, on the whole, than the
Supreme Court, which always does its best to go
right—in a strictly legal sense.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
London Referee on Party Government
as it is now conducted : “ There are a dozen ques-
tions to be answered, but the elector has only one
vote. Nothing so insensate as asking a man to
vote ‘Yes’ or ‘No’ to a dozen wholly different ques-
tions has ever been devised by the wit of man,
only we are so much accustomed to it in politics
that it seems to have lost its meaning to our
minds. If, now, we were to adopt it in private
life, consider what would happen. I meet a friend
in the street, and I ask him in one breath (1)
whether he does not think the weather too mild
for Christmas; (2) whether Lord George Hamilton
is an ideal Chairman of the School Board ; (3)
whether the Japanese ought to accept a big
indemnity and stop the war ; (4) whether, instead
of being at the trouble of buying a friend a
Christmas present, I ought not to send him the
money; (5) whether the Times ought to come
down to a penny; (6) whether the modern
society girl is immoral; (7) whether Beer-
bohm Tree or George Alexander is the
greater actor; (8) whether it is possible to
draw the line anywhere between the Lyceum
‘Hamlet’ and the itinerant Punch and Judy
show ; (9) whether Christmas ought to be abo-
lished ; (10) whether half the people who send
good wishes at Christmas mean them ; (11)
whether we have not too many evening papers ;
and (12) whether the New Humor is really funny.
Confine the answer to a single ‘ Yes ’ or‘No ’ on the
whole batch of questions, and what would be the
value of the opinion ? Most people, I dare say,
would be frank enough to own that in the indi-
vidual case the answer meant nothing, and could
not by any amount of ingenuity be twisted into
meaning anything. Yet, supposing the
questions I have written down constituted a party
programme—and party programmes are nowadays
made up of just such diverse elements—the answer
‘ Yes ’ or ‘No’ delivered by the electors in their
hundreds of thousands would be thought to mean
something. Is it not strange that we should have
t,he patience to discuss politics at all?”
THE HANDFUL OF THINGS WE KNOW
Several years ago an American humorist and poet
published some verses called “Little Breeches.”
This was an odd name given to a very small boy
who was caught out in a tremendous snowstorm,
and finally found in some hay quite a distance from
the house. However the boy got there bothered
everybody to explain. It was certain he never
could have walked. So his father said the angels
must have done it;“ they just stooped down and
toted him to where it was safe and warm,” he said.
The poetry about it (supposed to have been written
by the youngster’s father) starts off in this way :
I don’t go much on religion,
I never ain’t had no show ;
But I’ve a middling tight grip, sir,
On the handful of things I know.
That’s it; the handful of things we know. There
aren’t many of ’em, but there are a few. And one
of them is this : That for a hundred results there
is only one cause. Nature developes and makes
differences ; never a new force.
Here, for example, is an incident which shows
our meaning. About Christmas, 1889, Mr. E. B.
Wright had an attack of influenza. Previous to
this he had always been strong and hearty. Well
he got over the influenza ; still, it had given him
(as lie says) “a shake.” After this he got along
fairly well, until February of this year (1892) when
the influenza attacked him again. This time the
malady “meant business.” Nearly every bone
and muscle in his body ached like sore teeth. His
skin was hot and dry, and to bed he was obliged
to go. For sixteen days he was under a doctor.
At the end of that time he found himself alive
and that was about all you could say for fiim.
In his letter he goes on to tell what happened
next. “I had a foul taste in the mouth,” he says,
“and my teeth and tongue were covered with a
thick slimy phlegm. My wife says my tongue
was like an oyster-shell, and I’m sure it was rough
as a nutmeg grater. Whab I ate, which wasn’t
much, gave me pain in the chest and sides. After
a mouthful or two I felt full and blown out, and
I used to swell to a great size. By-and-by a
hacking cough set in and my breathing got short
and quick. At night I lay for hours gasping for
breath, and often coughed so I was afraid I should
burst a blood vessel. I got weaker an 1 weaker and
was like a broken-wmded horse. The doctor said
it was asthma, but he wasn’t able to relieve it.
Although I live only two minutes’ walk from the
factory where I work, 1 had to stop and rest on
my way many a time.
“ Thus matters went with me until June, 1892.
Then one day I took up the Essex Newsman, and
read of a man living at Earsham, near Bungay,
having been cured by Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup. I got some of this medicine from the
International Tea Company, Braintree. After a
few doses my breathing grew easier, and by
keeping on with the Syrup my food soon digested,
the cough left me, and I gained strength. lam
now as strong as ever, can eat anything, and walk
for miles. 1 am a brush maker, and work at the
factory of Messrs. John West and Sons, High
Street, Braintree, and have lived in this town over
forty years. (Signed) E. B. Wright, Sandpit
Road, Braintree, Essex, August 23rd, 1892.”
Now let us see how this illustrates the proposi-
tion wo started out with. For almost three years
Mr. Wright was ill with what seemed like a series
of different diseases. He had the influenza twice,
the asthma once, and another disease which he
gives no name to —even if he recognised it. Look
for a moment at the variety and incongruity of the
pains and troubles he mentions, and he doesn't
describe them all, either. You would fancy he
had half a dozen ailments at least. Yet he had
but one —indigestion and dyspepsia—of which all
his bodily disturbances (influenza included—a
blood disease) were symptoms. All came out of
the stomach, and when Seigel’s Syrup set that
right the others quietly departed.
What, then, is one thing of “the handful of
things we know?” Answer: That nearly all
sorts of diseases are really symptoms of indigestion
and dyspepsia, and that Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup cures it.
Double that fact up in your fist and hold on to
it tight.
Professor Richard and
His Critics.
(Broken Hill AGE. May 21, 1895.)
In conversation yes-
terday afternoon at
his consulting rooms
with a representative
of this journal, Pro-
fessor Richard re-
ferred to comments
upon the results of
his treatment and a
challenge which had
been thrown out to
him at his last heal-
ing demonstration at
the Town Hall.
Speaking first in re-
ference to the case of
the blind patient
whose sight has been
partially restored at the first demonstration, Pro-
fessor Richard said that he did not know the
name of the woman, nor indeed of any of his
patients. He understood, however, that the
woman was well-known locally, and had been
treated by many medical men without success,
and it was quite open to anyone who doubted the
result produced by the treatment to interview the
patient. The fact that she was able to distinguish
objects after having been for years totally
blind ought, he thought, to be sufficient
answer to his critics. Professor Richard, in
reply to a question regarding the challenge
thrown out to him, said : “ Before I can
accept a challenge, I would like to know who
the individuals are who make a challenge, and
what are their credentials. I cannot accept'a
challenge throw out by persons who are notin
the medical profession. Ido not profess to be an
engineer of electric-lights. Those persons who
have thrown cold water on me and my method of
treatment for the purpose of advertising them-
selves and those connected with them I would
strongly advise to secure the Town Hall, at their
own expense, and pay for the advertisements, and
then demonstrate to the public their skill as
tradesmen and mechanics, as I have done mine as
a medical electrician. No doubt the onlookers
would give them credit for their knowledge in
their own trade or profession. But if the medical
faculty should throw out a challenge to me
as regards my medical knowledge and the
hona fides of my method of treatment,
and secure the Town Hall, I should at
once accept the challenge. I would then suggest
that cases hitherto regarded as incurable should
be the subject of a test before a committee of
magistrates. The challengers would have the
opportunity of curing or even benefiting the cases
in one treatment, or in six treatments by galvan-
ism or medicine, or any other curative agency. If
they fail, I will undertake to succeed. I wil'
undertake to put down a stake of 2 to 1 for any
thing above ,£lOO. If the challengers win they
may retain the money ; if I win, I will undertake
to hand it to the poor of Broken Hill.” Professor
Richard, in conclusion, said that he had refrained
while in Broken Hill from attempting to discredit
the medical faculty. He hoped shortly to give
another series of healing demonstrations.
MEDICAL ELECTRICITY.
To the Editor of the Barrier Miner.
Sir, —Possibly some of your readers felt no little
contempt as they perused the jealous carping of
certain correspondents. “Professor” Richard
showed modesty rather than conceit or egotism.
He did not maintain that he would be able to re-
lieve or effect a cure in every case ; nevertheless,
he did so, and as he asked to be judged by what
he did, rather than by what he said, it matters
little if he failed sufficiently to explain scientifi-
cally “appearances.” The plaudits of the three
great crowds sufficiently expressed the opinion, as
a whole, of the people of Broken Hill, that he had
proved his ability as a medical electrician ; and,
personally, I think the time must soon come
when such men should be legally registered as
“specialists,” and not termed “quacks.” We
all welcome Edison’s wonderful discoveries in
electricity, as applied to the convenience
of commercial, and the comforts of private
life. Why should we not appreciate more the
power of electricity for the relief of suffering
humanity? Great stress is laid upon the question
of the permanency of cures. Our medical im n
effect cures ; but if their patients subsequently
continue the habits that created the disease cured
or relieved, do we blame our friends the doctors?
Should Mr. Richard succeed in sending a patient
suffering from lead-poisoning to work again before
he leaves Broken Hill, who is to be blamed if that
patient becomes bad again through the poisonous
fumes from the mines? My reply is—Your cor-
respondents, if they fail to do their best to bring
about a cessation of such poisonous mineral fumes
and dust. I have heard of a pamphlet entitled
“If Christ Came to Chicago.” I can well sup-
pose that if He came to Broken Hill He would
bless the work of Richard in his attempt to relieve
suffering humanity, and condemn those of us
who, by reason of our innate selfishness, think we
are at liberty to gain our livelihood at the cost of
the loss of the health and the lives of many of our
fellow-creatures. —I am, &c.,
CHARLES COUNSELL.
Broken Hill, May 21.
To The Editor Sydney “ Bulletin.”
Sir, —
Will you please publish the above correspond-
ence for the information of the public generally.
It is my intention, after I have completed my
second tour through Australia and New Zealand,
to establish in Sydney a private Medical Electrical
Institution for the treatment of diseases of men
and women. In connection with the above I pro-
pose establishing a private college for students
with the object of training nurses, &c., using the
very latest methods and appliances.
Yours, &c.,
Professor Richard, M. E.

" That which we call a rose, by any other
name would smell as sweet,” and so it
happens that we want to explain what
is MYFA? It is only a name,
the new name for Mitchell’s
“My Favorite” Tea. Why do
we change the name? Simply to
protect you from fraud. The success of
“My Favorite ” Tea has been
gigantic; its immense popularity is so
pronounced that it has numbers of imita-
tors already, who have neither the brains
nor the inclination to get an original
name of their own
A recent decision of the Supreme Court has revealed the fact that the
words “ My Favorite” can be used by anybody, and we have therefore, in
order to prevent your being misled into buying “ Favorite ” Tea, and
thinking it the celebrated blend of Mitchell’s, decided to change the name
for one that cannot be held by anyone else. . :

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TIHIIBIBIE CASTLES ” and “
We have deilined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and ‘‘Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke,

TO AGLE STREET. BRISBANE, Q.
The Wilde case attracted enormous atten-
tion in London. All the papers were full of it
save the St. James’s Gazette, which was adver-
tised as “ the only paper in London that does
not report the Oscar Wilde case.”
English view of Hercules Robinson’s
appointment to the Cape Governorship (he was to
leave England on 11th May) ; “In any case, an
old man is not much use at the Cape. An old
gentleman in Cecil Rhodes’ pocket affords an even
less satisfactory object for contemplation.”
Dear Bulletin,— The Sydney Good Tem-
plars tilt at the Anglican Bishop of Goulburm
Dr. Chalmers, because that prelate backs “fer-
mented wine ” as being the “ real Mackay ” of the
Bible, What has particularly made the teetotal
fur fly is the statement of Sydney journals report-
ing the Right Rev. Chalmers as having said that
“any person likely to lapse into drinking habits
by sipping fermented wine at the Sacrament has
no right to partake of the Sacrament.” To which
common-sense would say, “Hear hear” were
there any such probability, or even more than
scant possibility. Dr. Chalmers “ didn’t ought ”
to scare such tender feet with a bogey. “ Wine ”
never yet wet-nursed a dipsomaniac. No positive
drunkard ever yet dreamed of opening up a
“ bender a campaign of liquoring—on “ wine.”
No negative drunkard ever yet fluked on to a
“ high old spree ” from a debauch of wine. Let
me, as a specialist in this matter, instruct G. D.
Clark, the crow-throated and pelican-headed.
There be spirit-swallowers, beer-bibbers, and wine-
drinkers, these three ; and the mildest of these are
the wine-drinkers. The spirit-swallower gets mad
drunk; the beer-bibber gets sodden drunk; the
wine-drinker gets mercurially excited. Again, the
spirit-swallower gets brain-maddened ; the beer-
bibber “ tanked ” and brain-drowsed; the wine-
warmer brain-titillated. Also, the spirit-swal-
lower vaporises his “higher levels,” and leaves
the “lower levels” dry; the beer-bibber floods
his “lower levels” and drowses his “upper
flumes the wine-warmer but tickles his “upper
levels ” and dilutes his “lower reticulations”—a
sort of half-and-half compromise between the other
tw’o. Catch a drink-lunatic and catechise him.
Does he like wine? No. Will he take wine?
Yes—if there isn’t beer, or spirits, or colza oil, or
painkiller, or .—Yours, The Dipsomaniac,
Nervous sufferers will liud it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.


A Matter of Economy.
According to custom in New South Wales,
when a prisoner accused of a capital offence has
no money with which to pay for his defence,
t^% Wn all ° WS tl,e miseral,l y inadequate sum
oi 7s. to hire a cheap solicitor on his behalf,
and £lO 10s. for a cheap barrister. On several
recent occasions, however, even this paltrv sum
has been refused, on the ground that “no"funds
are available.” The prisoners were, therefore,
defended by unpaid counsel “assigned ” by the
Judge, on the morning upon which the Court
sittings begin. The counsel “ assigned ” under
such circumstances is generally a man who has
little business, no experience worth mention-
ing, and any amount of time to spend on unre-
raunerative jobs. He has either to pay out of
his own pocket the expenses of hunting up
evidenceortodo without evidence, and lie usually
has to take the latter course. The apparent
reason why the Government can’t fulfil its
obligations in this small matter, so as to give
an impecunious prisoner at least some slight
semblance of a fair trial for his life, is that
£13,000 is being spent on the statue of Governor
Phillips, the first gaoler, and boss hangman
and flogger of Botany Bay, and Phillip’s statue
has absorbed ail the available coin. Crick,
M.L.A., drew attention to this matter recently
in the Assembly, and the reply of Minister of
Justice Gould— the godfather of the new
“painless” nickel-plated gag—was mainly
to the effect that the question had been
raised “for purposes of advertisement.”
Also, he grew eloquent on the sub-
ject that the Attorney - General had lost
no time in the matter, but had refused to
pay up the customary ,£l7 17s. after only
twelve hours’ consideration ; and that he had
three times written to Crick’s partner “asking
him to call”—in order that he might be
officially informed that he could defend the
prisoner at his own expense if he liked; and
Premier Reid declared, in a loud voice, that
theadministration of justice was being “dragged
through the mire.” He also complained that
Crick hadn’t written to him, while Minister
for Justice Gould complained that Crick had
written to him twice. But neither answer even
touched on the matter really at issue—
that when the Crown engages the police-
detectives, Crown Law Department, Attorney-
General, public prosecutor and the Treasury to
hunt up evidence against a penniless man ar-
raigned for his life, and who is in gaol and can’t
look for his own witnesses, and it refuses to
pay even the miserably small sum commonly
allowed for his defence, it is just in the same
position as Commodus when he went down into
the arena and stuck his sword through an un-
armed gladiator. The Ministry, in fact, care-
fully said nothing at all- about the question
involved, and merely “shied” its little lump of
mud with a feeble aim at the man who insisted
on digging up the unpleasant question.
Premier Rosebery has introduced in the Lords
his Bill enabling colonial Judges to sit on the
Judicial Committee of the Privy Council. The
important point of salaries is not dealt with,
for it would not have been judicious for the
Lords even to hint interference with the Com-
mons’ prerogative, lest the jealous representa-
tives should throw the Bill out altogether. But
“it is understood” that the Imperial Govt,
will find the cash—if the Commons does not
object. If the Commons does object, an effort
will doubtless be made to get “ the colonies ” to
pay up. As the chief purpose of the agitation
is to provide fat billets for Australian Judges
with Jingo tendencies, it was evident at once
that this vagueness on the vital point of screw
would never do. So the barrackers who are
pulling the leg of London Times complained
that the measure was “microscopic and tenta-
tive,” and the barrackers who are pulling the
wires of S.M. Herald echoed that it was most
unsatisfactory. The hint that “ former colonial
Judges ” will be allowed to sit also rouses the
Herald to indignant fury on behalf of the very
present “colonial Judges ” who are itching for
the billets. Altogether, Premier Rosebery’s
endeavor to add “another link to the golden
chain ” of kinship cannot be voted a success.
The Bulletin is still waiting, in order that the
Imperialistic snake may be scotched before it is
fairly alive, for a Democratic M.P. of every
Australian province to move that Rosebery’s
proposal is unnecessary and unacceptable.
N.S. W. Full Court has decided that, where a
municipality supplies gas or electric-light, or
any other service, any person making use
thereof becomes a contractor, “within the
meaning of the Act,” and can’t legally be a
mayor or alderman. In such a city as Glasgow,
therefore, under this rule, the only kind of
person eligible as a mayor or member of the
council would be one who never washed him-
self or drank water, never used gas or elec-
tricity, never rode in a tram-car, never had his
dust-bin emptied, or the street swept in front
of his house—who never did anything, in fact.
Considering that the person who uses a
municipal electric - light doesn’t “contract”
in any ordinary acceptation of the term, but
merely buys an article which is offered for
sale at a fixed price, the decision of the Full
Court looks like a piece of learned and archaic
foolishness. Unluckily, the Court doesn’t exist
so much to enforce the spirit of the law as to
dig out wonderfully profound reasons why the
law should be null and void. Acts of Parlia-
ment are often, no doubt, thrown together in a
loose and haphazard fashion, but still no half-
dozen laws could be drawn up—even if all the
learned lawyers of the province spent a year
over the work-in which a Full Court couldn’t
pick large holes. The reason is that it is
so much easier at all times to destroy
than to create. Parliament is the artist who
has to paint the picture, while all the Full
Court has to do is to throw a brick through it
when it is finished. The production of the
picture is so difficult and the brick-throwing is
so easy that a good many of the jibes levelled
against the Legislatures are wholly undeserved.
Probably if the Full Court drafted the laws,
and then the 12 stupidest men in the Assembly
sat in judgment upon them, quite as many holes
would be discovered as at present. Most laws
are drafted by lawyers, and then they are
afterwards proved, also by lawyers—sometimes
by the same lawyers— to be no earthly good.
And as lawyers live and move and have their
tees and refreshers by this sort of verbal quib-
bling, and it has been carried to such perfection
that now no one really knows what the law is,
or what or where it isn’t, a time has at last
arrived when the whole civil code dunno where
e are.
Cardinal Moran’s Book.
Card. Moran’s book is a painstaking record of facts
which have a good deal of interest for the Catholic
sectary, and a good deal less interest for anybody
else, Ihe author hardly ever takes the rdle of ex-
pounder or commentator. Occasionally he turns
aside to recount, with considerable naivete, a
dubious miracle or a trivial anecdote ; but for the
most part he is satisfied to link reference and
quotation in chronological narrative. From
ms standpoint there was probably no alternative.
Mad he deserted details and minutiae in order to
give a vivid picture of the life and struggles of his
church, of its achievements outside the field of
sacerdotalism, of its influence on contemporary
thought and conduct, lie would indeed have done
more service to literature, but he would have done
less to Ins sect. So his “ history ” remains chiefly
a laborious sectarian chronicle.
* * *
For the writing of history, in fact, Card. Moran
seems to have little equipment beyond his zeal.
His literary style is moderate: in 1000 pages
he has not written much which invites
quotation. He grants authority to news-
paper-clippings which support his own side,
and denies it to the weighty arguments which
have induced every Australasian province but
Westralia to establish a system of secular educa-
tion, and to disestablish denominational schools.
He is not too particular to score a point over
Henry Parkes (whose statements that “it is the
peculiar genius of the Roman Catholic Church to
thrive upon the enslavement of the human in-
tellect, and that the priests oppose secular educa-
tion “ because the rearing of children in the free
exercise of their faculties is death to their calling,”
are as true to-day as when made) by stating the
half-truth that for 14 years before the passage of
the N.S.W. Public Instruction Act of ’79,
appointments of teachers in the Catholic
schools were made, not by the priests or
managers, but by the Council of Education
through its inspectors. That is so ; but the
inspectors had, of course, to choose Catholics
from the candidates with which the priests and
managers supplied them, and they cannot, with a
restricted choice, be held accountable for the
deficiencies which Mr. Parkes alleges existed in
the CatholU teaching body. They had “the
pick of the basket ” —but what a basket I Nor is
Card. Moran too careful not to confuse Thos.
Mackay, editor of the various essays called “ A
Plea for Liberty,” with Charles Fairfield, who
makes in one of the essays a statement which it
suits Card. Moran to quote ; but which Mackay
no more “attests” than the man in the moon.
* * #
Card. Moran’s book is lopped off short in the
middle, as if he had suddenly found his material
too big for his space. The three heroes of the
Catholic Church in Australasia are Archbishop
Ullathorne, Archbishop Folding, and Archbishop
Vaughan. Only the two first are adequately
dealt with. The extracts from the pamphlet by
Archbishop Ullathorne on “The Catholic Mis-
sion ” (Lond., 1837) cover the pages best worth
reading in the book. Archbishop Vaughan’s 10
militant years get only a poor 30 pages (besides
some references in a chapter on “The Education
Question). If it is the printers, however, whose
imperative mood has done this wrong to “the
Catholic Church in Australasia,” they have tried
their best to atone by making Card. Moran’s book
a credit to local typography. It is profusely
illustrated, and the binding of the copy which has
reached this paper is most admirable.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Winter millinery has excelled this year in
adding artistic adornment to beauty—the current
fashions being specially adapted to set off the fair
wearer’s charms to the best advantage. How the
charm of the “Toque ” with its soft crushed folds
of velvet, and the elegance of the “Picture ” hat,
will stir up envy and covetousness in the hearts of
those who cannot possess these attractive and
fashionable adornments ! In form and combina-
tion of color very stylish evidences of the change
of season are on view at Messrs. Hicks, Atkin-
son and Sons’ establishment, where Mrs. White,
the Court Milliner of Collins-street, whose taste
is unrivalled, has the managernentof the millinery
department.
That old and high-class firm, Hardy Bros.,
jewellers, of Sydney and Brisbane, follows the
noble business principle of stamping its name
upon every article as a guarantee of excellence,
and while the interior of the Sydney shop is
remarkably handsome the outside is altogether
free from untoward show. The firm sends an
illustrated catalogue post free, so that the miner
at Ooolgardie who wants to send his sweetheart a
present can make his choice by inspecting the
catalogue after the day’s work is over. The firm
manufactures largely, has a big business in racing-
cups, trophies, shields, souvenirs, &c., &c. It
manufactured the souvenirs for the Duke of Edin-
burgh, for the English princes, for many N.S.W.
Governors. And it sends goods to country cus-
tomers on approval.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Melb, football-goera are rather disgusted with
the game as at present played. The chief complaints are
that there are too many men on the hall, and that the
umpires are too particular about pushing, &c., giving
free-kicks for the least touch. To see a match such as
that lately played between Footscray and Melbourne, one
would think the players were made of glass. A man
couldn t be touched without the free-kick penalty being
incurred. And, strangely enough, anyone reading the
list of complaints will see that none of them are to be
found in the Rugby game—to which the visit of the M.L.
team may give strong impetus.
* • » ♦
A few members of a Melb. suburban “push,”
charged with playing football in the street and damaging
a policeman in the execution of his “ jooty,” when asked
"by they had assaulted XXX, replied, through their
spokesman, “He held the ball, yer Worship.” What is
the majesty of the law to the might of the ’sociation
rules ?
• * • •
The football-match under British Association
rules, which took place on Sydney Cricket Ground the
°L her Saturday, was the most wretched spectacle ever
offered to the Sydney public.
* * * *
The Norwood team, in their Melb. match the
other day, introduced some novel tactics which surprised
both players and spectators. One (rick was so simple it
seemed strange no one had thought of it before. Stand-
ing to receive a long mark, as an Essendon man ap-
proached, the Norwood man would drop both hands to
his side, stand still, and allow the ball to pass him. The
Victorian, thinking he would mark and run, would hold
him, as is customary, and the Norwood player being held
while not in possession of the ball, would appeal for and
get a free kick.
* * • »
Barracking, ’’the lowest form of encouraging
your own side and discouraging the opponents, is becom-
ing a public disgrace in Sydney and suburbs. Last Satur-
day, in the junior match Petersham v. Leichhardt, the
local crowd continually insulted referee M'Manamey. The
rabble that follows the Sydney University team is among
the loudest and worst of the “ barrackers,” and because
the University mob “barracks,” other mobs think it
quite the thing, and, therefore, howl, groan and boo-hoo
likewise.
* * ♦ «
ihe Sydney match, Wallaroo v. University, was
won by the latter by 8 points to 6. It was anybody’s
game till the last whistle went, for the Wallaroo forwards
are far better than University’s, and kept their opponents
continually on the defensive. But any two of the Wal-
laroo backs are not equal to Abbott, and he, by grand
play, scored two tries and one goal for his side, and thus
won the match. The University team has a doctor play-
uig —a solid person with 3ft. of beam, and whenever a
player was laid out (there were quite a number of tem-
porary corpses) the doctor capered to the spot, and, in
quite a professional way, conducted an examination.
Once, when a player’s nether garments were badly torn,
the doctor recommended a sweater which fell to
the knees. The advice was adopted. The Wallaroos have
a popular amateur boxer in their ranks.
» * » *
Geelong put up the Vic. season’s record against
Port. Melb. F.C. last, Saturday, scoring 6 goals without a
behind at quarter, 10 goals to 1 at half-time, and finishing
with 15g. 13b. to 3g. 2b.
• • * *

date.

* » * »
Kerr, one of the N.S.W. crack cyclists, will
only race now in club handicaps. Up to date he has won
over 90 first prizes, and 135 prizes in all. He weighs
under 9st.
• * • ♦
With the introduction of the tandem for pacing,
cycling in N.S.W. will make records. Last year Megson’s
time for the 10-mife championship was 32min. 54 2-ssec.
O Brien’s time, the other day, was 25min. 16 2-ssec. Had
the track been dry O’Brien would have been still faster
» * * »
Powles, the N.S.W. cyclist, a few months ago
inherited over £25,000. He intends to live on his means
and make cycling his hobby.
» * ♦ *
In extenuation of cash cycling it has just been
written that “ the object in racing for cash is not to make
money out of the sport. It costs a man £6O to throw his
leg over bis machine before he can hope to race. He has
to pay £25 for a roadster, and 30 guineas for a racing-
machine. Then he has to pay a man to train him. There-
fore, to enable a man to recoup himself for his outlay,
there must be substantial mom-y-prizes. Without money-
prizes cycling races could never succeed.”
* * ♦ *
Zimmerman, the cycling wonder, who has just
got married, visits Australia in Sept. The great man won
100 firsts last season and has never been beaten in a scratch
race. A match for 25,000d015. has been proposed between
him and Houben, of Brussels. Zimmerman has been
offered £3OO for one race in Christchurch, M.L. This
champion has a rather small calf for his sft. Him, hut
his thigh (on which comes the greatest strain) is remark-
ably developed. Surgically he is of great interest, as the
doctors attribute his extraordinary performances in great
measure to the phenomenal size and power of his heart.
* * ♦ »
It is quite evident that O’Brien and Walker
(they rode in the final of the One Mile Cycling Champion-
ship of Australasia) meant to show their disapprobation
of the manner in which the committee treated Lewis.
Lewds easily won the first heat, hut Keil objected
because Lewis took the inside running. The committee
upheld Keil’s protest, but public opinion was strongly
for Lewis. So in the final O’Brien and Walker allowed
Keil to sail away with the tandem, and raced one another
home for second place—the post of honor. The public
and the other cyclists cheered. Later in the day Lewis
won Ihe half-mile open handicap, and the 25 miles’ cham-
pionship of Victoria. He won this race in fine style, and
lowered the record. The previous hour’s record' was 22
miles 769 yards ; Lewis did 23 miles 105 yards.
♦ * » *
W. G. Grace, the cricket machine, has an
average of over 100 runs per innings for 10 innings this
season, and has twice topped the 200.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
Phil. Sheridan is regarded as a certainty for the
managership of the next Australian cricket-team visiting
England. A good many prominent players are barrack'
ing strongly for Major Warded—who, however, does not
at present see his way to accept.
♦ » » *
“It is sufficiently wonderful that Australia,
considering the relative population, should run England,
in cricket, to a short neck, head, or tongue, but one can
imagine a Britisher arguing that the real cricket-playing
English population is not more than double that of Aus-
tralia, an advantage much discounted by climate.”
* * * ♦
George Giffen was refreshingly modest at the
presentation of 400 sovs. by his admirers. Tne speeches
were mainly over-flavored, and Giffen reminded the crowd
that Grace had completed his 100th century, a feat he had
yet to rival. “In fact, I’m only half-way up the ladder
yet, but I’ll get there,” said Giffen. Later, over a glass
of fizz, George admitted that he hoped to receive an
invitation to take a team to England. This will certainly
improve Walter Qiflen’s chances.

The little pacing mare Edith, by Startle, has
rather a unique career. Purchased in Kiss’s Bazaar,
Sydney, with foal at foot for £9, she has passed through
several hands at increased prices ; the highest considera-
tion being £l5O. Edith can pass the bar under 14 hands,
and in “nick ” can pace a mile in 2.40.

A curious coincidence. Last year, the Warrnam-
bool “lepper” Corangaraite won the Maiden and Handicap
Steeples at Warrnambool, the Steeple at FK-mington on
Queen’s Birthday, and started a hot favorite for the
National Steeple, won by Daimio. This year Knebsworth,
another Warrnambool jumper, has won the same three
races, and is the favorite, so far, for the National Steeple.
It is reported that Miller Bros., who have nothing very
good over big jumps, are negotiating for Knebsworth’s
purchase.

Fate pursues some stables whenever their horses
start hot favorites, and luck attends them whenever the
public is “not on.” Singularly enough, such stables
represent many conflicting interests, as a rule.

One of the finest-looking colts seen for some
time is Bandmaster, the well-named Strathmore—Melo-
dous prize in the St. Alban’s lottery, who has the blood
of such wonders ns Fisherman, Barb, and Musket under
his brown coat. Bis sire, Strathmore, was without doubt
the best horse of his year, and with a bit of luck should
have been No. lin the Cup of ’9l. His win in the Cham-
pion proved he could slay. Melodious, besides being
sister to the great Melos, gave us Meli and Wallace.

Petronel, the son of Musket, is not owned by
the Duke of Portland, but by His Grace of Beaufort.
The grand old winner of the Two Thousand of ’BO has a
full list for the next two seasons. He is beautifully-
formed and much handsomer than Carbine, whom he
heats in all measurements. His stock are very promising.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent hears that some
people in England are nibbling at Nordenfeldt. There is
in fact, a regular craze for the Musket blood just now’
and with good reason, as stayers become scarcer year by
year. J
* ♦ ♦ •
Croker and Dwyer, the Yankee sports, scored a
win the first time of asking, at Newmarket, with Eau
Gallic, a son of Iroquois. Sims (colored) rode the colt
and much amused the cognoscenti by his style of horse-
manship, as by comparison with English riders he seemed
to sit with his knees in his mouth. However, “ hand-
some is as handsome does,” and getting oft like a flash
from a gun he made the running from end to end and
scored. The success of the American horse is a triumph
for “the watch,” as the race was run to a tick as shown
by a time trial.
* ♦ * *
The late Duke of Hamilton, w’ho died the other
day, was the greatest plunger ever known on the English
turf. Unlike Hastings, Benson, and other j.’s, he took
his gruel without wincing.

THE SOLE REASON
WHY YOU SHOULD INSIST ON
CLEMENTS TONIC
Is because of its merits. Remember “ Bilson’s
Tonic ” will not do you any good “ because ” Syd.
Gregory made 200 runs, nor “ because ” Megson
won the championship, there being no connection
between them. You might just as well expect
Bolus’ pills to cure you because they are the same
shape as the earth. Remember ! When you are
sick you want a remedy of proved therapeutic
value, and not an imitation or substitute for one.
THE ONLY REASON
We have ever put forward in favour of Clements
Tonic is its efficacy, and this has been proved
thousands of times, and this is just the reason of
the crowd of servile imitators who trade on our
reputation and the renown of Clements Tonic.
DON’T BE DIDDLED !
Insist on having the proved and genuine article,
that you have proof and evidence of its power to
cure disease. None of the vile imitations will do
any good, and you only waste your money and
perhaps risk your life by being bamboozled into
purchasing imitations. Listen to no specious
blarney or plausible humbug about “tonics.”
Remember, Clements Tonic has many virtues
besides its restorative properties, therefore it is
best. You know you can depend on its curative
properties, and neither you nor anyone else knows
anything of the imitations, because they are only
made to sell.
NOTE!
There is only one reason why you should insist
on Clements Tonic, that is, its proved efficacy,
therapeutic value and reliability.
There are scores of reasons against the imita-
tions, some of which are :
1. Their only raison d'etre is to line the pockets
of the vendors.
2. They never were heard of till Clements Tonic
was discovered and made its reputation.
3. They have no merits and no proved value.
4. They must be inferior in quality or they
would not cost less money and allow the
sellers a bigger profit than Clements Tonic
does.
5. You only waste your money.
6. Your case may progress beyond cure whilst you
are trusting in a false security.
7. That the mere fact of their being called
“Brown’s,” “Jones’,” or “Robinson’s”
Tonic is proof of the attempt to trade on
our reputation, because if their mixtures
had any real value they would adopt a new
name and thus get the full benefit due to the
virtues of their own article.

to bold race-meetings. He raced as a hona-fide amateur
for prizes ; and pictures of him are to be seen, with the
rider standing by his “ bike,” and cups and clocks and
spoons and forks, and other glory. Then ’cycle-makers
saw a chance to push biz, and when a rider made a record
they presented him with their best bicycle, or sold it to
him at half price, and when said rider made more records
they advertised the fact and made much of him to sell
their brand of ’cycle. Then the rider began to ask
whether he couldn’t make money at the game, so he
either bought a share in a biz. or started one “on his
own." And then he took sub-champions into his employ
and corrupted them with the notion of cash, and the sub-
ohampions corrupted the whole race of amateur cyclists,
and the result is that the cyclists have all become cash or
bogus amateurs.
» * • *
Kadavu Levu, the second highest chief in Fiji
(he will be the highest when his father dies) is a remark-
ably powerful athlete. At cricket he is a tremendous
slogger ; he often hits the ball from the middle of the
Suva ground into the sea. At Auckland, M.L., he made
the biggest stroke ever seen there.
* * » *
A year or two ago someone taught the Fijian
natives football, and when H.B.M.S. Orlando visited Suva
a game of football was arranged between the lithe,
brawny Fijians and a team of bluejackets. Never before
had Jack-tars played Fijians football; never again will
they. For it was not football, it was bull-fight; it was
Jack-tar in the air, Jack-tar gruelled and twisted and
carried on stretchers aboard H.M. Orlando, to the sound
of muffled drum. And in the wake of the flying squad
oame the Fijian braves chanting a death-song, and loudly
cursing the fate that cheated them of a cannibal feast.
* * * »
The dispute about the rowing amateur defini-
tion recalls the old English athletic wrangle, when men
who sold cloth by the piece would not compete against
those who sold by the yard. But snobbery of that kind
collapsed when the University men—prominent among
them B. R. Wise—put amateurism on the broad basis of
“ fun, not lucre,” and gave it practical effect by them-
selves competing agaii st farm-labnrors and mechanics.

What with everlasting lickings and continual
bickerings over the “amateur definition,” N.S.W. would
be s udying the interests of peace and Federation if she
quitted eight-oar rowing, and stood around crowing and
whooping at her neighbors to bring a man to beat her
professional oarsmen.

Any adventurous spirit who wants big
sport should go up to Melville Island, where the
buffalo-hunters are now making another start and
forming a permanent camp, writes a Bulletin
correspondont.
The story goes that attorney Crick’s seat
in N.S.W. Assembly will be challenged, on the
alleged ground that he has, contrary to the pro-
visions of the Constitution Act, entered into con-
tracts of profit with the Crown —by accepting
Treasury money for the defence of capitally-
arraigned prisoners. Even so, there is nobody in
Australia who could beat Crick in West Mac-
quarie—especially at the present juncture.
Yic. postal authorities recently handed
over to the press a dead letter showing the spirit
in which one soulless male has received the
advances of the New Woman. The letter is
an answer to a proposal of marriage from some en-
terprising spinster, and the writer, after expres-
sing his gratification, &0., states that he is for-
warding to his admirer by accompanying mail an
old damaged shirt. The nature of his final answer
to her proposal, he says, will depend upon the ex-
pertness she displays in restoring that shirt to a
habitable condition. The experiment went wrong
in the post, but the man who would marry a
woman on the strength of an old shirt deserves all
he is likely to get.
The leading Germans of Adelaide have
appealed to the German women of S.A. to register
as voters without fail. The five local German
pastors back up this appeal by a letter in which
they refer to the woman-suffrage law as one
“hated by Germans,” but of which, as it is
the law, the Germans must avail themselves to
preserve their civil and religious freedom. In
Ohio, America, they point out, the Legislature,
elected with the help of the women, of whom
a majority consisted of members of English sects,
nearly passed a bill according to which every
Lutheran pastor who used wine in the celebration
of the Lord’s Supper would be thrown into gaol.
“ Did not the sects in America try to close by
force the church schools of our Lutheran co-reli-
gionists in America, and to force them to send
their children into the irreligious State schools ?
Let us, therefore, not lay our hands in our laps,
but let us fulfil all our civil duties.”
“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “ a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and F. It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “ Killaloe ” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing ior an aspir-
ing oomique, pro. or amateur.*
At latest dates the Victorian Crown Law
authorities were trying to settle the painfully
funny question as to whether the action of an ex-
M.P. in travelling on an expired railway pass,
which he had been requested to give up, revealed
any “felonious intent.” They decided long ago
that a prosecution of the ex-member would lie on
the ground of “felonious intent,” but seeing that
the aforesaid ground is an unapproachable allot-
ment in the gentleman’s heart, the Crown Law
authorities can’t swear that it is there to lie upon.
Meanwhile, the prosecution of hundreds of ordi-
nary people for travelling without a ticket, or
riding in first-class carriages with second-class
tickets, has assumed a new complexion. They
have been summoned and fined without proof of
felonious intent, and, consequently, should be in
a position to sue the Railway Department for
damages on the evidence of the Crown Law oracles,
who state that a prosecution in such oases cannot
lie.

In 1890 the gold coin in circulation throughout
the world was 5i limes as much as in 1848 ; the silver coin
more than twice as much ; the paper money 3J times as
much. Altogether there was in 1890 three times as much
money as 42 years previously. This seems to utterly
knock the bottom out of the silver cranks’ theory that
prices have fallen owing to the scarcity of the “circulat-
ing meijum.”
* * * *
Apart from all currency bigotries, the fall in
silver is bringing quite anew danger into speaking dis-
tance. The counterfeiter can now produce at a large
profit unexceptionable imitations of the British, German,
French, and U.S.A, silver coins—the counterfeits being
perfect in assay, design, weight, and workmanship, and
undist inguishahle by any process whatever from genuine
goods. British silver coin is issued at a valuation of ss. 6d.
p w oz. standard— i.e., two half-crowns and a sixpence
weigh loz. (troy). The market value of the metal in a
shilling, when silver is at 33i1. per oz, is 6d. —when silver
is 30 1., about s.Jd.—when silver is 27d., about sd. Taking
silv r at 30ch, the value of the metal in £IOO British silver
coin is £45 9s. Id., the expenses of coining to a (strictly)
private syndicate working on a commensurate scale soine-
w ere in the South Seas might bring the cost up to £6O.
As ther • is practically no limit to the amount that could
be put judiciously into circulation, the margin of profit
has big attractions.
Value for value, British coins contain 2J per
cent, more silver than the silver tender of France and
U.S.A., so that the advantage would be a trifle larger for
these countries. Spurious shillings have already reached
Australia from China, but they are, as a rule, stiuck from
badly-cut dies. Tue matter at the present, juncture does
not concern Australia much. The profit on the local
silver is all taken by England, and there does nob seem
to be any earnestness on the part of Parliament to secure
amendment of the rule. So, whether Australia pays
seigniorage to London, or to a South Seas counterfeiting
syndicate, is not a thing of much, concern. So long
as the seigniorage must be paid away it does not matter
what foreigner gets it.
» * * *
A warning to busbmen—storekeepers, dealers,
drovers, and pub-keepers. Station cheques, which are
often on city banks, pass current for many months—first
because the station is thought “ as safe as the bank,”
and, second, because nobody' wants to pay exchange. But
these cheques are signed by the manager ; and suppose
the manager has a row with the station and is “sacked,”
or is retrenched ! The station may honor the cheque, of
course ; but in some recent Western cases it hasn’t, on
the ground that the manager has failed to “settle his
accounts.”
♦ « • *
Beet can be grown in cold regions where the cane
and the imported nigger don’t flourish, and its advan-
tages can be gauged by the fact that nearly two-thirds
of the world's sugar-supply now comes from this
roat. When Federation arrives, and there is Free-
trade throughout Australia, it is just possible
that the N.S.W. cane industry will be unable to hold
its own against that of tropical Queensland, while
Victoria is altogether out of the sugar-cane industry.
Therefore, the two southern provinces have every reason
for pushing on their new experiment.

Someone named Pearce said in London recently :

A Johannesburg correspondent to The Bulle-
tin (March 4): “Our market continues to boom, and
flotations and reconstructions are the order of the day.
The Feb. output of 169,2950 z., although less than Janu-
ary’s, is 17,4250 z. better than for Feb. last year. The
great feature of the Rand is now the development of
deep level mining; and the colossal undertakings of
the Rand Mines G.M. Cos. will certainly be a striking
feature of the gold-mining history of the century. A
second, or lower, series of deep levels has been com-
menced at the Simmer and Jack, which expects to get
the reef at about 3000 ft. How much deeper they will
find it practicable to work remains to be seen. We get
some very sensational reports from W.A. They tell us
that 30oz. to the ton is quite common there ; but an
average of lOdwts. suits here very well.
“During 1894 thirty corapanieson theßand mam
reef, working 1650 stamps, crushed 1,987,000 tons ore for
1,346,1770 z.; value, £4,635,600; or 13|dwts. (47/6) per
ton. Seventeen companies paid divs. aggregating
£1,152,103. Most of the remaining 13 main reef co.’s are ex-
pected to pay divs. this year, when development is further
advanced. These oo.’s by no means represent the whole
of the working properties of the Rand, as 47 contributed
to the Feb. output of 169,2950 z. The main reef is fully
proved as regards outcrop co.’s, and there seems little
reason to doubt that the adjoining deep levels will also
pay divs. Other reefs, notably' the Black, have been
proved in several places, and promise well.”
« » ♦ *
“ The Scott-Knox patent mineral concentrator
and separator, the latest and best machine for treating
battery' crushings and tailings,” gained first prize at the
recent Agricultural Society’s Show in Sydney, and proved
the most attractive exhibit upon the ground. The
machine worked all day every day during show-time, and,
under the guiding hand of its inventor, there is no deny-
ing that it gave excellent results. Ils mechanism was
investigated by Mr. James Taylor, the N.S.W. Govern-
ment Metallurgist; he subjected it to a severe test, and
expressed his official opinion upon it in the following
terms to the Minister for Mines: —“The machine as
exhibited is said to require less than one-half h.p. to
drive, and to be capable of taking the tailings from ten
stamps. It is claimed that all quicksilver floured or other-
wise carried off by the pulp is caught in the upper dish,
whence it is removed from time to time as may be
necessary. The concentrates are collected in a box
beneath the lower dish. The whole is built in a substan-
tial and workmanlike manner, and, judging from the
demonstrations given, the machine will do good work.
Whether it can do better work than is being done by
machines of established repute remains to be proved. It
is intended to have one working shortly in a battery
running other concentrators, and working the two under
equal conditions as far as possible ; when, with equal re-
sults, the much lower cost of the Soott-Knox machine
will tell in its favor.” The machine is the property of a
company whose present office is at 53 and 55 Hunter-street,
Newcastle.

A Melb. remittance-man has just gob ahead of.
Uncle Mo’. Generally, about a month before his quarterly
payment came to hand, he was compelled to part (tem-
porarily) with an expensive diamond-pin, on which
“Minefrendt” willingly advanced £3O. So often had
this little transaction been repeated that whenever he
entered the money-lender’s office, the latter would, with-
out being asked, count out the usual £3O. However, the
prodigal lately had a tempting offer for the diamond,
which he accepted, and had a paste one put in its place.
Being short, lie once more repaired to Uncle’s. The
£3O was, of course, forthcoming, and the pin safely stowed
away. When it was not redeemed, two months later,
Mo’, got suspicious, examined the pledge, and—cursed
his eyesight.
S. R. Wilson, at one time a very prominent figure
at Broken Hill, and brother of the famous W.E., is manage
ing the leases held at Hermes by his brother’s syndicate.
The syndicate employs many hands, amongst them being a
son of Richard Speight, ex-chairman of the Vic. Railway
Commissioners, who is engaged on picK-and-shovel work ;
a son of “Boh” Walsh, formerly one of the Vic. Crown
prosecutors, and now acting County Court Judge ; and a
nephew of a Melb. newspaper-proprietor, once possessor
of a six-figured fortune.

THE MOST IMPORTANT Wo..r*
EVER WRITTEN
BY A PRINCE OF THE CHURCH.
HISTORY
OF THE
CATHOLIC CHURCH
IN
AUSTRALASIA.
By PATRICK FRANCIS CARDINAL MORAN.
The History op the Catholic Church in Austral-
asia is a Crown Quarto edition and contains
nearly 1100 pp., which are embellished and beautified
with illustrations of different individuals and buildings.
The engravings consist of portraits of all the notable
Chur on dignitaries— past ana present—a.....
assisted in the founding and building up of the
Church under the Southern Cross. A number of
prominent laymen closely connected with the
Church also find a place within its pages, together
with illustrations of the Convents, Colleges, and
other institutions, which have done so much to
bring the Church in Australasia to its present
proud position. The illustrations alone are worth
more than double the price of the book, awakening
as they do pleasant associations past and present.
No reasonable expense has been spared in its
publication, aud we are satisfied that in its general
get-up it will compare favourably with works
issued from the most prominent English and
American publishing houses.
Ist. The world-wide reputation held by His
Eminence the Cardinal as an Historian and
Author, which, coupled with the fact that
neither expense nor time has been spared in
order that the History may be complete in
every respect, guarantees for it a reception
of a national character amongst the entire
English-speaking race.
2nd. That the vast amount of detail at command,
comprising as it does the first century’s his-
tory of Australasian life and grand develop,
ment of the noble work accomplished by our
self-sacrificing pioneers, provides an almost
inexhaustible fund of thrilling episodes of
personal and public events, which cannot
fail to charm aud enlighten the reader-
irrespective of creed—combining as it does
a great literary triumph together with a
treasure store of untold wealth of important
historic information, covering the whole
range of affairs, both clerical and lay,
affecting the Church in Australia and New
Zealand.

SOME ST. ALBAN'S CHRONICLES.
ZAtmsKl, brother to Havoc, was one of the best
PhaTnS 1 T? WaS 6Ver , at o fc - Alban ’ s * and, in the
Ohampion Race won by Strathmore, Mr. W. R.
Wilson had to back them both. Had the race been
th ®. manner most suitable for Zalinski,

amongst the winners of the greatest classic race in

• # •
Zalmski was heavily backed during the pre-
ceding winter for the Melbourne Cup of 1893, but
unfortunately developed a “leg.” Mr. W, R
Wdson unlike some owners, immediately
scratched him, and never tried to get out of his
money.
* * *
Some years ago an astute old trainer and horse-
owner who lived afc Flemington arranged with a
former proprietor of St. Alban’s to have a “trial
on the quiet. The Flemington man arrived at
b-eelong by the afternoon train, and when going
through the town became aware that he was fol-
lowed, evidently by someone who wished to know
where he was going. Next morning early the
paint-brush was used to change a chestnut with
four white legs into a bay, and a bay into a chest-
nu.t ™ th L v y h,to , ]e S s - Th e trial took place, and
actually before the owners got to town the horse
badly beaten was a hot “first favorite ” for the
Australian Cup, and the winner of the trial was
knocked out. Fact.
* * *
When a celebrated Scotch divine, who is married
to the daughter of a leading Geelongite, was inter-
viewing Trenton at St. Alban’s, he expressed the
opinion that he would mate such a sire with a pure
Arab mare, and proceeded to give his reasons.
Archie x mile, who was standing by, quietly
asked him if he thought a daughter of Cain would
nick well with an Englishman, for that was a
similar case ? His reverence took it kindly, and,
strange to say, saw the simile.
Some talented theatrical artistes have paid visits
to bt. Alban’s at different times, amongst them
being poor Fred Leslie, of the Gaiety co., whose
story of the tune he visited the Duke of West-
minster s stud at Eaton Park brought down the
house.
• * *
. Man ,y visitors to St. Alban’s have displayed an
inquiring turn of mind as regards the racehorses,
hoping, no doubt, to pick up some knowledge
whereby they might line their pockets, but the
training-ground is so well marked off that unless
the onlooker is up-to-date as regards the posts, he
might as well stay in bed as try to secure a surrep-
titious tip. 1

Mr. Nathan Herman, of the Misfit Par-
lors, No. 9 Wynyard-street, Sydney—the place
tf l e banner is, and where the immense
J looks like a frozen boa-constrictor over the
door, and the huge lamp serves as a beacon to
guide the wandering footsteps of the pilgrim from
the busu—-has vastly increased his business within
the last few weeks. A mammoth new depart-
ment has been added for the sale of furniture
crockery, pianos, bedding, ironmongery, &c., and
[h ■ young man who is going to get married, and
be afterwards run-in on his mother-in-law’s evi-
dence, can here find absolutely everything that
he wants. A new feature of the jewellery depart-
ment is the reliable watch, with gilt chain which
looks like gold-all for 6s. 6d. The misfit tobacco
and cigar department has been greatly enlarged,
and all kinds of plugs are now supplied. Herman’s
tea is a wonderful speciality. The dress and
mantle department has also greatly expanded, and
a large staff of ladies—most of them young and
beautiful are kept on hand to make all necessary
alterations while you wait. Sundry large ship-
ments of Brussels carpets have arrived, and are
being sold at 2s. a yard, with no charge whatever
for making and laying; and various consignments
of rugs, blankets, boots, shoes, overcoats, water-
proofs, &c., <fec., are being sold at the same ruinous
rates. Also, the four winds which blow in the
halls of heaven continue to bring in orders from
everywhere for the famous £2 parcel, which in-
cludes a suit of clothes, two boiled shirts, two
under-shirts, six pairs of socks, two ties, two
collars, six handkerchiefs—no person with the
kind of cold which prevails just now can get
through with less than six—set of studs, hat, pair
braces and a pair of boots. The said orders, each
with remittance attached, now average 100 daily,
and many letters expressing the satisfaction of
the purchasers can be seen at the office. A large
consignment of misfits in every branch of clothing
and bought at merely nominal prices, has just
been received per Orotava, and Mr. Herman is
shortly starting for Europe to make contracts for
the purchase of all misfits from the leading tailors
of London, Glasgow, Liverpool, Paris, Vienna,
and other cities. When this arrangement is com-
pleted, he calculates that the business will be the
most extensive of its kind south of the line.
Meanwhile, an additional elevator is being put in
and the trade is already slopping over far beyond
the limits of the original premises, and Mr. Her-
man is concocting schemes for absorbing the whole
block from George-st. right down to York-st |
