“Religions die when they are
proved to be true.”—Oscar Wilde.
In a proclamation the Emperor of
China states that his policy henceforth
will be to support truth, to eradicate
abuses, and to stop bribery and corruption
in the public service. It sounds like an
Australian election-address.
(>ne day lately a group of people at
Gastlemaine (Vic.) were talking about
death. “I’d like to die suddenly when
my time comes,” one of them remarked,
and with that he fell forward and expired.
This is the sort of incident that the
average parson likes to descant upon just
before taking up the collection. If elo-
quently handled it helps the collection
materially. Most people have a vague,
unacknowledged idea that death can some-
how be guarded against by subscribing
liberally to the collection.
Premier Reid (N.S.W.) states that
he and Attorney - General Want are
stiongly opposed to local option without
compensation, while the rest of the Cabinet
supports it. Local option and compensa-
tion are just as urgent questions as Free-
trade. and Protect on, and if these two
supp r'ers of drink are prepaied to coale ce
with their opponents, what right have they
to throw bricks at the Dibbs-Parkes coali-
tion, which is just as immoral? What
is there to choose betwc en, the man who
sinks his 1< cal opti >n principles pro-
fessedly for the sake of Foreign-trade, and
the 01 e who sinks his fiscal principles pro-
fessedly for the sake of Federation?
Gladstone’s refusal to participate in a
public howl against Turkish atrocities sets one
thinking of the old time, when he published a
pamphlet on the same gruesome subject. Disraeli
was then the Turk’s champion and Gladstone his
bitterest foe. The aforesaid pamphlet, which
dealt with Bashi-Bazouk doings ip Bulgaria, had
an enormous sale, and Judy, the Tory “comic”
paper, cartooned the G.O.M. in the act of pump-
ing sovereigns from a “Bulgarian atrocities”
machine into a sack. Underneath the picture-—
“There is no truth in the rumor that Mr. Glad-
stone intends to donate the profits of his pamphlet
to the Bulgarian Relief Fund.’’ Another Tory
gibe at humanitarianism was expressed ina sketch
of a murderous-looking collier saying to his feeble
wife—“Get me pound o’steak and onions’gin I
come back from ’trocity meetin’, or I’ll knock thy
dom’d head off.”

Up East, after a recent murder, a lawyer’s
clerk was heard to say, “Thank God ! now I can
garnishee the hangman for a claim we have
against him.” Dog does not eat dog ? Sometime^
Editor Bulletin, —About popular Aus-
tralian superstitions-—“ Cattle on a camp see
ghosts.” All animals have fancies in the dark.
When a boy, I used to ride a pony which would
never, at night, go down a certain lonely lane
where a man had been murdered ; and once, when
I forced him into it, he got into such a pitiable
state of terror that I let him turn back. I have
known courageous dogs, at night, run howling
with fear from purely fanciful dangers. Mania
not the only animal with an imagination.—R.
The constables connected with the Yic.
quarantine-station have a grievance that should
appeal to the most callous heart. New regula-
tions have recently been passed calling upon them
to report themselves for vaccination oftener than
hitherto, and they venture to object, remarking
that they have already been vaccinated in a
most intemperate and unreasonable manner—one
veteran, for instance, having the marks and
brands of no less than 40 “takes.” It is under-
stood that the officers do not object to being
vaccinated once all over, but when there is no
room left on their bodies for further scarifying,
and the doctors start to double-bank the brands,
there is going to be trouble. Thb Bulletin
sympathises with the constables : this excessive
vaccination is unnecessary, for, as Noah said,
Melbourne policemen rarely catch anything.

HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
The Old Convict .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A “HARD CASE,” his back seamed with inglorious
soars, painted with a free hand, the work of the
“old masters”; thick grey hair like the fur on
the north-end of an old native bear; hard, cold,
steel-grey eyes looking furtively to right and left;
large ears that, in their intensity of listening,
moved forward and backward in obedience
to the muscles that have become useless in
the ordinary human being, like the third eyelid,
memento of our bird ancestors. His wrists,
wrinkled and hairy, bore the marks of the ancient
“bracelets,” and his ankles were furrowed by the
141 b. irons of the days when he would cheerfully
have cursed God and died. He walked as he bad
walked 60 years ago, leg-ironed on the shores of
Norfolk Island. By my subtlest diplomacy did I
obtain the confidence of that old man, made sp
sensitive and suspicious in the bitter experience of
the long-dead but unforgotten past. Now and
then, as the red-coated soldier, or the overseer, or
the flogger, drifted across the field of memory, the
hard, bony hands would clench, the grey eyes
flash an infernal fire, and the deep voice utter a
fearfully bitter —“ Curse them ! Curse them 1”
And yet in the inner nature of that man there
were noble qualities, green spots in the awful
desert, springs of living water in the thunder-
scarred granite of that human rock. His code of
honor was narrow, but inflexible. Friendship-
true, unselfish, willing to risk death if called upon;
and, on the other side, a hate pitiless, remorseless,
unscrupulous, compared to which the fury of a
tigress robbed of her young might be regarded as
compassionate.
He was in Port Arthur when the Lady
Franklin called in ’63 to take a band of “lifers ”
to Norfolk Island. He had been to the Island
before; landed there from the China with 300
others in ’45, from London. John Price was com-
mandant, and the red-coats were a part of the 11th
Regiment. The 300 from the China made up 2200
Prisoners. Capt. Willett stood on the deck of the
iady Franklin as the boats came off with the 25
lifers (first three years in irons) and swore that no
man should come on board unless naked as the day
he was born. So the 25 stripped and climbed over
the side. This was the usual closing of the door
after the steed had gone. Boat crews had been
off to the vessel with wood and water, and friendly
bands had taken concealed implements and
secreted them on board. The clothes were
handed up and the lifers were dressed on deck.
“ Eleven of them,” said the old man, “ were
awful scoundrels.” The sort of men deserving
that title from him would baffle the human
imagination to describe. Capt. Willett had a pre-
sentiment that this would be his last voyage,
hence his unusual precautions. Nine of the 25
joined the band of sworn conspirators, and of these
there were five who “jibbed ” at the last moment.
But the remaining four were men prepared to
do or die. During a squall, when the sailors were
aloft, and the captain in the cabin, they out a
hole ih the deck and the four men crawled along
through the lazarette into the cabin, and looked
out to see a soldier on the poop. They crept
softly up, caught him by surprise, and threw him
overboard Bill Davis, the ringleader, gave each
of the three a musket and bayonet, and told him
to use it if necessary. Then he “stalked" the
man at the helm and laid him out with a belaying-
pin. The four then covered the sentry below with
their muskets, and told him to hand up his
weapon “ butt upwards.” They forced the sentry
to tell the sergeant that “the ship was taken and
they were all prisoners, and to hand up the fire-
arms I” The sentry warned the sergeant not to
shoot. The sergeant did shoot, and shot nobody.
All the muskets and bayonets were handed up and
flung overboard. '■
Bill Davis ordered the sergeant up on deck and
told him to stand there, while he told a seaman,
just down from aloft, to bring the carpenter.
Davis then went and called through the bars :
“ Any of you Norfolk men who want to be in this
better come up 1” And they all came with a rush.
Then the carpenter had to strike the irons off all
the prisoners and put them on the captain, the
sergeant, and the 15 soldiers ! The captain fought
like a demon when being brought up from the
cabin. Fifteen soldiers’ wives were on board.
They were asked if they would stay on deck or go
below with their husbands. They preferred to
stay on deck. Not one of these women received
the slightest insult; on the contrary, they were
treated with the greatest politeness. All the
spirits were thrown overboard. The convicts
disabled the ship, and took to the boats, being
then about 104 miles from Levuka, where Bill
Davis, Joe Merry, and an Irish Cockney convict
were finally captured and taken to Sydney,
brought up at the police-court, and remanded to
Hobart Town. Oapt. Willett and the soldiers’
wives spoke highly of the consideration shown to
them when the ship was taken, and they got off
with six months in irons. “ Only six months for
mutiny and piracy, ” said the old man sadly,- “ and
I got 18 months in irons for absconding as assigned
servant from an old pensioner’s widow, Mrs.
Oleary, who kept a pub. called the ‘Connaught
Rangers! ’ ” And the old fellow nearly shed
tears at the recollection of so glaring an anomaly
in the awards of justice.
And here is how he escaped, and his subsequent
proceedings. He had a brother, a free man,
possessed of much coin, the magic “open sesame”
of so many locks and bars. This brother suc-
ceeded in getting him assigned to Mrs. Cleary,
who was one of the conspirators. “ Let him stop
here,” said the old lady, “ two captains are court-
ing my two girls, and I’ll get him away all right!”
But the brother had him removed to a pub. kept
by an “old hand,” and concealed there for six
weeks. To deceive the servants, he was repre-
sented as a martyr to acute rheumatism ; in fact,
one leg was completely paralysed. There was a
grim humor in the old fellow’s description of the
awful agonies he suffered from that imaginary
rheumatism, and the groans he uttered when one
of the servants even touched the unfortunate
limb. One day a visitor, dressed in great style,
drove up to the pub. in a cab. He was sent by
the brother. “Has Mr. Wright recovered from
his severe indisposition ?” The reply was that he
was “ too ill to move!” The visitor said he would
drive him down to the ship, where a cabin was
taken, with the intention of trying the effect of a
sea voyage ! “ Oh, Lord,” said the old chap, “to
think o’ me being carried into that cab, groanin’
like a dyin’ ’orse, when I could ha’ jumped over a
five-railed fence !” So he was driven down to the
shore, put into a boat and rowed off to a schooner
called the Freetrader, bound to Port Albert for a
cargo of bullocks.
He was followed by the gentleman who came in
tne cab. This imposing personage leaped on
board the Freetrader, and, walking up to “ Mr.
Wright,’’ shook hands with him effusively and
wished him a pleasant voyage.
It seems necessary here to remark that “Mr.
Wright,” the hero of this episode, was in great
demand by the authorities, and there were special
precautions to guard against him escaping by any
of the sea-going vessels.
“In them times I was a bit of a devil 1” said
the old man, modestly. This statement passed
entry in my credulity without any taxation what-
ever.
The swell, who drove to the hotel in a cab, saw
“Mr. Wright” off in the boat and then shook
hands with him on board the Freetrader, was the
official “searcher,” who boarded all vessels and
searched them for runaway convicts. He had
been judiciously interviewed by the “brother,”
the man with much coin ! Oh ! that we all had
similar brothers!
After one of the boatmen saw the searcher
shake hands with “Mr. Wright,” he touched his
hat and remarked, privately, “that’s the man
we’ve been looking for, sir !”
“The man be damned,” said the searcher,
“that’s an old friend of many years 1” (Dear
“ brother,” with thy puissant coin !)
Of course, the searcher acquitted the Freetrader
without a stain on her character. This searcher
was named John Smith. He was an ex-Manches-
ter pick pocket, who kept a grog-shop at Hobart.
So, “Mr. Wright” left by the Freetrader for
Port Albert. There was a fine humor in the old
fellow’s observation, “She was an old tub goin’
for a cargo o’ bullocks for the butchers. No
wonder I was the only first-class passenger I”
On arrival at Port Augusta the first man he met
had been a fellow-prisoner in the “Old Bailey.”
“Mr, Wright” landed in gorgeous raiment, swell
walking-suit, bell-topper, and Wellington boots
that cost ,£3 10s. The Old Bailey friend gave one
glance and observed “For God’s sake, Johnson,
don’t wear them togs here! Go and wring your-
self !” The latter remark was an Old Baileyism
for “ change your clothes.”
Johnson walked up to the hotel and ordered a
room. The landlord and the guests took him for
a disguised duke, or some capitalist come to buy
up all the neighboring stations.
Johnson sat down in a private parlor and rang
the bell. It was answered by the waiter, in whom
he at once recognised a man who had been hang-
man and flogger on Norfolk Island, and flogged
Johnson himself on one occasion. .
The waiter knew the visitor at a glance.
“ Hillo!” he said, “ain’t you out a little afore
your time ? ”
“ Bring me some brandy,” said Johnson, sternly
and contemptuously, for no flogger or hangman
was a special favorite in the penal settlements.
Johnson drank the brandy, walked into another
room, full of cattle-men, and shouted for all hands.
“Won’t you include the waiter?” “That ain’t
no waiter,” said Johnson, in the man’s presence,
“that’s White, the hangman from Norfolk Island,
the fellow who hanged ‘ Jackey Jackey ’ and 11
other men in one mornin’ !”
This damaging testimonial was regarded by the
waiter as good enough to travel on, and he left
that night without even saying good-bye.
Johnson also decided that there might be more
congenial localities even for himself, and started
overland to Port Phillip on foot, wearing the
Wellington boots, which were not removed until
cut off his swollen feet, with a razor, in the
Clarence hotel, in Melbourne.
The old fellow still retains some of his primeval
robustness, and his taste for beer and rum is in no
way affected by time and trouble.
Brisbane.
A. MESTON.
A Dream of God .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw the awful oountenanoe of God
Distorted with a nameless agony
Keener than Christ’s, when, ’neath the olive tree,
His bloody sweat bedewed the sacred sod.
Silent and helpless as a carven clod —
Racked with a fierce remorse He seemed to be,
The countless wrongs of an Eternity
Scourging Him with His own avenging rod.
All vestige of His Deity was flown
Save immortality, which, hour by hour,
Shot sharper pangs through every quickened sense
As, dreaming of His former pride and power,
He sat upon the ruins of a throne,
Abject, despoiled by His Omnipotence.
N.S.W.

According to private letters, the most
flourishing industry at Southern Cross (\SjjA.) is
coffin-making. The Govt, hospital and the hos-
pital founded by the Wesleyan Church (move-
ment initiated in Melbourne) are crowded with
typhoid patients.
Dear Bulletin, —A N.S.W. Attorney-
General has appeared to prosecute in person at
Sydney Criminal Court since Wisdom prosecuted
Dunn 15 years ago. W. B. Dailey, when A.G.,
appeared in person about ’B7 to prosecute in a case
of highway robbery committed on the Liverpool
Road. The case was one of modified bushrang-
ing, and, if I remember rightly, the person robbed
was the pay-clerk of Monie and Co., the contrac-
tors for one of the Sydney Water Supply con-
tracts. —Paolo Maoaui.
The Adelaide Hospital squabble, after pro-
viding the “ holy ” oity with diluted scandal for
weeks, has ended in the nurses who raised the
storm backing down, apologising for the naUghty
things they said about the management, and ask-
ing to be reinstated. But the fun isn’t all over.
The members of the board are wroth because the
Hospital Commission recommended the retirement
of two of their number in order to make room for
a couple of lady “ boarders.”
A man named Smith, while walking along
a country road near Oooma(N.S.W.), abstractedly
put his lighted pipe in the same pocket with 24
boxes of matches. Presently the matches flared
up, and burned everything off him save his boots
and his collar. A few hours later a charred object,
which was almost too indefinite to be called Smith
or anything else, expired in the local hospital.

Dear Bulletin,—-Although, as you say,
there should be no regiments but “ Australian,”
there are excuses for the “ Sydney Scottish ”
which wouldn’t apply to the Cardinal's “ Faugh-
a-ballaghs.” Irish national sentiment is hyper-
sensitive, irascible, aggressive; Scottish ditto is
placid, good-humored, defensive. The Caledonian,
like the Hibernian, thinks himself the apex of
humanity, but he isn’t so ferociously resentful of
scepticism on the point. The Scot, in fact, is
much the less “scotty.” And, in any case, the
raison d’Stre of the Scottish Rifles is not so much
the nationality as the rig-out. Take away the
kilt, and you take away the label I—Bbebks.

JIM .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was an. illogical, irrelevant sort of a man—
inconsistent, of course, but not merely inconsis-
tent. That would be ordinary. Jim never failed
to carry his inconsistency to the pitch of unneces-
sary unreason.
We were mates in ’B4, and had bottomed five
holes without luck. We were at work on the
sixth ; and funds were nearly exhausted.
Another day’s work would decide our fortune,
and we sat outside the tent in the dusk and specu-
lated on probabilities. Jim seemed more than
ordinarily* sanguine ; while I felt anxious—for if
no luck this time, what next?
I lowered him down the shaft next morning,
and he worked for two whole hours, and then
came up for a few minutes’ blow. He said he
thought we were close on it—talked of the trips
we would have together to Melbourne, Sydney,
Tasmania —anywhere and everywhere. Then he
went below again and worked for another hour —I
meanwhile grafting steadily at the windlass.
, Bresently came a short spell on his part, and a
consequent cessation of windlass work. I sat on
the handle in the sun and built imaginative palaces
of delight until called back to stern reality by his
call—“ Haul up !”
I wound away at the windlass with blissful
energy—for surely his voice had a ring of gladness
in it—and when the coils of rope on the barrel
told me he was near the top, I called out “At
last, Jim 1”
He did not answer, so I peered down the shaft
as I wound him up. . . . My God ! what was
this ! He was on the rope, it was true, but from
his upturned, quivering face, the starting, glaring
eyeballs seemed to shoot through me.
The windlass refused to turn : I strained and
strained until he came clear of the shaft, and then,
horror-stricken and panting with fear, looked, as
I held the handle against my chest.
Jim had fastened the rope round his neck : I
had hauled up a strangled man.
We had bottomed on another duffer.
D.M.
Madame Blavatsky’s friends, in contradict'
ing the charges of immorality reiterated against
her, point out that “indisputable evidence ” fore d
the N.Y. Sun to withdraw similar charges.
“ That evidence was a medical certificate that
Madame Blavatsky was a physical ascetic.” Neat.
In Melb. Criminal Court, the other day,
a woman appealed for mercy on the ground that
she was soon to be a mother. The sympathetic
jury found her not guilty, and the woman laughed
openly. Judge Holroyd said he understood quite
well that she laughed because she thought she had
duped the jury. And hadn’t she?
The policy, economical and otherwise, of
introducing third-class carriages on N.S.W. rail-
ways gives the board of two a topic for animated
discussion. The Bulletin wonders who is at
the bottom of the third-class idea, which has
nothing whatever to recommend it. To create
a third-class would involve a very expensive
alteration of rolling-stock, and of all the depart-
mental machinery; and where is the profit to
come from? Three classes have had a long trial
in England, and only one or two companies now
maintain them, the others running first and third
only, or first and second only.

But before the noon to-morrow they’ll be singing

For the Western creeks are calling,
And the idle days are done,

And the Queensland sheds begun.

Mudgee Western Post of 10th May pub-
lishes, under the heading of “Deceived,” a poem
beginning : “ When we two parted in silence and
tears.” This effusion is signed “James Evans,
Oapertee Valley, April, 1895.” Shade of Byron !
They’ll soon be printing the Lord’s Prayer as an
original contribution.
The S.M. Herald complains, and with
justice, that the absence of a tea-duty has led to
the N.S. W. market being flooded with a cheap
and utterly villainous description of Chinese tea.
The same paper also complains that the presence
of a heavy duty on spirits leads to bad liquor
being put on the market. Also, though the want
of a duty leads to shoddy tea, the Hekald flatly
• Inclines to see that the want of other duties will
lead to other kinds of imported shoddy. Some-
thing has gone askew with the old paper’s logic.
The present predicament of Oscar Wilde
is not the first difficulty in which the family
has been concerned. In ’62, the late Sir William
Wilde, “ surgeon oculist to the Queen in Ireland,”
was defendant in a sensational action for “as-
sault,” brought by a Miss Travis, who gained
some literary repute under the pen-name of
“Francis Boyle Price.” This lady was “like
one of the family ” at the Wilde mansion in
Merrion Square, the most fashionable part of
Dublin in those days, and now. But at length
“Speranza” began to regard her with distrust,
and a social earthquake took place when Miss
Travis openly charged Sir William with a heinous
crime. She did not take vulgar criminal proceed-
ings, but brought a civil action for damages, and
employed, as leading counsel, the most brilliant
man then at the Irish bar —Isaac Butt, Q.O. Her
case was, that one day she was in Sir William
Wilde’s surgery, that he placed her under chloro-
form But the ooulist emerged safely
from the scrape.
